Question asked on: 2007-05-29 00:00:00 by Lizbeth

As part of the makeover for the Australian Broadcasting Corporation's online news site the project team have developed a profile of the Dublin Core standard (with some additional elements, and customised value lists) for describing its news objects (stories, images etc.).

We have used Dublin Core encoding standard for mixing Dublin Core and non Dublin core metadata as a guide for embedding this metadata record in an HTML/XHTML web page. While this all seemed pretty straightforward, we are not sure what is involved in declaring a namespace for the ABC's custom elements and value lists. Currently, we have no expression of these in RDF, or a metadata register other than a spreadsheet. Is it acceptable for the ABC to use a URI which resolves back to a page on our website which includes a PDF (that functions as a reference guide) with the ABC's custom elements and value lists?

I can send through a copy of our intended reference guide if this is useful.

Many thanks,
Lizbeth Moore
ABC Metadata Registry Group | AUSTRALIAN BROADCASTING CORPORATION | address GPO Box 9994 SYDNEY 2001 | email moore.lizbeth@abc.net.au | phone +612 8333 2524

Response given on: 2007-06-04 10:30:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Lizbeth:

There is a draft DCMI recommendation that might be helpful to you: "Guidelines for assigning identifiers to metadata terms" available at
http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/dcmi/term-identifier-guidelines/ You will note that it is dated a few years ago and has not progressed to a recommendation but there are reasons for this that are not related to the validity of the advice, so I think you can use it with confidence.

I think it's great that you are going about your project with attention to standards and interoperability, but as you've no doubt recognized, there are no well-lighted paths yet to achieve all of your goals in that regard. I think the plan you've articulated in your question, using a URI that resolves to a webpage, is a perfectly adequate initial step along the way. I get the feeling that you recognize that there are probably better alternative, given that you're likely to modify and change your vocabularies over time, and I hope you'll follow through on those intentions and move to a fuller registry for your terms as those become available.

On additional point: DCMI usually doesn't recommend embedding metadata in webpages, given that most search engines don't use them (thanks to the spammers) and they're very difficult to scale and manage over time using embedded HTML or XHTML.

Good luck with your project!

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2007-03-17 00:00:00 by Mike

How do I put the place of publication of a book into the Dublin Core record? Do I use the coverage element?

Response given on: 2007-04-01 17:12:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Mike,

The answer to this depends on whether the place that the resource was published is relevant to the resource itself, or just the publishing entity. In other words, the place of publication automatically noted in a MARC record does not automatically map to DC:Coverage.

If you look at the definition for Coverage, you'll see that this is so: "The spatial or temporal topic of the resource, the spatial applicability of the resource, or the jurisdiction under which the resource is relevant." The question you need to ask, therefore, is whether this place of publication tells you something about the topic, the jurisdiction of application, or the spatial applicability. Otherwise, the place is an attribute of the publishing company, not the resource you're describing.

Regards,
Diane

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2007-03-14 00:00:00 by Pallas

When filling in values (not encoded) for the relation element, best practices recommend using a string or number from a formal identification system to identify the referenced resource. How should these numbers be indicated? For example, if I choose to use an ISBN, what is the standard format for showing that it is an ISBN rather than another number combination?

Response given on: 2007-03-30 09:00:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Pallas:

We generally recommend the approach suggested in RFC3187: http://www.ietf.org/rfc/rfc3187.txt

An example of how that might be encoded in HTML is included in: "Guidelines for encoding identifiers in Dublin Core metadata" (see: http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/dcmi/dc-identifiers/2002-01-07/) and noted below.

Regards,
Diane

XML Code


&lt;meta name=&quot;DC.Source&quot; scheme=&quot;URI&quot; content=&quot;urn:isbn:1-56592-149-6&quot;&gt;

However, it would help me greatly if someone could show what a typical reocrd looks like, whether it be simple or qualified. Which is used most frequently and why would one be chosen over another?

I assume, for my purposes that XHTML is what I want but am not entirely sure of the differences. What would be best for use when learning how to use DC?

Response given on: 2007-03-12 16:17:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Pallas:

I've included some examples below: note that they are not the book you asked for, and they are not fully encoded XML (they lack namespace declarations and schema locations), but include I think what you're looking for.

I suspect that the Simple DC is more prevalent, particularly since OAI-PMH (the Open Archives Initiate Protocol for Metadata Harvesting), requires it as a minimum standard. For most purposes where designated vocabularies are needed, it's not sufficient.

For more learning materials, check out the DCMI website (http://dublincore.org) and look for the usage guide and the tutorials (under training materials).

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane

Question asked on: 2007-03-10 00:00:00 by Pallas

What would a Dublin Core Record look like for the book "Blink", written by Malcolm Gladwell?

Response given on: 2007-03-10 17:43:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Pallas:

That would depend on a number of variables. First would be whether you're interested in Simple Dublin Core (the original 15 elements) or Qualified Dublin Core, which includes a number of refinements and allows more specific use of controlled vocabularies.

Then, of course, you'd have choices about encoding, whether XHTML, XML or RDF. So it's not so easy to answer your question!

One way to try and explore what the options are, and incidentally get some simple examples, is to try out the DC-Dot metadata generator (see: http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/dcdot/) and see what it comes up with. Unfortunately it requires a URL to begin, so you'll need to select an online resource, but you can select any encoding, and also edit the output.

I hope this helps.


Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Implementation; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2007-02-23 00:00:00 by Oliver

Dear DC community,

I'm working for an ejournal publishing scholarly book reviews. We would like to encode metadata information of each review in an RSS feed. Specifically, Recommendation 5 on ISBNs says:

"Encode ISBNs as URNs in DC metadata, using the 'isbn' URN namespace identifier and indicating a 'URI' encoding scheme in qualified DC applications. For example:



I hope this is useful to you.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2007-02-22 00:00:00 by Jennifer

Hiya,

Can I just check the correct implementation for the 'dc.rights' element. Can we list the organsations name and link to their copy statement in the one tag e.g. <meta name="DC.rights" content=Murdoch University, http://www.murdoch.edu.au/copy/copy.html> or should the tag be split into two: 'dc.rights.owner' & 'dc.rights.URI'

Kind regards,

Jenni

Response given on: 2007-02-23 16:03:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Jennifer

It would be fine to use a statement such as the one you propose, but it might be good to include a term designating the rights you're claiming rather than leave that all to your linked information. If it's copyright, just include that at the head of your statement.

If you have some need to separate the parts of the statement, you should do that, but do note that you've then gone into the realm of Qualified DC, and you then need to pay attention to the fact that this puts you into a separate namespace, and "dc.rights.owner" and "dc.rights.URI" are both incorrect expressions. Ergo, it's best to stick to the simple alternative!

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2007-01-25 00:00:00 by suzanne

Hello DCMI gurus,

I'm looking for descriptions, in layman's terms, of some of the Dublin Core Elements so I can equate them to an image database I've built. I've looked at the DC reference descriptions, but I was unable to parse them for the following terms.

Is "Format" comparable to what an art historian might call "medium", eg, painting, drawing, sculpture?

Is "Identifier" comparable to what a curator might call an accession #?

I'm guessing "Source" could be identified as the book from which a VR curator might have scanned an image? Yes, no?

Could I equate "Publisher" with a museum that holds the original artwork from which my digital image is derived?

I don't understand the definition of "Type" in the DC reference description. Can you give an example? Ditto for "Relation".


Thanks for your help.


Response given on: 2007-01-30 16:07:00 by Holly Mercer

Hello Suzanne,
Format is comparable to Medium; in fact, Medium is an element refinement (or qualifier) of Format. Accession Number would be a locally-assigned Identifier. Source could be identified as the book from which an image is obtained; include the citation and page number.

It is important to keep the 1-1 principle in mind when applying Dublin Core metadata: The principle whereby related but conceptually different entities, for example a painting and a digital image of the painting, are described by separate metadata records. In your example, Publisher would refer to the entity responsible for making the digital image available. You could then use the Relation element to show the relationship of the digital image to the original artwork.

I highly recommend consulting Diane Hillman's "Using Dublin Core" http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/. It is a usage guide that includes definitions, descriptions, and usage examples. Sections 4 & 5 may be particularly helpful, and may further clarify how to use the Dublin Core elements. Best of luck on your project.

Regards,
Holly

Question asked on: 2007-01-20 00:00:00 by patricia

Hi, I am doing a research about LO metadata and I found some papers that consider 15 elements for the dublin core specification (http://dublincore.org/documents/dces/), and others with 22 elements (http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/elements.shtml)
how many elements should I consider?
Thanks
Patricia

Response given on: 2007-02-04 16:31:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Patricia:

Dublin Core comes in two "flavors." Simple Dublin Core consists of 15 elements only, but Qualified Dublin Core is richer, and includes element refinements and methods to specify controlled vocabularies. Unfortunately, there's still quite a bit of confusion about this but DCMI is attempting to clarify the situation.

In any case, you can find all terms recommended by DCMI at: http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2007-01-19 00:00:00 by Kevin

Hello DCMI,

I'm confused about dc:author - I can see many uses of this on the web (http://www.google.co.uk/search?q=dc:author) but no mention of it in http://www.dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/. Is it a valid element? Was it once valid but dropped in favour of dc:creator?

Kevin.

Response given on: 2007-01-20 10:06:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Hello Kevin!

Your suspicion is correct, dc:author is not valid Dublin Core, and never was. I'm afraid that since the beginning, some implementors have completely misunderstood DC and believed that calling some element they wanted to use a "dc" element could make it so. That's certainly not the case!

However, because "creator" is only the name of the element, and not necessarily the label that must be used when displaying the information to users, there's no real need to argue. But it's extremely important to interoperability to be firm about using the same element names, not matter your decisions about display labels.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2006-12-11 00:00:00 by Shan Shan

What are the biggest advantages for using DC over MARC for cataloguing digital objects?

Response given on: 2007-01-05 15:15:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Shan,

I don't think it's really possible to come up with generalized arguments about the advantages or disadvantages outside the context of a particular implementation. For some projects, MARC makes more sense, and for others, DC is more useful.

One consideration that may be relevant is whether the project is implemented within a library where MARC data is already available for material that is being digitized. If the library can more easily manage MARC data and can find an efficient way to re-use the MARC data for the digitized material, that might be better. If, on the other hand, there are no precursor resources with reusable data, DC may be easier to implement, given its overall simplicity and shorter learning curve.

In short: one size does not fit all, and we always suggest that decisions on metadata format be based on thorough analysis of project needs.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Implementation; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2006-12-04 00:00:00 by Michael

Dear all,

does the "location of a recording" fit to DC-element ref. "Coverage spatial", since it can be separated from the recording content on spatial concerns? Example: Nigerian music recorded in London.
Thanks in advance.

Best regards,
Michael Fuhr

Response given on: 2007-01-05 14:54:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Michael:

The definition and comment for Coverage provides some of the answer to your question:

Definition: The spatial or temporal topic of the resource, the spatial applicability of the resource, or the jurisdiction under which the resource is relevant.

Comment: Spatial topic may be a named place or a location specified by its geographic coordinates. Temporal period may be a named period, date, or date range. A jurisdiction may be a named administrative entity or a geographic place to which the resource applies. Recommended best practice is to use a controlled vocabulary such as the Thesaurus of Geographic Names [TGN]. Where appropriate, named places or time periods can be used in preference to numeric identifiers such as sets of coordinates or date ranges.

It seems to me that, if you use Coverage for both the "topic"--which in your example would be Nigeria--and the place where the recording was made, you risk filling your search results with information that most of your users would not find useful. Now, recognizing that your users are better known to you than to me, this might be an incorrect reading, but certainly if you plan to distribute this information outside a particular community of those more interested in the music than the recording of music, the same concern applies.

One better way of dealing with the location of recording is to set up a controlled list of recording entities, with associated locations, and use the entity names in "Publisher" or "Contributor." This would allow you to enable more specific access to the details of recording without muddling up the topicality of the resources with technical information.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Even though this project is for personal use at this stage, it's ment as kind of a study for a larger project. For this reason you could say I'm looking at it from a film library perspective.

Age:

It might be more useful for you to expose some of your thinking in some accessible documentation, then ask on the DC-General list for a bit of conversation with other implementers.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2006-09-20 00:00:00 by Age Bosma

Hi,

The Encoding Schemes currently available in the DCMI specs are very limited. The value of the 'identifier' element, or 'format' and 'medium' for that manner, doesn't mean much if you don't specify what kind of identifier we are talking about. A scheme I would like to see for one would be e.g. 'EAN-13'. This one can also be used as a replacement for 'ISBN' codes.
Are there any plans to extend the amount of Encoding Schemes in the future? If yes, how, when and to what extent?

Until that time, what is the recommended practise to specify non-DCMI Encoding Schemes?
E.g. currently I'm specifying dates with DC in XML the following way (please correct me if I should do it differently):

<dc:issued xsi:type="dcterms:W3CDTF">1999</dc:issued>

In the 'identifier' element I'm storing 'EAN-13' codes the following way:

<dc:identifier>8713045200305</dc:identifier>

In what way can I specify that we are dealing with a 'EAN-13' code? I could create my own namespace again:

<dc:identifier xsi:type="myterms:EAN-13">8713045200305</dc:identifier>

But this seems to me like missing the whole point of a unified metadata spec if everyone starts creating their own bits when dealing with global standards like 'EAN-13' barcodes.

Are there already namespaces available for standards like this and others which can be used in combination with DC?

Yours,

Age Bosma

P.s. I think some additional info is required for people to be able to select a proper 'What is this question for' category. I e.g. don't know what 'Crosswalk' is. Maybe a link next to the dropdown, presenting a pop-up with a description of each group will do the trick. Did I submit these questions to the right group now?

Response given on: 2006-09-22 09:57:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Age:

Yes, agreed--they are very limited. Part of this limitation has to do with recognition by the DCMI that they cannot cover the varied needs of a broad range of communities via a manageable list of encoding schemes. In other words, the list is unlikely to grow, and DCMI is looking at more generalized strategies to support the use of encoding schemes in metadata.

The real challenge to supporting use of encoding schemes, regardless of syntax used, lies in persistent namespaces to a great extent. Given that you wish to use 'EAN-13,' which is a recognized standard, you will need to ascertain the appropriate namespace (you'll need to figure out who the maintenance agency is for EAN-13, and may need to inquire specifically) , declare that in your document, and supply as well a token (such as has been done in your example with 'W3CDTF'). The technique is the same, but you'll have to do a bit more legwork to set it up properly.

Right now, the infrastructure to make these tasks simple is not yet in place, but there is some activity going on that should move us all forward in supporting this work. It's going a bit slower than we'd like, though.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Encoding Schemes; Purpose:DCMI Participant; Audience:Programmer


Question asked on: 2006-08-26 00:00:00 by Gillian

When creating metadata for a text book where the chapters are written by different individuals and the book has a number of editors, who should I classify as creators and who as contributors?

Response given on: 2006-09-05 16:26:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Gillian:

There isn't really a one-size-fits-all answer to this question, since it depends entirely on how you want your metadata to function. Some implementors use the distinction between Creator and Contributor to express something about the importance of the role played by different entities, but this distinction is difficult to express and nearly impossible to enforce outside a particular implementation. Since most applications search both together (since even if a metadata creator enforces a distinction, a user is unlikely to understand it, or agree), you might want to clarify for yourself why you need to distinguish, and where.

Some implementors have chosen one or the other element and determined for simplicity's sake, others have chosen more specific roles (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/appendix_roles.shtml) when they feel their users will benefit from that specificity.

The key is that you make the decision based on the needs of your implementation, and document them so that users of your metadata will understand your decision-making.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:DCMI Participant; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2006-08-26 00:00:00 by Gillian

Response given on: 2006-09-05 16:26:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Gillian:

Question asked on: 2006-08-13 00:00:00 by zhuizhui

I want to develop a metadata set based on DC, I wonder if I can build refinements under element refinements.
Thank you very much

Response given on: 2006-08-14 02:57:00 by Pete Johnston

Yes. You can define new properties and declare that those properties are sub-properties/refinements of any of the properties defined by DCMI, including the "element refinements".It is probably best to think of the concept of "element refinement" in terms of a type of relationship that may exist between two properties, rather than in terms of a category of property.RegardsPete Johnston

Question asked on: 2006-08-02 00:00:00 by Jarl

Hi AskDCMI.

I'm working in a project in Norway which is implementing DC in a new document system. During this process we have registrated some difficulties during implementing Security Classification (ex. "Top Secret", "Secret", "Confidential", and "Restricted"). What element in DC-standard can or should be used for implementing this kind of data?
Best Regards
Jarl Kvanli

Response given on: 2006-08-04 15:06:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Jarl:

I think the answer to your question can be approached in a number of ways. One simple method would be to characterize the document itself as to its classification within your security system. For this you might use a controlled vocabulary within the "Access Rights" refinement to the Rights element.

The definition of Access Rights is as follows:

"Information about who can access the resource or an indication of its security status."

There is also a Comment: "Access Rights may include information regarding access or restrictions based on privacy, security or other regulations."

Obviously, though, you might have a requirement that the system be able to negotiate between a document and a user, where the user has a particular set of security levels and rights to view a certain kind of secure document. It's likely then that the use of "Access Rights" might well solve only half your problem.


Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2006-06-26 00:00:00 by ESTER

We are describing an object composed of several archives (a multipart media type).
How can we reflect the qualified metadada "Format.Extent"? Must we repeat the element to collect automatically the size in bytes of each archive of this object?

thank you very much

Response given on: 2006-07-14 19:57:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Ester:

There are no hard and fast rules about how to describe the kind of objects you're dealing with. It all depends on what your needs are, and what you intend to do with these objects that should indicate what your strategy should be. Keep in mind that, for the most part, DC data is designed to be easily interpreted by humans, so it might be best, if you're describing the object as a unit, that you be consistent about presenting information about its extent.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2006-05-09 00:00:00 by Andy

We are basing an electronic document management metadata standard on Dublin Core. We are having an issue with what element/refinement to use for tracking the version of the document.
We have looked at using identifier, hasVersion, modified. Current business practices use date for the chapter of the document being updated but no change in identifier or date of the document as a whole. We are suggesting the modification of business practices to include a form of versioning within the title section.

Any suggestions

Thanks
Andy Everett
Washington State Department of Transportation

Response given on: 2006-05-17 17:36:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Andy:

The expression of version or edition of documents has long been an area where there is considerable difference of opinion. Some projects are quite comfortable with expression within the Title element, others prefer a separate Description statement.

Of the ones you mention, Modified, of course, should contain a date (which is fine if your documents are versioned by date, but doesn't help with other kinds of versioning). Identifier isn't incorrect, but might tend to create problems when there's a need to distinguish different kinds of identifiers for action or display. HasVersion refers to another document, not the one being identified.

So, I think you've come up with a viable solution, so go for it!

Regards, and good luck with your project,
Diane I. Hillmann

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2006-05-08 00:00:00 by Adam

My query is what development has been done or is anticipated in providing a uniform strategy for the use of metadata in the Legal arena. Metadata has the capacity to revolutionise evidentiary content in the courts and hence have a massive impact on one of the fundamental ways in which we arrange our society. Who should I be contacting about engaging this discussion? I work in a top 15 UK London law firm and this question requires urgent attention. The lawyers do not know what to do. Many thanks. Adam Stead

Response given on: 2006-05-09 19:14:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Adam:

The short answer is that in fact numerous efforts have been made, but none are comprehensive. In the US, much of the work has been directed at interaction between lawyers and the courts, in the form of e-filing systems. See, for example, http://www.legalxml.org/ . In Europe, a good deal of effort has gone into XML efforts surrounding EU harmonization, particularly in regulatory areas. Finally, numerous individual providers of judicial opinions and legislation -- including the governmental bodies that create the information in the first place -- have done work in this area. Probably the best has been done in the area of point-in-time legislative systems, of which the Tasmanian EnAct system was the first. Another area relatively rich in activity is transcription of court proceedings.

As to why these efforts haven't been comprehensive, I think there are a number of reasons. One is that the best starting point -- a horizontal, lightweight standard intended to facilitate net-discoverability, interoperability, and aggregation of judicial opinions and other legal documents in much the same way that OAI has facilitated similar efforts in scientific literature -- just hasn't been done yet; there's nobody clearly vested in doing it. The second is that different jurisdictions -- and different groups within those jurisdictions -- find it difficult to agree on standards, or even come up with a forum in which such a discussion could take place. Finally, preparation of a comprehensive standard is difficult if not impossible, and extremely expensive to undertake -- it is an effort comparable in size and scope to TEI, for many of the same reasons, and indeed it may be even larger. Given that lawyers are involved, it will certainly be more contentious -- and that's not a cheap shot, it's a well-founded comment based on experience.

Bottom line is that many different groups of actors are chipping away at various parts of the problem but nobody knows enough -- or has seen enough benefit -- to justify a full-on standardization. I think we'll be waiting a long time. One is tempted to believe that such a thing might exist as an internal system at the larger commercial publishers, but I think not -- at least not yet. If it exists anywhere, it'd be Thomson -- and you can bet they're not talking.

Thomas R. Bruce
Director, Legal Information Institute
Cornell Law School

Tags: Name:Implementation; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Project manager

Question asked on: 2006-03-30 00:00:00 by Judith

Hello,
I am part of committee charged with updating the guidelines for use of Dublin Core in the University of Chicago Library's digital projects. We have been discussing the differences between the types, Still Image and Image and have come to no agreement about application, in part because some of the materials listed, maps, paintings, and drawings for example, are noted in both. Yet, Still Image is said to be narrower in scope, yet how much narrower?
A second question, why in the definition of Image are photographic prints limited to those of physical objects and do not extend to those of people?

Thank you for any help you can give us,
Judith Dartt


Response given on: 2006-04-24 15:33:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Judith:

Part of the answer to your question lies in the history of the addition of Still Image and Moving Image to the DCMIType vocabulary. In the beginning there was only Image, but a proposal came forward to provide more specificity for image collections, allowing them to categorize materials as "still" or "moving." In general we recommend that collections choose either the broader term "Image," or the two more granular terms, rather than all three together. It's likely that most image collections will find any of those three inadequate, and will likely use another type list as well. This is fine, but we do suggest that at least one of the DCMIType terms be used to ensure interoperability.

All of the examples listed in definitions and comments are just that: examples. They're not meant to limit the definitions in any way.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Vocabulary Terms; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2006-03-29 00:00:00 by Alessandra

I would be grateful if you could tell me if the value of Rights must be a type of right (i.e. intellectual property) or an entity (i.e.Ministry of Education). It may happen that a ministry is the sponsor of a project so it has got all the rights of the outputs of the project (documents, learning objects, newsletters, databases ecc). I hope you can understand my English...
Looking forward to hearing from you
Best regards.
Alessandra Cornero

Response given on: 2006-04-24 15:26:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Alessndra:

The best place to look for guidance information for Dublin Core is "Using Dublin Core" (http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/) which includes specific assistance on each element and refinement recommended by DCMI.

As for your specific question, "Rights" should include a rights statement. The name of a person or entity holding rights over a resource should to in "Rights Holder." These are two separate elements--but only "Rights" is available if you're using Simple Dublin Core.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2006-03-27 00:00:00 by Simon

Having read through a lot of documentation on dates and date ranges, I am confused as to what constitutes an acceptable encoding of a date range in HTML, in particular with reference to DCTERMS.valid.

Firstly, I have seen examples of all four of the following used on various web sites as a scheme for date encoding, both in DCMIPERIODS, and for DCTERMS.date and its refinements.

W3CDTF
W3C-DTF
DCTERMS.w3cDTF
DCTERMS.w3c-DTF

Please could you clarify which is correct.

ISO 8601:2004 allows for using two dates separated by a solidus to specify a range, i.e. 2005-01-01/2005-01-31, but this does not appear to be valid under W3CDTF, so presumably, this is incorrect:

meta name="DCTERMS.valid" scheme="DCTERMS.w3CDTF" content="2005-01-01/2005-01-31"

Is the scheme attribute mandatory? Or is the following valid, but less useful?
meta name="DCTERMS.valid" content="2005-01-01/2005-01-31"


Assuming that the correct way to specify the encoding of a date is DCTERMS.w3cDTF, is the following a valid content string for a DCMIPERIOD?

start=2005-01-01;end=2005-01-31;scheme=DCTERMS.W3CDTF;

And finally, is it necessary to prefix the scheme DCMIPERIOD, i.e.

scheme="DCTERMS.DCMIPERIOD"

or is scheme="DCMIPERIOD" equally valid?

Your assistance in this matter would be greatly appreciated,

Regards,
Simon

Response given on: 2006-04-24 15:20:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Simon:

Date ranges are something of a problem, certainly, but the problem is not limited to HTML encoding. In general we suggest using the DCMI-Period encoding scheme (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-period/) for date ranges, since W3CDTF doesn't accommodate ranges.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Question asked on: 2006-03-23 00:00:00 by Neil

Response given on: 2006-04-02 19:44:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Neil:

In general DCMI relies on specialist communities and their vocabularies for the values that you're attempting to express. We're a small standards organization, propelled primarily by volunteers, and the expertise and resources to attempt such vocabularies is simply beyond us. We have often provided an umbrella for such activities, however, and would urge you to contact us if you wish to work on such issues.

Question asked on: 2006-03-23 00:00:00 by Denise

I am looking for a recommended set of Metadata for identifying the Lifecycle phases of Content Management such as; Plan, Draft, Review, Approve, Publish, Archive, Destroy. Do you a set of Lifecycle attributes that you would recommend for this data set?
Thank you, Denise

Response given on: 2006-04-02 19:52:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Denise:

A few years ago, DCMI spent considerable time and energy attempting to define a core set of administrative metadata, but decided in the end not to recommend a specific set. It seemed pretty clear to us, after the fact, that there were so many needs that went beyond the core set we'd been discussing, that it was of limited value to continue. It was also true that most needs in this area were internal, not designed to require interoperability, that we felt we were attempting to move beyond our area of expertise.

As the world of metadata continues to change, with new methods of content management and content (and metadata) distribution coming along, we continue to feel that it makes more sense for projects to carefully analyze their own needs and do what works for them.

One strategy you might try, though is to look for projects that seem to be similar to yours, or use the same content management application, and see what they've worked out. The DCMI project pages (http://dublincore.org/projects/) is a good place to start.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff

Question asked on: 2006-02-15 00:00:00 by Emily

Can you give me some clarification about the format element? How much information about the creation of the image is supposed to go into the description fo the Format element? Thank you!

Response given on: 2006-03-01 14:10:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Emily:

In general, the purpose of describing the Format of a resource is to make it easier for machines to interact with it. This is one reason why DCMI recommends the use of the IMT standard vocabulary for the element. An IMT term should allow a user (perhaps with the help of a browser or other tool) to know whether or not they can read or use the resource, without having to know too much about the technical details.

As you can see, this doesn't usually require detail about the creation of the resource, and "description" insofar as it might imply a lot of text designed for human understanding, is definitely not recommended. I hope this helps!

Regards,
Diane

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Cataloger

Response given on: 2006-02-28 14:56:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Joergen:

It seems to me that "email" is really a genre of material, or "resource type" in your context. Question asked on: 2006-02-01 00:00:00 by Teresa

I want to know about RDF. I´m looking for a metadata for description images. These images have format tiff. could i use RDF or it´s better use IPTC???

Thanks for all and excuse mi badly english gramnar

Response given on: 2006-02-07 18:38:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Teresa:

I think you've become a bit confused about the choices you need to make and how they fit together into a metadata plan for your project.

First, I assume from your question that you're trying to describe tiff images--this implies that you're talking about still images rather than moving ones. Depending on what these tiff images are depicting, you might want to look at Dublin Core, VRA Core Categories (http://www.vraweb.org/vracore3.htm) [used for art images] or some other metadata schema, perhaps even NewsML (which is what I think you're refering to by IPTC). A lot depends on the context in which you're working and who you'll need to share your data with--be it libraries, news organizations, or museums.

RDF is a syntax or 'wrapper' around your metadata schema. Dublin Core can be used with different syntaxes (RDF, XML, XHTML and others) and so can many of the others. Again, your choices will depend on the context you're working in, who you'll be sharing with, and what your local requirements might be. There are no perfect choices in the abstract sense, it all depends on what you're trying to do.

Some places to look:
RDF Primer: http://www.w3.org/TR/rdf-primer/
DCMI Encoding Guidelines: http://dublincore.org/resources/expressions/
DCMI Training Resources: http://dublincore.org/resources/training/

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2006-02-01 00:00:00 by Evan Prodromou

I'm trying to figure out a way to represent the stage of development of a work.

For example, a document often goes through stages like "draft", "review version", "final".

A piece of software will be in "alpha", "beta", "release candidate", "release".

I note that Dublin Core documents and elements have a property "status", which seems to be similar, and yet that property is not itself an element!

Any suggestions?


Response given on: 2006-02-14 14:19:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Evan:

DC elements are specifically for the purpose of discovery of resources, not necessarily for the management of information about those resources. While the functionality you're looking for is important to any implementation, we've found over time that there's not much of a consensus about what kinds of elements implementors need in the administrative area.

As you note, even DC needs to track the status of elements, and so we use properties suggested by ISO 11179, the standard for metadata registries.

Our suggestion to you would be to look for a similar project to yours, either by browsing our Projects pages (see: http://dublincore.org/projects/) , and see whether you can determine what they're using (asking them is sometimes effective!) and how well those elements work.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2006-01-24 00:00:00 by Guy

How should I handle dc.title across multiple pages in a long document?

Response given on: 2006-01-26 05:51:00 by Palle Aagaard

Hi Guy,

You have a "Long document which consist of several pages". I understand this as the "long document consist of several separate files". If my understanding is right I have the following answer:

In Denmark we have an eGovernment standard for electronic reports published at the web. This standard describe how to separate the reports into chapters, which e.g. mean "one chapter one separate file". These chapters are linked toghether by ordinary hyperlink. There is also a title-page (a separate file linked to the chapters). Only this title page (file) is embedded with metadata including dc.title. DC.title then contain the title of the report. It ispossible to use dc.Relation.HasPart to mentionthe file-names for all the chapters in the report. This metadata-element will also be embeddedin the title-file.

Questions are welcome

Regards

Palle

Response given on: 2006-01-30 19:53:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Robert:

The date that a record was added to a database seems to be an administrative need, rather than something that would be part of a descriptive element set (what Dublin Core is designed for). DCMI recognizes that the needs for administrative data cannot really be satisfied by the DC element sets, but though we've tried to come up with a recommendation for administrative elements we've never managed to agree, given the diversity of projects and needs.

Question asked on: 2006-01-09 00:00:00 by Nico

Is there a reason for using MARCXML and DUBLIN CORE for one object in a RDF document?

I was thinking of doing that for the reason DC is a popular metadataschema for Information Retrieval on the web and USMARC not. On the other hand USMARC gives better support for more specifistic and larger metadata. With using both metadataschemes I end up getting redundancy, what I don't like. So therefore this question.

thanks in advance
Nico Roosenboom

Response given on: 2006-01-10 15:06:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Hi Nico,I'm not sure I understand how you plan to use MARCXML (which I understand to be a non-RDF-based XML format ) in an RDF document (other than as an XML Literal? )But, yes, as you suggest, there may be value in making your resource descriptions available in multiple formats to meet the requirements of applications which consume only particular formats, or to take advtantage of the descriptive features of one particular format that are not available in another format.Even within a purely RDF-based application, it may be worth exposing both, sayresource:x rdfs:label "My Resource" .andresource:x dc:title "My Resource" .A general-purpose application which "understands" only the RDF Schema language might make use of the former triple to generate a label in human-readable displays, but not the latter; whereas a DC-based application might ignore the rdfs:label triple, but make use of the dc:title triple.Your decision probably depends on what advance knowledge you have of the applications which will be processing/consuming your metadata.RegardsPete Johnston

Question asked on: 2006-01-03 00:00:00 by Robert

Following up on a similar questions asked by another, I would like to know exactly how DC works when using an in-house database (oracle based). How does indexing a date, for example, work with other databases if I choose the same field name as in DC? I'm not I understand all the intricate details of how choosing DC metadata elements would work with ours. If we have a field that is similar to one of yours, for example, how do you "map" it to one of yours (how do you even know if it's the same definition)? I keep hearing how important it is to standardize, so that asset information can be shared, but I have yet to see a true-to-life example of how it works. Thanks so much.
Robert.

Response given on: 2006-01-04 10:06:00 by Palle Aagaard

Dear Robert,

Your question is not so much a DC question as it is a question about how databases can become interopero
able.

The crusial item is the semantic of the fields in the data-base. If you want to merge fields from two different
databases the programmer has to specify which fields can be merged. An mergin two databases will normally
only give mening if the semantics of the fields which are merged are the same.

An example:
DatabaseA with surname_field SURNAME and name_field NAME. DatabaseB with firstname_field FIRSTNAME and
name_field NAME.

You can not meaningfull (semantic)merge e.g. SURNAME and FIRSTNAME fields. You can merge the two NAME
fieldsIF the semantic definitions are the same. This semantic definitions are made when you establish the databases - and it is the "owner" of the database who decide these definitions.

Of cource it can be decided to choose a DC definition of a e.g. date_field or a title_field when you establish your
database. This database will then be interoperable (can merge with) other databases which uses the same definitions (semantics)

further questions are welcome

Regards

Palle

Question asked on: 2005-11-26 00:00:00 by Carl Eric

Greetings,
I have a question regarding the dc:type element, if i understand correctly, it is not necessary to use the pre-defined controlled vocabulary terms... Am I right?

Actually I am implementing dublincore elements in Adobe XMP, and i was wondering if some additional vocabularies could be added, and if so, how and where do i register them??

I would like to register the following dc:type vocabularies:
- ID3V1 Metadata sound genre vocabulary
- Portable application description software Category class (XML_DIZ_INFO/Program_Info/Program_Category_Class)
of www.padspec.org.

Are these vocabularies appropriate for dc:type? As they describe the genres of the resource depending on the high-level type, no?

On another note, I would also like to know if there are official sub-category classes (like above) for Images, Text and MovingImage types?? (From any standard organization, the only thing i have come close to was the IMDB Genre classification)...

Finally, is there a way in RDF to specify the exact classification used? Would it be possible to use an URN to identify these types of vocabularies??

Any clarification or help would be greatly appreciated.
Thanks, Carl


Response given on: 2005-11-26 16:03:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Carl:

You are correct that use of DCMIType is not required, however, it is recommended that they be used, even if you use additional, more domain-specific or granular vocabularies as well. In this way, your data can be more easily re-purposed by others.

We'd certainly applaud your efforts to add other vocabularies to your implementation, but it's not now possible to register them. The best alternative might be to take a look at the work being done with Application Profiles (see the tutorials on the DCMI Webpage under RESOURCES-->TRAINING for some information to get you started). Documenting your usage of a vocabulary in this way is a good step towards registration. At one time DCMI had hoped to register vocabularies for implementers in this way, but unfortunately a lack of resources forced the abandonment of that plan. Other registry projects are likely to assist in this effort within the next few years, but none are yet available for actual registration.

I'm not familiar with the vocabularies you propose to use, but so long as they reflect genre and not technical format they should be appropriate. Again, if you use DCMIType as well, you make your data more interoperable with other data. In answer to your question about other sub-vocabularies, there are none specifically recommended by DCMI, but I know that some projects are using them, though sadly it's not always easy to figure out who's doing what in the absence of a distributed registry infrastructure. You might ask on DC-General, and see if anyone can suggest something.

As for your last question concerning RDF--there is a proposed recommendation for Qualified DC in RDF/XML (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-rdf-xml/) but progress on moving it to recommended status was halted due to the untimely death of one of the authors. I think caution should be exercised in using the proposed recommendation, as there have been some changes in thinking since it was drafted. Given that, I'd suggest that you ask your RDF question on the DC-Architecture list (see the SUBSCRIBE page at: http://dublincore.org/about/contact/#dcgeneral for information on all the DC mailing lists). Most of the people conversant with current RDF thinking hang out on that list, and although you may not find a consensus there, you may find enough of one to help you make a decision on moving forward.

Good luck with your project!

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Programmer

Hi I am trying to put up a RDF with a date scheme but when I validate it in http://www.w3.org/RDF/Validator/ there is an error.

My sample rdf is as follows:
<?xml version="1.0" encoding="ISO-8859-1" ?>
<rdf:RDF xmlns:rdf="http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#" xmlns:rdfs="http://www.w3.org/2000/01/rdf-schema#" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" xmlns:dcq="http://purl.org/dc/qualifiers/1.1/" xmlns:dct="http://purl.org/dc/terms/" xmlns:vCard="http://www.w3.org/2001/vcard-rdf/3.0#">
<rdf:Description rdf:about="http://mydoc.doc">
<dc:date>
<rdf:Description>
<dct:DateScheme>Future Review Date</dct:DateScheme>
<dct:W3CDTF>
<rdf:value>2005-12-30</rdf:value>
</dct:W3CDTF>
</rdf:Description>
</dc:date>
</rdf:Description>
</rdf:RDF>

The error message is:

Error: {E202} Expected whitespace found: &apos;2005-12-30&apos;.[Line = 8, Column = 24]

Grateful if you can advice what's wrong.
Thanks.



Response given on: 2005-11-23 09:22:00 by Pete Johnston

Roger,

It might be helpful to remember that RDF/XML is a representation of an RDF graph. An RDF graph is made up of nodes andpredicate or property arcs linking the nodes. When the RDF graph is represented in RDF/XML,the outermost XML element (other than the rdf:RDF element) represents a node in the graph, and the child XML elements each represent predicate/property arcs. And child elements of those XML elements represent nodes, and so on throughout the XML tree. This alternation of node element/property element is sometimes referred to as "striping". (There are some syntactic features of RDF/XML which can alter the "striping" but I won't bother about them here)

So looking at your example:

-your outermost rdf:Description element represents a node (with the URI http://mydoc.doc)
- your dc:date element represents a property arc with the URI http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/date
- your next rdf:Description element represents a blank node
- your dct:DateScheme element represents a property arc with the URI http://purl.org/dc/terms/DateScheme (N.B. DCMI does not define a property with this URI; there is a class with this URI)
- your dct:W3CDTF element represents a property arc with the URI http://purl.org/dc/terms/W3CDTF (Again. DCMI does not define a property with this URI; there is a class with this URI)
- your rdf:value element is treated as a "typed node element" i.e. it represents a node andanrdf:type predicate. The parse doesn't expect a node element to have literal content, so it complains about the content "2005-12-30".

First, I think you intend the rdf:value element to represent a property not a node. Second, I'm not quite sure what your intent is with the use of dct:DateScheme, but I think you want to define a subproperty of the dc:date property to represent a "date of future review"?

So essentially I think you need something like the RDF/XML below, with your:dateOfFutureReview defined as a subpropert of the dc:dateproperty. Sometimes the AskDCMI system seems to scramble XML documents, so just in case this is eligible, I've put a temporary copy at

http://homes.ukoln.ac.uk/~lispj/temp/askdcmi/reviewdate.rdf

Regards

Pete Johnston

Tags: Name:RDF; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2005-11-22 00:00:00 by Joanne

Please could you supply me with a list of subject/vocabulary schemes which have been registered with DCMI.

Response given on: 2005-11-26 15:32:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Joanne:

The list of currently recommended schemes is at: http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/#H4 At one time, DCMI was planning to "register" more schemes, but after some extensive discussion, determined that it could not muster sufficient support to manage such a registry. This means that the list you see at the URL above is unlikely to grow very much, and will not be sufficient for implementers. There is some renewed insterest lately around registry applications and functionality, so it's likely that this sorry situation will improve within the next year or so. Let's all cross our fingers!

Question asked on: 2005-11-10 00:00:00 by wendy

Hello and good afternoon,
I wanted to know what exactly are DC meta tags and why are they used on some websites and not on others? what purpose do they serve search engines and visitors? Do they serve any purpose at all? Your website dublincore.org was difficult for me to navigate to find these answers. Can you please help me with the answers to these questions, or know who on the Internet can help me answer these questions? Thank you.

Response given on: 2005-11-22 14:22:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Wendy:

Dublin Core is a standard for describing resources, and not always used in the context of "meta tags" (which are associated with HTML or XHTML expressions). Like all standards, it has not been adopted across the board--only where it has been deemed useful. What purpose the metadata description serves really depends entirely on the context and goals of the project that uses it, so there's no "one size fits all" answer to your questions.

You might consider taking a look at "Using Dublin Core" (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/) which has some introductory material about how DC is used. Also, our Projects pages (see: http://dublincore.org/projects/) might be helpful to illustrate how DC has been used in a variety of implementations.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-11-08 00:00:00 by Olivier

Is thera any research or framework
about DC metadatas including Geographic datas
We are currently seeking free datas from Public Geographic Information System (as "GPS" datas) for an artistic purpose and we want to include it in our new indexation method (new website).
If somebody is interested in cooperating with us, he will be welcome!
Best regards
Olivier
project coordinator
http://edith.paname.org (french only)
www.sound-drop.org (resume in english)

Response given on: 2005-11-22 14:03:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Olivier:

Dublin Core developed a number of structured methods for geographic information a few years ago. See:

DCMI Box (http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-box/)
DCMI Point (http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-point/)

Our current recommendation is based on our DC Abstract Model (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/abstract-model/) and stems from the notion that geographic data is most properly part of a separate "package" of information that is linked to descriptive metadata via a number of possible ways.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann

Question asked on: 2005-11-01 00:00:00 by Christopher

I noticed that the language element has the xml:lang attribute assigned. Is the intention to place a lnaguage code into the 'language' attribute, or to place a textual name in the language specified by xml:lang?

Also do you recommend a suitable sub list of supported languages or can all of the 3 character language codes be used?

Response given on: 2005-11-11 18:22:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Christopher:

The DC:language element and the xml:lang attribute are talking about two different things. The Language element describes the language of the described resource; the xml:lang attribute describes the language of the statement. An xml:lang attribute might be used, for instance, to differentiate between parallel titles or translated titles, or subject headings in different languages. In this way, the descriptive metadata can be used more specifically by a service to meet user needs.

There are two available language encoding schemes recommended by DCMI: ISO639-2 (http://www.loc.gov/standards/iso639-2/langhome.html) and RFC3066 (http://www.ietf.org/rfc/rfc3066.txt). The RFC is probably the most flexible option, but you should take a look at both before determining which meets your requirements.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:XML; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff

Question asked on: 2005-10-17 00:00:00 by Nada

I have found at the FIATECH Data Standards Clearinghouse a document providing characteristics to compare different interoperability systems. I couldn't find an answer on the DCM website for all the characteristics mentioned. These are:

1- The cost per one interface; a general indication of the typical cost to build one software-mapping interface.

2- Focus: Usage-driven vs. Comperhensive; usage-driven approaches are only for information that organizations agree should be exchanged. A comprehensive approach tries to define a standard that is completely open and flexible to any data exchange.

3- Scope; multiple or single-domain.

4- Extensibility; extensible vs. fixed: extensible allows for two parties to extend the standard without having to go through the full standardization process. A fixed solution would force people to build separate special purpose interfaces.

5- Model content; focused (detailed descriprion of data), broad (generalized frameworks that provide some high level data description) or mixed.

6- Usage; exchange or repository sharing

7- Exchange file content; data model + data (both contained in the exchanged file) vs. data-only (contained in the exchanged file)

8- Data model approach; abstract-domain vs. partitioned domain: the abstract domain adresses the need to support multiple subject domains, whereas the partitioned domain approach can accomodate by partitioning the multiple domains into some domains, which are general purpose and reusable and others of which are very subject specific.

Could-you please let me know where the DCM stands within these characterisitics?

Thank you,
Nada.

Response given on: 2005-10-19 14:55:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Nada:

I'll do my best with these criteria, but with the caveat that I'm not sure what an "interoperability system" is in this context, so some of these may not apply very well to Dublin Core.

"1- The cost per one interface; a general indication of the typical cost to build one software-mapping interface."

I don't think this really applies, since DC is not an application.

"2- Focus: Usage-driven vs. Comperhensive; usage-driven approaches are only for information that organizations agree should be exchanged. A comprehensive approach tries to define a standard that is completely open and flexible to any data exchange."

Dublin Core has been created by consensus, and though intended to be "core" and not "comprehensive" applies fairly well across domains. It is, by the way, already a standard: ISO Standard 15836-2003 (February 2003) and NISO Standard Z39.85-2001 (September 2001).

"3- Scope; multiple or single-domain."

Dublin Core is definitely used in multiple domains: for confirmation take a look at our project pages: http://dublincore.org/projects/

"4- Extensibility; extensible vs. fixed: extensible allows for two parties to extend the standard without having to go through the full standardization process. A fixed solution would force people to build separate special purpose interfaces."

DC has always been extensible, and the DC community is working closely with others to define appropriate methods to define Application Profiles to allow re-use of properties from a number of schemas. The DC Training pages (http://dublincore.org/resources/training/) include tutorials on Application Profiles you might find useful.

"5- Model content; focused (detailed description of data), broad (generalized frameworks that provide some high level data description) or mixed."

The Dublin Core Abstract Model is described here: http://dublincore.org/documents/abstract-model/

"6- Usage; exchange or repository sharing"

Simple Dublin Core is the minimum standard used in the Open Archives Initiative Protocol for Metadata Harvesting, and Qualified Dublin Core (a richer extension) is used as a base format by a number of important metadata aggregators.

"7- Exchange file content; data model + data (both contained in the exchanged file) vs. data-only (contained in the exchanged file)"

Normally data only.

"8- Data model approach; abstract-domain vs. partitioned domain: the abstract domain adresses the need to support multiple subject domains, whereas the partitioned domain approach can accomodate by partitioning the multiple domains into some domains, which are general purpose and reusable and others of which are very subject specific."

I'm not sure this applies, but you'll need to make your own determination on this one based on the information I've provided.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:DCMI Glossary; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Cataloger


Question asked on: 2005-10-13 00:00:00 by David

I'd like a suggestion on the best content of DC.title for parts of a Web-based publication. Assume I have a Web publication called "My Project", whose HTML home page has the tag '<meta name="DC.title" content="My Project"/>'. The publication has parts as separate Web pages with titles like "Introduction", "Acknowledgments", "Contents", etc. Each component page has a tag like '<link rel="DCTERMS.isPartOf" href="http://myserver.school.edu/MyProject.html"/>, relating it to the home page. What's the best way to handle their title element? Just '<meta name="DC.title" content="Introduction"/>' and so on, or something like '<meta name="DC.title" content="Introduction (to My Project)"/>' to be more self-explanatory?

Response given on: 2005-10-19 14:10:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

David:

My inclination for titles with little semantic content (like "Introduction," "Contents," etc.) would be to suggest that you use them as subtitles. As an example:

My Project: Introduction
My Project: Contents

This strategy might also relieve you of the necessity for making "isPartOf" links, which can get pretty difficult to maintain over time.

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-10-03 00:00:00 by Jackie

We are just working on a pilot project implementing a Content Management System on the postgraduate recruitment section of the university web site. We are using some of the Dublin Core elements and need to include the names of the academic departments involved in running each course and producing the description which is displayed on the web site.

We have decided that the Creator element is the best one to use, but where multiple departments are involved, should we have separate Creator entries for each, or can multiple departments be listed in one?

Also, should be be using the full form of name for each one or can we leave out "Department" and "School" (we have departments within schools) eg the proper form of name is: "School of Agriculture, Policy and Development. Department of Agricultural and Food Economics" or can we use this shortened version "Agriculture Policy and Development. Agricultural and Food Economics" The cataloguer in me wants to do the former (ie use the proper form of name) but the project manager argues that users won't search for "school" or "department" so we don't need to include those words. Any guidance about how strict we should be on this is very gratefully received!

Response given on: 2005-10-19 13:55:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Jackie:

The role that you describe for your departments seems appropriate for either Creator or Contributor, although I must admit a continuing bias towards using Contributor for corporate bodies. Best practice for multiples is to have separate iterations, rather than lumping them together. This is particularly important for corporate bodies where there's hierarchical considerations (as you point out) and punctuation doesn't work very well to separate them.

Eventually you're going to need to manage these corporate names in some way to make your information display well as your information scales up. I'd suggest that you either adopt AACR2 conventions for constructing those names or use the entire hierarchy.

As for searching, you need to point out to your project manager that the data will likely last lots longer than your search application (and is much more expensive to create and maintain), which argues that the search application conform to the data, not the other way around. A big rule for me has always been: DON'T MANIPULATE YOUR DATA TO MAKE UP FOR INADEQUACIES OF YOUR SYSTEM (Shouting intentional!).

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Vocabulary Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian

Question asked on: 2005-09-14 00:00:00 by Ana

Is there a property/key to indicate the level of cataloging? For instance, the thesis is submitted and accepted, but the catalogers have not completely described the work (however, it is searchable and accessible to the users). We would like to add an element where we would mention that the work has not yet been completely cataloged. Is there a recommendation for this?
Thank you very much.

Response given on: 2005-10-04 15:56:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Ana:

The status information you're describing falls primarily under the rubric of 'administrative metadata'--something that Dublin Core does not handle. We've found over time that most projects must be quite specific in how they handle things like status and level of description.

Our recommendation would be that you look at similar projects (the DCMI Projects pages are often good for this, http://dublincore.org/projects/) and see what they do, and whether they're happy with their decisions!

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-09-11 00:00:00 by Judith

Is Dublin Core, simple, qualified, or both, considered a content standard?

Thank you,
Judith

Response given on: 2005-10-04 15:45:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Judith:

Simple Dublin Core is both a NISO and ISO Standard:

ISO Standard 15836-2003 (February 2003): http://www.niso.org/international/SC4/n515.pdf

NISO Standard Z39.85-2001 (September 2001): http://www.niso.org/standards/resources/Z39-85.pdf

Qualified Dublin Core, which includes additional elements, element refinements and encoding schemes, is a growing entity, and has not been submitted to standards agencies. I think it could fairly be said to be a 'de facto' standard, in the sense that it has become something of a 'lingua franca' in the metadata world.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-09-08 00:00:00 by John

I am a developer working on an application that must support multiple languages. Response given on: 2005-09-15 13:31:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

If you go to the DCMI Registry (http://dublincore.org/dcregistry/) and click on "Browse | Search" and look at the language preference list (there's a link just below the blue header). Question asked on: 2005-08-31 00:00:00 by Roger

Hi there,

I am considering annotating a collection of digital photos with DC. If I encode the DC using RDF, and use the dumddown algorithm as described at http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-rdf-xml/#sec3, how compatible will this be with existing DC/RDF parsers? That is, will all/most/some/none DC/RDF parser be able to read this?

Is this algorithm something that people have generally accepted, and moving forward will be much more common? Thanks.


Response given on: 2005-10-04 15:36:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Roger:

The document you cite, with the dumb down algorithm, is a DCMI recommendation, which means that it has been vetted through the Dublin Core Community, and reflects the best consensus about how to do things properly. That said, there's no guarantee that *every* RDF parser that professes to know DC will be in compliance with these recommendations. As you know, this world is fairly unregulated, and some people tend to go off into the bushes!

Good luck with your project.

Question asked on: 2005-08-27 00:00:00 by vasanth

How can I extract automatic metadata?

Response given on: 2005-10-04 15:31:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Vasanth:

Metadata extraction is a fairly complex proposition, not really answerable in a few paragraphs. There are a number of projects studying the how-to of automated metadata creation, but for most of them it's not really at a production level, push-the-button stage.

If you need a quick tool, you might look into DC-Dot (http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/dcdot/). Another project working in this area is iVia, associated with the University of California, Riverside (http://ivia.ucr.edu/).

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-07-25 00:00:00 by Paul

Dear all,

I am currently implementing application schemas to represent data, and the data about that data (the metadata). This is where the Dublin Core metadata comes in, in describing that data. I would like to make use of the terms you describe, as well as defining some additional terms which come from the IEEE LOM, and then ensure that all XML files conform to both the Dublin core metadata schema and the IEE LOM. How would I go about this?

I've tried downloading the simple DC XML schema for use in my application, but using XMLSpy, when writing a sample XML file out from it, I have to choose a root element from one of the 15 terms of the DC metadata schema, but I want the root element to be something more decriptive like 'metadata' and then followed by the dublin core terms.

Best Regards,
Paul Kiddie

Response given on: 2005-07-27 14:15:00 by Pete Johnston


Paul,


Taking your last point first and working backwards ;-), the current DCMI-recommended XML binding for DC metadata [1] does not mandate any particular root/container XML element. It is left to the implementer to provide a container element. The reason for this is that different implementers may choose to apply different constraints to the content model of that container element, in order to reflect the particular DC "application profile" for which they are providing a binding. The document at [2] provides some examples of how you might do this.

The question of using DC and LOM metadata terms in combination is rather more complex. When you say you wish to "ensure that all XML files conform to both the Dublin core metadata schema and the IEE LOM", do you mean that you wish to create a single XML document that can be interpreted as both a LOM-XML document (conforming to the LOM XML binding) and a DC-XML document (conforming to the DC XML binding)?

If so, I don't think that is possible.

You _may_ be able to construct a document that is syntactically valid in terms of the XML schemas provide for LOM and for DC. The LOM XML binding (I think?) requires a fixed name for the root XML element (lom:lom); as the DC XML binding permits any name for the root/container XML element, in theory you could use lom:lom as he name of the container element.

So far so good.

However, it is critical to remember that in each case the XML format is simply a binding for an information structure which is constructed according to the "abstract model" associated with the individual metadata standard. i.e. the XML document is interpreted in accordance with the rules of the binding to generate a data structure described by the "abstract model" associated with the individual metadata standard.

For DC, that abstract model is described in [3]. A DC application interprets an XML document conforming to the DC XML binding in terms of that binding and maps components within the XML document to constructs within the DCMI Abstract Model: each child XML element within the container element is interpreted as representing a statement about a resource; the XML QName which is the XML element name is mapped to a URI, and that URI is interpreted as the URI of a property; the XML element content is interpreted as a value string; the XML QName that appears as the value of the xsi:type attribute is mapped to a URI, and that URI is interpreted as the URI of an encoding scheme; and so on.

For LOM, the abstract model is not explicitly described, but the LOM standard describes a LOM instance as a hierarchical/tree structure of "LOM data elements" where one element is nested within another. "Leaf" elements are associated with specific datatypes which determine how their values are to be interpreted. A LOM application interprets an XML document conforming to the LOM XML binding in terms of that binding: again, it maps components within the XML document to constructs within the LOM hierachical/tree structure. Because an XML document is also a representation of a hierarchical data structure, that mapping is fairly straightforward - but it is important to remember that the components within a LOM-XML document are different things from the data elements within a LOM instance.

So, let's go back to our XML document with lom:lom as root/container element. Consider the child XML elements of this root element: for a DC application, they represent statements; for a LOM application they represent LOM category data elements. A DC application interprets the content of the child XML element as a value string; a LOM application expects further child XML elements and interprets the in terms of the tree structure described by the LOM standard.

The examples in 6.1 and 6.2 of [1] are misleading in this respect: they may be syntactically valid but at least some parts of that document have no interpretation defined by the DC XML binding.

The difficulty arises because the two abstract models used by DC and by LOM are quite different: they include _different_ types of construct (in the DC abstract model all terms are identified/referenced using URIs and there is no notion of element containment/nesting; and in the LOM abstract model there is no notion of properties and statements and no notion that terms are identified by URIs). The types of term used in each are different (LOM data elements are containers within a hierarchical structure, and the interpretation of that structure is defined by the LOM standard; DC "elements" are properties which are referenced in statements which described relationships between resources). Terms which are usable in one model are not meaningful in the other.

So, in short, LOM data elements can not be used in DC metadata descriptions, and vice versa. Rather it is necessary to analyse the information which is being conveyed by a construct within one standard, and to create the appropriate components which _can_ be used to represent that information within the other standard.

For the case of the LOM, this process is made more complex by the fact that the interpretation of a LOM data element is determined by its context within the LOM tree data structure: it is not possible to say simply that, say, all LOM "leaf" data elements are equivalent to properties. Rather it is necessary to examine each part of the LOM instance in turn, analyse its interpretation in terms of the LOM abstract model and the LOM standard, and identify what components are required to express that information in terms of the DC abstract model, i.e. what properties, vocabulary/syntax encoding schemes are required to express the information represented by LOM data elements, LOM datatypes etc.

Mikael Nilsson provides an excellent account of this work for mapping between the LOM and the RDF data model at [4]. He notes the complexity of the process, and the fact that it has to be done by considering each LOM data element individually. Because the DC abstract model is compatible with the RDF data model, the terms used within the LOM RDF binding [5] are usable within DC metadata descriptions (and indeed the LOM RDF binding makes use of terms from DCMI vocabularies).

So, for example, the property URIs

http://ltsc.ieee.org/2002/09/lom-general#structure
http://ltsc.ieee.org/2002/09/lom-general#aggregationLevel

can be used in statements in DC metadata descriptions. To use them in the DC XML binding it would be necessary to construct suitable XML schemas, so that those URIs can be represented as the QNames of XML elements.

Some work is planned to build on the LOM RDF binding to develop a mapping from LOM to the DC abstract model, and hopefully this will make it clearer how to address the sort of issues which I think you are seeking to address.

Pete Johnston

[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/
[2] http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/qdc/2003/04/02/notes/
[3] http://dublincore.org/documents/abstract-model/
[4] http://kmr.nada.kth.se/papers/SemanticWeb/LOMRDFBinding-ARIADNE.pdf
[5] http://kmr.nada.kth.se/el/ims/metadata.html

Question asked on: 2005-07-11 00:00:00 by Denise

Can I declare a new element in an application profile? Or, must I first declare the new element in my local element set and then reference it in the application profile?

In my records management application profile, I am going to reference DC elements but since the application profile deals with records management, I also have to include records management elements (that obviously DC does not address, e.g. retention period, disposition authority, etc). Do the latter elements have to be declared first in my local 'records management element set' before they can be included in the application profile? Or can I create them within the application profile itself?

Thanks,
Denise

Response given on: 2005-07-12 05:59:00 by Pete Johnston

Denise,As you suggest, an application profile "references" terms rather than "declaring" them, so you should "declare" your application-specific terms before referencing them in your application profile. At a minimum your term declaration should be a human-readable document which specifies- the URI for the term- the "type" of the term, as defined by the DCMI Abstract Model [1] (property, vocabulary encoding scheme/class, syntax encoding scheme/datatype ) - a definition for the term- any relationships to existing terms (e.g. refinement/subproperty relationships )You may also wish to consider providing a machine-processable version of that declaration using the RDF Vocabulary Definition Language (RDF Schema ).RegardsPete Johnston [1] http://dublincore.org/documents/abstract-model/
