Question asked on: 2005-07-06 00:00:00 by Kathy

Abstract
Can an abstract be taken from the text of a web site word for word? What do you mean that an abstract can only be formal?

Response given on: 2005-08-17 13:15:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Kathy:

It is true that the refinement Abstract was intended to be used when Description was not sufficient. The proposers of the refinement felt that there was a substantive difference between an Description (regardless of source) and a abstract created according to formal standards. This was particularly a concern for those describing scholarly papers for which an abstract was an important part of a submission.

That said, if the distinction isn't important in your context it might be best to stick with Description. I've often taken a description from the text of a web site, and recommend that strategy often.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2005-07-04 00:00:00 by Alexander

Hi, you use Document Status within your own documents, as I would like to with in mine. We manage many iterations of a specific document and may also have a number of published versions on record, including their respective interim versions.

I do not see a Term or Term Refinement that covers the state of a document. In my case I would expect the value to be a title, version, state (ie awaiting approval etc) value.

Is there a term that specifically (as opposed to ambiguously) supports document state?

What would you recommend?

Response given on: 2005-08-17 13:06:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Alexander:

You are correct that Dublin Core maintains a status for all its documents. However, we consider the status an administrative function, not a descriptive one. If you click on the metadata link at the bottom of the first page of "Using Dublin Core" (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/) you'll note that the link goes only to the current version, and not to the dated versions.

We could, if we wished, reflect those links in our metadata (though the maintenance would be more onerous). Note that in the Relation element, there are refinements for IsVersionOf and HasVersion, which can be used to link between versions if desired.

It's certainly true that you want to keep track of the status of documents, but using an element or refinement to do so is not necessarily the best way to do that. If you're using a database to manage your documents, you'll likely be storing much of the administrative information there, and working out a method for managing status within that context is probably your best bet.

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2005-06-28 00:00:00 by Denise

I would like to ask something that sounds very obvious but which still niggles at me a bit.

If I change one word in the DC term definitions (element and refinements), have I then created local terms? In each of the definitions, for my particular local application, I would like to change the word "resource" to "record".

Similarly, with all of the other definitons.

Response given on: 2005-08-17 10:53:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Denise:

In general, when building an Application Profile (which is what you're doing) the context you place around that profile should obviate the need to change wording in the way you suggest. So the simple answer is, no--don't change the wording.

Question asked on: 2005-06-28 00:00:00 by Roger

Hi there,

I am considering annotating a collection of digital photos with DC. I was just wondering why the encoding schemes are defined as RDF classes and not RDF properties. Is it to make them more closely correspond to RDF collections? Thanks.


Response given on: 2005-06-29 04:04:00 by Pete Johnston

Hi Roger,I'm not sure that the correspondence with RDF collections was a consideration. Rather, I think it was the notion that a reference to an encoding scheme indicates the "type" of the value that made it seem appropriate to model them as classes. So e.g. in the case of a resource being associated with an LCSH subject heading, the relationship between resource and LCSH heading is one of (resource ) "has-as-subject" (value ), and the value is an instance of the class of LCSH headings.RegardsPete Johnston

Question asked on: 2005-06-03 00:00:00 by Russell

None of the examples in the dublin core documentation talks about using dublin core relations in HTML anchor elements. Can I use dublin core in HTML anchors? For example:

<a title="J. R. Shoenfield. Mathematical Logic. Addison-Wesley, 1967." href="URN:ISBN:0201070286" rel="DCTERMS.references">...</a>

Response given on: 2005-06-05 11:50:00 by Pete Johnston

Russell,The DCMI recommendations for encoding DC metadata in X/HTML do not adopt that convention, so an application written to support the DCMI recommendation would not extract DC metadata from the "a" X/HTML element.Having said that, it seems to me your construct is consistent with the semantics of the X/HTML "a" element and the "rel" attribute, so in theory you could define your own X/HTML profile to indicate that you were encoding DC metadata in this way?One point to note is that you would need to clarify what the subject of that statement is.For info, the development work on XHTML 2.0 has led to a proposal for an attribute-based syntax for RDF:http://www.formsplayer.com/notes/rdf-a.htmlwhich (see section 6 ) would support almost exactly the construct you describe - the only difference being that (I think ) in RDF/A the value of the rel attribute is a URI or XML QName. Note also the point there that "The statements are not, however, about the document -- the two anchors in this example will generate two unique [anonymous node]s"N.B. As far as I know, RDF/A is still currently work-in-progress, but if it is integrated into XHTML 2.0, I imagine DCMI would amend the DC-in-XHTML recommendation in some way to reflect that.RegardsPete Johnston

Question asked on: 2005-06-01 00:00:00 by alessandra bianchi

Hello,
for my single-author bibliography Application Profile I thought all was clear in my head, but some doubt still arise.
About the TITLE element, where to put the classical "subtitle": in the same entry of the main Title, or in the refinement Alternative?
And, if the first case, could I use 2 subfields in the implementation of the same Title element - that is: one for the main part, the other for the subtitle?
Thank you for any help
Ale


Response given on: 2005-08-17 10:44:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Ale:

These are good questions, but I'm afraid that there are no hard and fast answers. As you have noted, Dublin Core doesn't have a clear way to separate and describe subtitles. Some projects come up with internal rules about what to do with "important" subtitles, others do the simple thing and keep them with the main title, using a colon to separate.

In one sense, the most important thing is consistency--with consistent data, an application that wants to separate subtitles from main titles can do so, using the punctuation to parse the subtitle, whether or not you do the work up front.

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Response given on: 2005-06-03 16:25:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Peter:

I took a look at your first example:

http://www.japanesestudies.org.uk/reviews/2005/Iles.html

and you've made a good start! I have the following suggestions:

1. You have the author listed as Creator and Contributor, and in the Subject area. If he is indeed the author only, it's best to choose either Creator or Contributor for your authors and include the name once. You might want to put the first author in Creator and all subsequent in Contributor, but whatever you do, choose something and be consistent.

2. If you're using the DCMIType vocabulary in Type (as is recommended), you need to use the term "Text" in this instance, not repeat "text/html" as you also have included (correctly) in Format.

3. In Identifier, you should reference the resource you're describing, which is represented by the URL: http://www.japanesestudies.org.uk/reviews/2005/Iles.html The ISSN and title you include is for the journal itself, which is a separate resource. You might more properly include the journal title and ISSN in an IsPartOf refinement if you wish. This would be expressed as:

4. Because this is a review of another work, you might wish to make standard reference to the other work, in addition to (or instead of) the inclusion of the work in the Description. This could look like:

5. If you haven't already reviewed "Expressing Dublin Core in HTML/XHTML meta and link elements (http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-html/) you should surely do so!

6. Including an abstract or a first paragraph into your description might well serve as a good indication to users of the subject and scope of the resource. Particularly if others capture your metadata and re-use it, that extra effort might pay off!

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane

Question asked on: 2005-05-16 00:00:00 by Johan

I'm writing an article for a document management course, about metadata and Dublin Core especially. This article will be used afterwards for implementations of Dublin Core with CMS solutions.
What I would like to know is what possible evolutions we can expect in the future of Dublin Core and metadata standards.

Response given on: 2005-06-03 16:32:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Johan:Dublin Core is celebrating its Tenth Anniversary this year, and I'm not sure any of us could have predicted a decade ago what we'd be experiencing now. So, anything I might say in answer to your very interesting question has to be regarded as crystal ball gazing, and I'm not so sure about the crystal ball!That said, I would predict that we would see additional development of domain specific standards for metadata expression, using as a basis a number of extant formats. We will also see new formats (though, perhaps some of that has slowed? ) Dublin Core is likely to continue to be a "lingua franca" mediating meaning amongst those developments, and possibly leading the way in filling in the missing infrastructure pieces to assist in the development of the Semantic Web. Please check with me in another decade and see how good my crystal ball is behaving!Regards,Diane

Question asked on: 2005-05-11 00:00:00 by Sadegh

An NSF document sayas:
"For projects that plan to post material on the web, the proposal should include a sustainability plan for tagging these products with descriptive metadata (http://dublincore.org/ and http://metamanagement.comm.nsdlib.org/outline.html) to ensure they can be indexed and cataloged within the National Science Digital Library (www.nsdl.org)."

Question asked on: 2005-05-09 00:00:00 by Andy

If another metadata scheme, such as e-GMS "extends" a DCMI term, is that extension only valid within that scheme, or can it be somehow attributed to DCMI. For example...

e-GMS defines the element 'date' and maps it to the term 'date' in DC. Therefore full terms 'DC.date' and 'eGMS.date' are equivalent.

e-GMS defines the qualifier 'available' for 'date' and again maps it to the qualifier 'available' in DC. Therefore full terms 'DC.date.available' and 'eGMS.date.available' are equivalent.

e-GMS also defines the qualifier 'declared' for 'date' but there is no equivalent term in DC. Therefore whilst the full term 'eGMS.date.declared' would be valid, the term 'DC.date.declared' would not be. Or at least that is my opinion!

I would be grateful for confirmation or otherwise of this view.

Many Thanks

Andy


Response given on: 2005-06-02 11:08:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Andy:e-GMS is essentially an Application Profile, developed for a particular purpose at a time when the concept was a new one. In general, as we've gotten more comfortable with APs, it's become clearer where it's "safe" to use extensions and where it creates problems.In the example you give, where the e-GMS element "date" maps explicitly in the e-GMS specification to the DC element "date" you're very safe in calling those equivalent properties. And again, when refinements like "Available" are defined the same and equivalence assertions are made, you're on safe territory.You're absolutely correct in stating that where there is no equivalence asserted, there is no way to "extend" that term into DC. Thanks for your question!Regards,Diane

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2005-05-09 00:00:00 by Deirdre

If I have a webpage listing, for example, the publications available for the Planning Sections of a County Council website, what is the best way to make the list of documents searchable. (Site will be using Verity Ultraseek). In this example, on the current webpage there are 2 PDF documents to download. Meath County Development Plan, and Navan Town Development Plan. Should I use:
<meta name="DC.Subject" content="List of Meath County Council Planning Publications" />
<meta name="DC.Subject" content="Meath County Development Plan" />
<meta name="DC.Subject" content="Navan Town Development Plan" />

Thanks in advance,

Deirdre




Response given on: 2005-06-02 11:14:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Deirdre:I'm not sure you've provided enough information to allow a recommendation on the "best" way to make a list of documents searchable, but I think it's possible to make a few helpful suggestions.In the examples you cite, "Meath County Development Plan" and "Navan Town Development Plan" you have Titles, not Subjects. Ergo, the Meta Tags should reflect that. If you want subjects as well, that'd be great, but you'd need to either extract some keywords from the documents or use a controlled vocabulary and search for appropriate terms. In the case of the titles you cite, you might also consider the DC element "Coverage" which allows you to specify the spatial extent "covered" by the document--in this case a town and a county.I hope this helps.Regards,Diane

Question asked on: 2005-05-04 00:00:00 by Rich

Hi.

We were planning on including the following line in all pages:

<meta name="DC.creator" content="Ordnance Survey, the national mapping
agency of Great Britain, xxx@ordnancesurvey.co.uk" />

However, we're getting more & more spam to email addresses available on our websites. To mitigate against this, we're planning on obfuscating (essentially hiding) the actual addresses available in MAILTOs using the principles detailed at http://www.mways.co.uk/prog/hidemail.php

Bearing this in mind, I was wondering what your thoughts would be on including email addresses in DC.creator content? Presumably it doesn't really make sense to obfuscate any email address included in this? An alternative could be to include a URL to a contacts page (which included an obfuscated MAILTO) rather than an email address itself? The documentation I have though (UK e-Government Metadata Standard v3.0) suggests including an email address in DC.creator.

Look forward to hearing your thoughts.

Best wishes,

Rich.

Response given on: 2005-05-04 09:19:00 by Stuart Sutton

Rich, in all honesty, to include the email address of the creator of the resource in the metadata for the resource being described violates the Dublin Core "one-to-one" principle. You can read more about this principle in the DCMI Abstract Model, section 3 (http://dublincore.org/documents/abstract-model/
index.shtml/#sect-3 ) where it states: "The abstract model described above indicates that each DCMI metadata description describes one, and only one, resource. This is commonly referred to as the one-to-one principle." In terms of the creator's email address, we can say that the resource (say it is a document ) being described "has-a" creator and the creator "has-a" email address. So, while we can say that the creator is a property of the resource being described, we _cannot_ say that the email address is a property of the resource--i.e., the document does not have an email address, the creator does! So, "email" is not an apropriate refinement of the property creator in the description of a document under the DCMI Abstract Model. In the end, what this means is that to make the metadata statement that "Jane has the email address jane@here.org";, you need a metadata description of Jane (since the email address is a property of Jane ) that you reference from the creator property in your description of the document.

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor

Question asked on: 2005-05-01 00:00:00 by Philip

Where a resource exists in translation as well as the original language, is the translation a version of the original (isVersionOf) or a related resource? Or is the original a "source" in that the translation is derived from it?

Response given on: 2005-06-02 10:51:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Philip:If you are using Qualified DC, it is probably best to use IsVersionOf and HasVersion to relate the translation to the original and vice versa. Where this strategy has implications is when the information is "dumbed down" into Simple DC and the relationship information is lost. It is this issue that prompts some to use Source instead, but, of course, there is no reciprocality available. That said, in general we recommend the use of the Relation refinements rather than Source.Regards,Diane

Question asked on: 2005-03-30 00:00:00 by Jan Egil

http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/#coverage refers to TGN. How then, do I spcifiy that dc:coverage is the "New York" of http://www.getty.edu/vow/TGNFullDisplay?find=New+York&place=&nation=United+States&prev_page=1&english=Y&subjectid=2539851 ?

That URL is a bit too ugly to serve as red:resource? But if I use the name "New York", you will not know that I refer to a place in Georgia, USA?

Response given on: 2005-06-02 10:41:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Jan:In fact, unless you use Qualified Dublin Core and a schema that allows for the representation of controlled vocabularies, you can't specify controlled vocabularies at all. And, in the case you cite, that wouldn't help much, as you know.The ability that you (and we ) yearn for requires that the vocabulary owner identify terms with a URI. As you note, URLs (particularly ugly ones from database queries ) are not optimized for this task. This is a big gap in the Web infrastructure, one that will require basic agreements and on techology and strategy by vocabulary maintainers. There are steps being taken to get that process going, but it will take a while.In the meantime, may I suggest "New York, GA, USA" as a compromise for the interim?Regards,Diane

Question asked on: 2005-03-29 00:00:00 by Claudia

We're trying to determine the most appropriate way of handling the title, creator, subject & description field, etc. for chapters from a book, which is to be digitized in full and organized by chapter. One approach would appear to be to use the source field for the book as a whole, and the title, creator, description, etc. for each individual chapter, with Relation[IsPartof] referring to the source. If so, should all the details on creator, description of the book be in the source field?

Another approach is to use multiple elements for both the book and the chapter (e.g. title, creator, description, etc. for both). This might seem to be somewhat confusing and overly redundant, but separate individuals on our team have come up with differnt perspectives on which best facilitates discovery. I've had little luck finding examples of how others have handled this; links to other collections would be greatly appreciated!

Many thanks, cp

Response given on: 2005-04-10 22:35:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Claudia:

It sounds like the chapters of this book have separate descriptive elements, and I think you're correct that it makes the most sense to consider this as a "part/whole" problem.

You might consider having a record for the book as a whole, using the "hasPart" refinement for the Relation element to link to each chapter. The chapters could then be described separately with an isPartOf reciprocal link to the original book. I'd suggest that you not use Source for this strategy, since it does not have the advantage of the reciprocality of the Relation refinements.

What you include in the links depends on what you need to rationalize the information for your system and your users. A URI link would be optimal in some ways, but might not work well in your system.

Good luck with your project.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian

By whom was it established?

Response given on: 2005-03-01 11:13:00 by Makx Dekkers

The work on the Dublin Core started with an invitational workshop, exactly 10 years ago today on March 1-3, 1995, at OCLC in Dublin, Ohio, USA (see: http://dublincore.org/workshops/dc1/). The location of that meeting was what gave the Initiative its name.

Question asked on: 2005-02-25 00:00:00 by Eleanor

I am looking at creating metadata for the webpages, manuscript records and images of a library Special Collections department. To comply with MINERVA technical standards, 'to support the discovery of their resources by a wide range of other applications and services, projects must capture and store sufficient descriptive metadata for each item using simple DC'. If search engines do not use this data, and there is no central index or catalogue to submit the DC records to, what will be the benefit of having them? How will these other resources and applications find us? Thank you for your help.

Response given on: 2005-02-26 05:03:00 by Andy Powell

I would tend to look at this requirement in a couple of ways. In the short term, capturing metadata at the level of simple DC may help with the management of your collection of resources. You may also be able to re-use it internally within your service as the basis of your internal search engine. Remember that (I assume ) you may choose to store your metadata internally using some other format (e.g. MARC ) as long as you know how to transform it into DC as and when it is necvessary to do so. You might also choose to expose your metadata using the OAI Protocol for Metadata Harvesting as simple DC (oai_dc ), so that services like ARC and OAIster can harvest it. The latter will result in your metadata becoming available thru Yahoo.Finally, I'd consider any simple DC metadata you create now in the context of your longer term 'preservation' metadata requirements. Clearly, simple DC won't provide everything you need here - but it will be a start.

Question asked on: 2005-02-22 00:00:00 by Jussi

Cheers!

Working for a project(website), where the documents (html-pages) contain 0-n attachments (propably no more than 10). The html-pages have their own metadata set, while the attachments have their own metadata sets. The sets are more or less similar in structure.

Should I add the keywords (and other metadata) from the attachments to the actual metadata of the html-document, or should I use some kind of subset (this document contains these documents, here is their metadata)? Any other approaches?

thank you in advance,
Jussi

Response given on: 2005-02-24 09:59:00 by Holly Mercer

Hello Jussi,Qualified DC offers two element refinements of dc:relation that you can use: dcterms:isPartOf and dcterms:hasPart. These two element refinements express "parent/child" relationships, where as in your example the HTML document is the "parent" and the attachments are the "children." This approach follows your second idea, "this document contains these documents, here is their metadata."Seehttp://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/andhttp://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/qualifiers.shtml#isPartOffor further explanation.Regards,Holly

Question asked on: 2005-02-22 00:00:00 by Phil

What is the DCMI position on translating the DC elements, terms and refinements into other languages? Do we translate the term presented to the user at the interface or have translations for the terms been devloped for FRENCH users?

Response given on: 2005-02-23 07:00:00 by Makx Dekkers

DCMI is very much supportive of translations of DCMI documentation into other languages. In fact, http://dublincore.org/resources/translations/ lists many translations and the DCMI Registry http://dublincore.org/dcregistry/contains the definitions of DCMI terms insome 25 languages.
However, you need to note that there is one thing in the definition of terms that mustnot be translated, namely what is called the 'Term Name' in http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/. This is also the name that is part of the URI of the term. The Label, Definition and Comment can be translated and this is usually what you would present to users in the interface.

Question asked on: 2005-02-03 00:00:00 by Ian

I need to be more specific in my metadata than merely "subject." No refinements exist for this element. Can I, without breaching the spirit of DCMI, introduce my own refinements, such as, e.g.:
subject.relative
subject.pupil
subject.publisher
subject.artist
subject.composer
subject.writer

and so on?

Ideally, there would be two levels of subject refinement, the first level having:

subject.person
subject.institution
subject.place

and the second level further subdividing, e.g.:

subject.person.relative
subject.person.pupil
subject.person.artist

and so on.

-- Ian Bent

Response given on: 2005-02-14 16:57:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Ian:The reason that no refinements exist for this element is that it is intended for use with controlled vocabularies, which are far more interoperable in general than refinements. The refinement scheme that you propose might work perfectly well within your own domain, but is unlikely to be suitable for (or easily explained to ) anyone else using your data.So, to answer your specific question: you would be breaching both the spirit and the practice of DCMI by introducing the refinements you suggest. It would be much better from an interoperability standpoint to set up local vocabularies to meet your goals for specificity. Better yet would be exposing them to others for re-use, but that may be a future enhancement.Regards,Diane I. HillmannEditor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2005-02-02 00:00:00 by martha

I'm a graduate student in Tennessee doing a presentation on DCMI.

I'm wondering (1)how many URLs are using DCMI at this time in your estimation?

and (2) where will DCMI be in five years?

Response given on: 2005-02-14 17:10:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Question asked on: 2005-01-21 00:00:00 by Jan Egil

Does the URI http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_hastings identify a battle, or a wikipedia article?

Could that be resolved by identifying the battle with URN:http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_hastings
and the article with URL:http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_hastings
?

Response given on: 2005-02-23 07:18:00 by Makx Dekkers


For me there is no doubt that the URI you specify refers to the Wikipedia article. In an early article http://www.w3.org/DesignIssues/Axioms.html, Tim Berners-Lee states that an URI refers to something "on the Web". I know there are some discussions in various place on whether and howtoassign URIs to physical entities (such as people) but I don't think there is consensus on how one would assign a URI to an event like the Battle of Hastings.

Question asked on: 2005-01-20 00:00:00 by Catherine

Hi,
We do electronic edition of older paper journals articles,and we want to describe them with DC elements and refinements in XML. So, what is better to use:
- first, to link the article electronic version to the paper version: 'source' or 'isFormatOf';for now, we don't make changes between the 2 versions. Is there a formal denomination to write the reference of the paper article in the element/refinement(ie: journal name,date,vol, number, page, article title, or so)?
- then, to link the electronic resource to its electronic collection, what value must be used in isPartOf refinement: url of the electronic issue table of content(I saw it somewhere), url of the journal welcome page, issn, journal title (short and long), other...
- then, I think that all date refinements are for described electronic resource, and not for older paper resource; is it right, or is there a refinement for the paper resource?
Apologize for my english speaking, thank you very much for your answer, sincerely

Response given on: 2005-01-27 09:34:00 by Ann Apps


1. I think it would be better to use dcterms:isFormatOf to link the electronic version of the article to the paper version, if you have a URI for that information. Using dc:source is another option and would not be wrong, but isFormatOf seems better to me. However if you do not have a URI, which is probably the case for a printed version, you should use dc:source because the value of dc:source can be a text string.

2. You can include the bibliographic citation details for the article as the value of a dcterms:bibliographicCitation property. This is a refinement of dc:identifier. The citation details effectively identify a journal article. The DCMI Citation Working Group are writing some Guidelines for encoding this information within a Dublin Core description. These are available at http://epub.mimas.ac.uk/DC/dc-citation-guidelines/ but note that this is still a working draft. However this document does not yet explain how to encode this information in XML, because the guidelines for encoding DC metadata in XML need updating and are not yet agreed. So if you want to use an XML encoding of citation information then you may wish to use a different metadata scheme that has been designed to describe bibliographic items (some are indicated in the guidelines document). Of course if your XML encoding is just within your own application you can choose yourself how to do it.

3. The citation guidelines recommend including the ISSN of a journal as the value of a dcterms:isPartOf property for an article. This should be encoded as a URI (ie. urn:issn:), as detailed in the guidelines. However, isPartOf can be repeated, so you could also include an isPartOf property that is the URL of the issue table of contents, or of the journal. Which you choose will depend on your application, and where you want a user to link to to find the journal issue.

4. The dcterms:issued property is usually used for the issue date for a journal issue. For a journal that is also published in print, this is the date shown on the cover. Alternatively, you could include this issue date with the 'date' key in an OpenURL ContextObject if you follow the citation guidelines (explained in there). You would use another date refinement for when the electronic article itself was made available - either dcterms:available or dc:date.

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2005-01-18 00:00:00 by James

I have to produce a website, and one of the requirements is to describe all my multimedia, which will be sounds(.wav or .mp3), pictures (.jpg or.bmp) and movies (.avi. mpg) what information should I describe about these resources? Or where could I find any information on how to describes bits of multimedia? Thank you.

Response given on: 2005-03-28 15:56:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

James: Apologies for the delay in answering your question. This information was provided by Erica Olsen of the Laboratory of Ornithology at Cornell University:
*********************

Here are some examples of what is currently being done on the web in these areas... The NISO Technical metadata for still images standard describes just about everything you could ever want to say about a still image. This is definitely overkill, but it's a great start. Their data dictionary can be found here: http://www.niso.org/standards/resources/Z39_87_trial_use.pdf

Alternately, the Getty image collection has a good example of how information about still images can be presented on the web in a user-friendly fashion: http://creative.gettyimages.com/source/home/homeCreative.aspx

For sounds and video, there are fewer formal digital metadata standards. The University of Virginia has published their guidelines here: http://www.lib.virginia.edu/digital/reports/DLMRPGroupReport.htm
The Defense Technical Information Center has published Video and Audio metadata guidelines for their collections: http://dvl.dtic.mil/metadata_guidelines/dvl_video_guidelines19Jun01.pdf http://dvl.dtic.mil/metadata_guidelines/dvl_sound_guidelines19Jun01.pdf

The Vincent Voice Library at Michigan State University has a gallery of sound files from it's collection. You can look through and see how they chose to display information about audio recordings on the web: http://www.historicalvoices.org/earliest_voices/

Finally, here at the Lab, we have a beta version of the Macaulay Library sound and video marine catalog that is (rather quietly ) available online: http://mlsource.ornith.cornell.edu/dam/ The search results and item detail pages can give you an idea of which metadata items we thought were important to display for audio recordings.

***************************
Regards, and good luck on your project. Diane I. Hillmann Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-01-12 00:00:00 by Rachel

Greetings.

I need to include the following meta data one each web page in a web site: expiration date, published date, and modified date.

What do you recommend using for expiration and modified dates? I planned to use the following three tags, but I was told that this is not the international standard.

date.expires
date.published
date.modified

Thank you!
Rachel

Response given on: 2005-02-15 11:01:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Rachel:While it's true that Simple Dublin Core supports only a single undifferentiated date, there are additional date refinements available for use with Qualified Dublin Core. If you check the complete Terms documentation (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/#H3 ) you will see the following refinements for date:availablecreatedissuedmodifiedvaliddateAccepteddateCopyrighteddateSubmittedWe'd suggest that you check the definitions for these refinements and choose the one(s ) that meet your needs, rather than go forward with a local solution. This will ensure that others can more easily use your data as you express it.Regards,Diane I. HillmannEditor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2005-01-07 00:00:00 by Frank

we are implementing dcmi elements in our dtd that is for educational content. but we are desparately missing a 'version' element in dcmi. is this a general missunderstanding? why is it missing? thanks for an answer in advance. frank marent

Response given on: 2005-01-11 09:44:00 by Thomas Baker

Actually, there is a term "hasVersion", with the definition:"The described resource has a version, edition, or adaptation, namely, the referenced resource". It is a refinement ofdc:relation and therefore intended to express the relationship between two resources, one of which is "a version of" theother.However, you are perhaps looking for an element to identify "the version of the resource described" -- for example, a versionnumber like "1.0" or "1.1" (i.e., a "Version Identifier" ).The need to identify version is clear in a general sense,but it is not immediately obvious how one would expressversion in a generic sense, and therefore how the element should be defined. Even assuming a generic concept could be defined, it is not immediately obvious how a separate elementwould serve the cause of resource discovery more effectivelythan simply including the version number in the title of theresource (which is often appropriate ) or in its free-text description.According to DCMI process, such issues would need to be addressed and documented in a formal proposal for a new term to the DCMI Usage Board. However, pastexperience withquestions related to identifiers suggests that the path to auseful consensus on this issue could be longer than one mightinitially assume.

Hello! There was a standard set of genre types for use in the Dublin Core Type element created by Resource Type and Format Working Group. Response given on: 2004-12-10 14:58:00 by Holly Mercer

Hello.

The controlled vocabulary you mention was a recommendation of the DC Type working group, but was not approved by the Usage Board. You may define your own genre types, but you may also use one of the registered Dublin Core controlled vocabularies or encoding schemes.

Question asked on: 2004-12-06 00:00:00 by Alexander

Hello,

We are interested in cataloging highly self-referential canonical works. We would like to capture such common relationships as:

Relation.TranslationOf
Relation.CommentaryOn

Further, there are some commonly accepted (but not definitive) authorative works often used as reference. When someone makes a translation of an ancient text they often refer to it by its similarity to a work done in London 150 years ago. They might refer to something more popular found on the net:

Relation.SimilarTo
Relation.IndexReference

Don't seem to cut it. Have we overlooked definitions found elsewhere in DC, is there a standard way to extend DC? If not, I would like to suggest these added to the core DC definition, particularly TranslationOf, as it seems a very common relationship.

Thank you,
Alex Genaud

Response given on: 2004-12-13 12:44:00 by Pete Johnston

Alex,You can extend the Dublin Core metadata vocabularies by defining your own properties as sub-properties (refinements ) of DC properties i.e. in the same way that DCMI declares its http://purl.org/dc/terms/isPartOf property to be a sub-property of http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/relation, so you can declare (for example ) http://your.example.org/terms/isTranslationOf to be a sub-property of http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/relation.This does depend on (a ) your assigning a persistent URI to your property;(b ) your making available the information about this relationship between your new property and the DCMI property - either in a human-readable document or using an RDF Schema description - so that applications processing your metadata instances can take advantage of that information;(c ) your observing the semantics of the sub-property relationship i.e. subproperty means that "If property A is a sub-property of property B, then whenever two resources are related by property A, they are also related by property B" - and this applies for all occurrences of the property A.Metadata implementers can make proposals to the DCMI Usage Board for new properties to be added to the DCMI vocabularies, so you could make a proposal for, e.g, an "isTranslationOf" property - though it may be more likely for your proposal to gain Usage Board support if you can show the support of a community of implementers. Details of the process for proposing new terms are available athttp://www.dublincore.org/usage/documents/process/#four.Incidentally, the current DCMI recommendations for encoding DC metadata in XHTML, XML and RDF prefer the use of the refinement name "stand alone" with the information about the refinement relationship being obtained from a source external to the metadata instance, rather than the "[name of element].[name of element-refinement]" convention. Regards Pete Johnston

Response given on: 2004-12-13 11:07:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Susan,

Dublin Core metadata can easily be used for the descriptive portion of a data warehouse or metadata repository applications. In fact, the project I workfor (the National Science Digital Library in the U.S.) has used it for just this since 2000.

The DCMI Bibliography (http://www.dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/bibliography.shtml) and the Projects pages (http://www.dublincore.org/projects/) are good places to look for practical insights on how others have used Dublin Core in both routine and creative ways.

Question asked on: 2004-10-06 00:00:00 by Jeff

How do I generate URNs for the XML/RDF format and are they required?
E.G.
<dct:Collection about="urn:x-cld:D0E55784-5A29-4A8D-B71B-BF3593716C73">

Response given on: 2004-12-13 14:35:00 by Pete Johnston

Jeff,

The example you quote uses an experimental URN namespace. See section 3.1 of RFC 3406 Uniform Resource Names (URN) Namespace Definition Mechanisms http://www.faqs.org/rfcs/rfc3406.html

I'm guessing that you obtained it from the RSLP Collection Description tool http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/rslp/tool/ which I think uses some randomisation algorithm to generate those strings.If you are using URNs in a live system, I think the recommendation would be to use only registered URN Namespace Idshttp://www.iana.org/assignments/urn-namespaces
if you wish to be sure of guaranteeing the uniqueness of your identifiers.RDF does not require the use of URNs; you could assign identifiers based on any URI scheme. Again the use of a registered URI scheme would be recommended.

Regards

Pete Johnston

Response given on: 2004-10-07 11:23:00 by Blythe Bennett

Digital reference has already had a tremendous impact on library practice across library types and geography. Anecdotal evidence of impact can be seen in the growing number of digital reference publications, conference presentations, and large-scale digital reference projects such as the Virtual Reference Desk project, QuestionPoint and the State of Washington LSTA efforts. Digital reference is no longer a limited experiment in some libraries, but increasingly a new reality in many reference service environments. However, this new reality has outpaced the library training and education infrastructure to support it.Digital reference has already had a tremendous impact on library practice across library types and geography. Response given on: 2004-10-22 15:46:00 by Pete Johnston

Carlo,Your XML document does not appear to be well-formed (no closing ">" on your metadata tag; incorrect closing tag for dc:date; and an unnecessary closing tag.If you corrected those errors, then, yes, I think it would be in line with the syntax recommended in [1]I'd add that "PDF Document" is probably not an appropriate value for dc:type, however, as dc:type describes "The nature or genre of the content of the resource".RegardsPete Johnston[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/

Question asked on: 2004-09-21 00:00:00 by Stanton

What's the difference between DC.date DCTERMS.issued? I'm afraid I don't see the point of these DCTERMS things, and thought the old system of DC.something.modifier was much clearer.

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:32:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

DCTERMS.issued has narrower semantics than DC.date. Its usage allows consumers of the metadata to know what kind of date is being encoded - rather than it just being 'any old' date.

I agree that in this form of encoding, the name of the element refinement is less intuitive to human readers than it might be - DCTERMS.dateIssued would have been better. All new DC element refinements are being given more intuitive names.

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:10:00 by Andy Powell

No, this would be against the spirit of the DC abstract model and gramatical principles. Encoding schemes should be used as encoding schemes, not as elements/properties.

Question asked on: 2004-09-16 00:00:00 by Eric

I'm curious if there is a study that looks at DC metadata user requirements. For example, how do those that access a DC metadata record use the various elements? Response given on: 2005-01-25 13:17:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Eric:Sorry for the delay in answering your question. I don't think that there's anything specifically that looks at user needs in quite the way you're looking for information. However, I'd suggest two places to start:One is some thesis research done by Jewel Ward:Link:http://www.foar.net/research/index.htmlAbstract:Jewel Ward. A Quantitative Analysis of Dublin Core Metadata Element Set(DCMES ) Usage in Data Providers Registered with the Open ArchivesInitiative (OAI ). A Master's paper for the M.S. inI.S. degree. November, 2002. 68 pages. Advisor: Gregory B. NewbyThis research describes an empirical study of how the Dublin Core MetadataElement Set (DCMES ) is used by 100 Data Providers (DPs ) registered withthe Open Archives Initiative (OAI ). The research was conducted todetermine whether or not the DCMES is used to its full capabilities. Eighty-two of 100 DPs have metadata records available for analysis. DCMES usage varies by type of DP. The average number of Dublin Core elements per record is eight, with an average of 91,785 Dublin Core elements used per DP. Five of the 15 elements of the DCMES are used 71% of the time. The results show the DCMES is not used to its fullest extent within DPs registered with OAI.Another is some research being done by Liz Liddy at Syracuse University. A short description of the project is at: http://www.dlib.org/dlib/july03/07inbrief.html#LIDDY. Regards,Diane I. HillmannEditor, "Using Dublin Core"

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:20:00 by Andy Powell

UKOLN, University of Bath

This value is intentionally missing from the DCMI Type vocabulary because that vocabulary is limited to the classes of things that DC is intended to be used to describe. So, for the time being you'll have to invent your own vocabulary for this - or find another existing vocabulary that has this term. You could ask for further help on the dc-agents working group mailing list.

Question asked on: 2004-09-05 00:00:00 by Jan Egil

W3C (http://www.w3.org/TR/webarch/#pr-uri-collision) discourages URL overloading.

Does that mean I should not use http://landsbank.fo/ to identify both my organization, and

its web site? After all, the organization was founded in 1988, the web page several years

later. And while while I am resonsible for the web page

(http://www.nic.fo/custom2/lookup/domain.asp?searchtext=LANDSBANK.FO&go=Leita), my boss is

responsible for the entire organization

(http://www.gulu.fo/Virki.vit?VirkiId=382809&language=2).

Then again, at http://www.w3.org/TR/webarch/#indirect-identification W3C seems to encourage indirect identification, thus allowing the same URI to identify both web site and organization.

WHen using Dublin Core - do you think I should overload http://landsbank.fo/ as an
indirect identification of the organization?

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:05:00 by Andy Powell

Hi Jan,I'm afraid I couldn't read the text of Andy's earlier reply because the AskDCMI system seemed to have done something strange with the character encoding, but I'm guessing Andy's advice was that if you wish to describe both an organisation and its web page or web site then you need to assign different URIs for those two things. Re your follow up, yes, it seems to me it's a good idea to avoid embedding in your resource identifiers information that is specific to your server-side implementation (".asp", ".jsp" etc ). Also I think it is important to distinguish between identification and "interaction". If I assign the URI http://example.org/myOrganisation to my organisation, it may well be that I configure my HTTP server, so that when someone issues an HTTP GET on that URI, they receive an HTML document in response. That is fine: that HTML document is a representation of the resource (my organisation ). However, if I wish to describe that HTML document separately, then I need a separate URI to refer to the document e.g. http://example.org/myOrganisation/intro.html. Now if someone issues a GET on that URI, they may retrieve exactly the same document i.e. the HTML document is its own representation (as well as being a representation of my organisation ). So in the case of the XHTML namespace URI, by definition (or through its use as an XML Namespace name ) that URI identifies/denotes an XML namespace. It's fine for W3C to return an HTML document when someone dereferences the XML namespace name - but if they want to make statements about both the XML namespace and that HTML document, then (as you suggest ) they need separate URIs for those two things.Re the W3C indirect identification section in the W3C TAG document, I don't think they are advocating using the same URI to denote two different things. I think they are careful to distinguish between global and local scope: e.g. locally, it may be OK to use mailto:nadia@example.com as an indirect identifier for a person, but in the global context it is quite possible that another party is using that URI to refer to a mailbox (which is what the mailto: URI scheme says it identifies ). So when exposing your local data to the rest of the world you would need to use conventions like "the person with the mailbox mailto:nadia@example.com". So to go back to your question, I think it is problematic to use the same URI to identify both an organisation and a website, and it is easier/safer to use two distinct URIs. It may well be the case that both those URIs dereference to the same representation, but as identifiers they denote two different resources, and you can clearly and unambiguously make statements about those two different resources.RegardsPete Johnston

Tags: Name:Encoding Schemes; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff


Question asked on: 2004-08-24 00:00:00 by Ricki-Ellen Brooke

Is there a standard -- eg. either comma or semi-colon deliminated -- for separating multiple subject terms in the 'subject' element? I've seen the DC note: "If multiple vocabulary terms or keywords are used, either separate terms with semi-colons or use separate iterations of the Subject element". But w3.org states: "Provide keywords and descriptions: Some indexing engines look for META elements that define a comma-separated list of keywords/phrases... Consider these examples,
<META name="keywords" content="vacation,Greece,sunshine">

Is there a standard or most-used form? Or maybe it doesn't matter if one uses commas or semi-colons to separate multiple subject terms?

Thanks for any guidance.

Response given on: 2004-09-14 16:51:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Ricki-Ellen:

"Using Dublin Core" recommends using a semi-colon to separate multiple subject terms in the same element instance. Because DC elements are repeatable, using separate element instances is even less ambiguous.

Commas often aren't a good idea where subject terms might use a comma within a term. LCSH subjects, for instance, often us commas to move the most important term to the beginning of the phrase. This makes a comma in such a list highly ambiguous--one cannot tell what its function actually is in that situation.

I suspect that the W3C folks are most likely to think of subjects as merely keywords, not subject terms that may be part of a complex controlled vocabulary.

So, yes, it matters--how much depends on what your goals are and with whom you're sharing data.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Syntaxes; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2004-08-20 00:00:00 by Kurt

The description of dcterms:available says "Date (often a range)..." I have been unable to find out just HOW to represent a range correctly. I know a single date would be "2004-08-19" but what about a range??? Thanks.

Response given on: 2004-08-26 15:30:00 by Holly Mercer

Holly Mercer
University of Kansas Coordinator of Digital Content Development

Kurt,
The date recorded as 2004-08-19 follows the recommended encoding scheme for dates, namely W3CDTF, an application of ISO8601. You are correct that W3CDTF describes single dates only; it does not specify how to encode a date range or period of time. If you wish to follow an encoding scheme to express date available, you can use the Dublin Core Period encoding scheme http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-period/ a DCMI recommendation. Other options include: ISO 8601 (range expressed as YYYY-MM-DD/YYYY-MM-DD ), AACR2R or other cataloging rules for expressing date ranges, or you may opt not to use an encoding scheme.

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2004-08-04 00:00:00 by Will

I am generating Dublic Core meta-data for a customer as part of an Open Archive Initiatives static repository. The customer has asked me to put HTML formatting elements (such as paragraph, subscript, and superscript) and HTML character entities inside elements of the Dublin Core XML representation. They are suggesting that I do so by wrapping the HTML in a CDATA section. While this will clear validate against the schema, it does not seem to be in the spirit of Dublin Core. Is such an approach acceptable use of Dublin Core, or should only plain text be used?

Many thanks,

Will.

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:09:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

I agree that this is against the spirit of simple DC. Use plain text in the element values. If you want to do richer stuff, then declare a separate metadata format (i.e. develop a different XML schema ) and then use that.

Note, DC tends to refer to values that contain markup as 'rich values'. These are (conceptually ) acceptable in qualified DC - but I'm not aware of any off-the-shelf XML schemas that support them.

Question asked on: 2004-08-02 00:00:00 by Age Bosma

In the DC documentation there's talk about different XML structures that can be used to include DC metadata.
Suppose I want to store Created Date in W3C-DTF format. This can be done by the following format (correct me if I'm wrong):

<dc:date>
<dcterms:created>
<dcterms:W3CDTF>
<rdf:value>1999-09-25T14:20+10:00</rdf:value>
</dcterms:W3CDTF>
</dcterms:created>
</dc:date>

But what if I want a structure based on the following:

<dc:date xsi:type="dcterms:W3CDTF">1999-09-25T14:20+10:00</dcterms:W3CDTF>

How should I include the "dcterms:created"? Is there a recommended way of achieving this?

Response given on: 2004-08-12 15:03:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

RDF/XML (see [1] ) permits various forms for expressing this but the following could be used:


http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#" xmlns:dcterms="http://purl.org/dc/terms/">



1999-09-25T14:20+10:00



(N.B. the dcterms:created XML element is _not_ nested inside a dc:date XML element )


Following the Guidelines for DC in XML [2], the following could be used:


http://example.org/myapp/"
xmlns:xsi="http://www.w3.org/2001/XMLSchema-instance"
xsi:schemaLocation="http://example.org/myapp/ http://example.org/myapp/schema.xsd"
xmlns:dcterms="http://purl.org/dc/terms/">

1999-09-25T14:20+10:00


Regards

Pete Johnston


[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-rdf-xml/
[2] http:/dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/

Response given on: 2004-08-05 09:27:00 by Holly Mercer

Holly Mercer
University of Kansas Coordinator of Digital Content Development

I refer to the Dublin Core user guide http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/ for guidelines on when to use the resource type Event: An event is a non-persistent, time-based occurrence. Metadata for an event provides descriptive information that is the basis for discovery of the purpose, location, duration, responsible agents, and links to related events and resources. A resource of type Sound is content primarily intended to be rendered as audio. Therefore, a transcript from a round table discussion could be
Type="Event" and Type="Text" (with any other vocabulary you wish to use ).
The recorded audio of that same round table discussion could be both
Type=Event" and Type="Sound."
The Type element need not specify whether the resource is available online. If you are unclear about the definitions of the Euler Type vocabulary, I suggest you contact the agency directly.

for the full list.

Question asked on: 2004-07-14 00:00:00 by alessandra bianchi

For book material, where in the DC to put the info about the number/qualification of edition? (2nd, new, ...)

Response given on: 2004-07-19 11:21:00 by Holly Mercer

Holly Mercer
University of Kansas Lawrence, KansasCoordinator of Digital Content Development

There are several ways to include edition information in a Dublin Core record, but no single authoritative approach.

1. Use the isVersionOf / hasVersion refinements of the Relation element. From SUsing Dublin Core http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/:
Most of the refinements of Relation are expressed as "reciprocals" and may be used to link resources in two directions, though this is not required. Implementors need not describe both or all resources involved in a reciprocal relationship to express the relationship--in other words, they may describe a later version and relate it to the earlier without having the need or opportunity to describe the earlier, and vice versa. In some of the relationships below, maintaining reciprocality is more important. In others, one direction of the relationship is more relevant than the other. These differences will be mentioned in the guidelines for specific terms.
In All cases, either a string or a URI may be used as a value. If a URI is used, the scheme should be designated. You may find the example given in SUsing Dublin Core helpful.

2. You can use the bibliographicCitation element refinement of Identifier and incorporate a bibliographic citation (including an edition statement ) for your resource. See http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/qualifiers.shtml#bibliographicCitation.

3. If you wish to note the edition of the print version from which an electronic resource was derived, you can use Source. Recommended best practice is to reference the resource by means of a string or number conforming to a formal identification system, but you can include a citation or other string, as is done in this example taken from the User Guide: Source="Image from page 54 of the 1922 edition of Romeo and Juliet"

4. The Dublin Core Libraries Work Group has also dealt with this. The DC-Lib application profile (current version http://dublincore.org/documents/2002/09/24/library-application-profile/ ) recommends using Edition from the MODS namespace <http://www.loc.gov/mods >. The DCMI Usage Board supports using terms (such as encoding schemes ) from existing namespaces instead of creating new terms to extend Dublin Core.

The method you should to employ depends on how you will use the Dublin Core records. The Dublin Core Libraries application profile (DC-Lib ) will provide a rich description of resources, but that richness may be lost if you wish to share your metadata records through OAI metadata harvesters.

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian

Response given on: 2004-09-22 18:00:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

When encoding DC in XML you have to declare the container element yourself - because it is application specific. Therefore, you are free to declare the container element in such a way that it makes use of an ID attribute.

Response given on: 2004-07-27 09:57:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

David:

I'm not entirely sure what you mean by the "old form." "Editor" was never an approved DCMI refinement, but was certainly used locally by some implementations.

The Dublin Core Usage Board is currently in the process of defining a role vocabulary for use with Contributor, based on LC's relator terms. Progress has been slower than we'd like, primarily because there are administrative and technical issues involved. When the vocabulary is available, you will certainly see an announcement on the DCMI webspace.

Question asked on: 2004-06-21 00:00:00 by Gabrielle

What are the implementation strategies related to maintenance?

Response given on: 2004-07-27 10:02:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Gabrielle:

To a great extent, maintenance strategies are tied to technical choices made by a project, rather than metadata. Another factor is resources--how much maintenance can you afford? What constraints do you have based on the level of technical support you can muster?

I don't know of an overall maintenance strategy that works for everyone, but WHAT you do and HOW you do it should be a part of your general metadata and technical planning effort.

into XML spy it tells me that

Any ideas?

Response given on: 2004-06-17 13:24:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

I haven't seen the error you describe and do not have access to XML Spy.

We have found that some validating parsers do throw errors when processing the complexTypes in dcterms.xsd based on the SimpleLiteral type. However, based on comments on the XMLSchema-dev list and our testing with some of the major parsers such as XSV and MSXML, we felt that this usage was correct. See Appendix A of the Notes at

http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/qdc/2003/04/
02/notes/

for more details.

The major rationale for defining the SimpleLiteral type as we did was for maximum flexibility. We expected implementors to be able both to extend and restrict this type to meet their own needs.

If you knew you would never need/want to extend the SimpleLiteral to allow child elements, you could simplify the schemas by taking a copy and changing the SimpleLiteral type in dc.xsd to a simpleContent model something like:









Regards
Pete Johnston

Question asked on: 2004-06-16 00:00:00 by Nadine

We are looking to implement a standard for our XHTML document metadata.

Is a mixture of HTML metadata tags, for example, <meta name="description" content="" /> and the new DC standard, for example, <meta name="DC.description" content="" /> sensible?

Will search engines such as Google pick up DC metadata or should we still be using the old style of HTML metadata?

If DC elements are used, is it essential to link to a scheme? (<link rel="schema.DC" href="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/" />)

If we use DCTERMS elements would we have to link to the DCTERMS scheme? (<link rel="schema.DCTERMS" href="http://purl.org/dc/terms/" />)

Can you also refer to a document that explains some of the practical advantages of the DC standard and our website would benefit?


Response given on: 2004-09-22 17:53:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

No, Google and most of the other big search engines will not make use of embedded DC metadata.

Linking to the schema is recommended but not really essential - but if you do decide to link to the schema and you use the DCTERMS namespace, then yes you do need to link to that schema as well.

I'm not aware of a document that discusses the benefits of using DC metadata as such. In general, I would recommend only embedding DC metadata where you control both the content and the search engine - for example, it might be sensible to embed DC metadata into your own pages if your internal/site search engine has been configured to make use of the embedded metadata.

Question asked on: 2004-06-06 00:00:00 by Kerrie

I notice in the DCMI Metadata Terms document that "Audience"is listed as a recommended element. What status does it have exactly? It is not list in the element list. We are not sure whether to use dc.audience or use EDNA.audience which is part of an Australian standard.

Many thanks for any help

Kerrie

Response given on: 2004-06-07 08:19:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

The audience element is indeed a full-fledged DC element. As with *all* new element additions to DC, it was put into a namespace separate from the original 15 elements (http://purl.org/dc/terms/ ). It has a status of "recommended" which is the same status as was given title, description etc. in DC. I, too, work on a project that had its own audience element. When this element was approved on 21 May 2001, we changed our usage to the DCMI namespace. Please note, however, that there are a number of new elements and element refinements that have been assigned the status of "conforming". They, too, are full-fledged DC elements and can be used effectively wherever there is a need. The "conforming" status means nothing more than: (1 ) the element was considered by the Usage Board to be useful for a specific domain but perhaps not generally useful across domains; and/or (2 ) its use was for purposes other than discovery.

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian

Question asked on: 2004-05-30 00:00:00 by Tsach

Hi,
Maybe it's more general question about XML,
but if I have metadata where the namespace declaration points to the save URI but have different prefix as the examples - e.g.:

<rdf:RDF xmlns:rdf="http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#"
xmlns:d_core ="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/">
.
.
.
<d_code:date>1998-10-10</d_code:date>

so - is it still a valid DC metadata or
whether the elements MUST use the "dc:" prefix?

Response given on: 2004-06-08 11:55:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

Hello Tsach,

Yes, your example is fine. For consistency to human readers, DCMI tends to use the prefixes "dc", "dcterms" and "dcmitype" in its documentation and in its XML documents.

But to an application, the prefix used in the XML QName doesn't matter. RDF/XML makes use of XML QNames and XML Namespaces purely as a mechanism for representing URI references, and the prefix used is transparent to the application.

Regards

Pete Johnston

Question asked on: 2004-05-26 00:00:00 by Rachel

I want to use the DCMI Type metadata terms at all 3 levels of a hierarchical database, but some of my colleagues (subject specialists, not metadata specialists) are questioning their usefulness. I've responded that it'll be helpful in setting up institution-wide browse functionality and for metadata harvesting. Are there other uses I'm overlooking? Thanks in advance.

Response given on: 2004-06-19 12:54:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Rachel:

Our general advice for projects using a more granular or domain specific type terminology is to add a DCMIType term in a separate Type iteration as well. This ensures that if another implementer doesn't wish to use your type vocabulary, they still have a vocabulary they can use and understand. It's not really necessary to link the vocabularies in a hierarchy to accomplish this kind of simple interoperability.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2004-05-22 00:00:00 by Peter

I am a project manager (also a professional librarian) at the University of Texas at Austin. I am currently working on a meta-search application for digital images which are currently owned/curated by a number of different departments in our College of Liberal Arts. Individual departments may have from about 5000 - 15,000 images. Some have associated metadata, but it is not all in digital form (e.g., a few notations on the slide itself).

I need to develop a classification scheme which will incorporate the richness of existing discipline-specific metadata and give direction to future cataloging efforts. Up to this point, departments have simply cobbled together "best guess" schemes based on the materials (images) at hand. (E.g., this is a picture of a vase, lets make a subject heading Vase--Egyptian).

My idea is to let each discipline develop their own dc dialect, comprised of qualifiers on certain Dublic Core elements. For example, Middle Eastern Studies may have dc:creator.architect in which will be placed the name of the architect of a Mosque and dc:creator.patronfor the patron of a Mosque. Likewise, the film department may have dc:creator.director and dc:creator.screenplay_author. Dc:subject will be even more widely ranging (if/when a particular subject scheme such as LCSH is not followed) and will provide a rich metadata set for users wishing to search a specific discipline. For the "digital tourist" a search over unqualified Dublin Core will simply ignore the qualifiers and retrieve, for instance, any images with a dc:creator equaling their search term.

These "dialects" of Dublin Core qualifiers will be developed by scholars in each discipline. The aim will be to provide a rich metadata framework while meeting the "dumb-down" principle that without qualifiers a search still gets useful results, but simply with more recall and less precision.

My question: is this a valid take on the goals of the Dublin Core effort? In many case we will not be using DCMI "best practices" qualifiers (although in some cases, such as dc:coverage, we certainly will), but rather developing unique sets of qualifiers. Is this taking the aims of Dublin Core beyond their intended scope?

Any other thoughts or comments would be greatly appreciated. Also, I would greatly appreciated the opportunity to speak with someone with experience and expertise in this area and any recommendations of who I might contact would be great.

thanks much-
Peter Keane

Response given on: 2004-06-18 05:34:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

Peter,

I think your description of your scenario probably reflects a common challenge facing Dublin Core implementers - and it seems to me your approach to it is quite sound.

It touches on a couple of important issues.

1. the distinction between the metadata schema used internally to an application and the metadata which that application "exposes" to the "outside world"

(I'm agnostic on whether that "exposure" is through result sets of searches or through some other mechanism like exporting/harvesting ).

It is quite common for an application to use some richer metadata schema internally and then to map/transform that richer structure to "Simple Dublin Core" for use by external applications.

It may be the case that that internal metadata schema was designed without any reference to the DCMES itself, or (as you suggest ) the internal metadata schema may be explicitly based on DC. Typically the mapping to Simple DC is much easier in the latter case because it involves a direct simplification/"dumb-down", rather than a more complex transformation.

2. "Dialects" of Dublin Core

Increasingly, implementers wish not only to document their internal metadata schemas, but to share metadata records conforming to these richer "dialects" between applications, and to ensure that their local refinements/extensions are "understood" by the recipient.

Researchers have developed the idea of the "Dublin Core Application Profile (DCAP )" to represent the practice you describe: using DC metadata terms (elements, element refinements, encoding schemes ) in association with terms either drawn from other metadata vocabularies or developed specifically for the purposes of the application. A DCAP often prescribes additional constraints on the obligation to use elements or on the encoding schemes to be deployed for their values.

The key factor (which again you acknowledge ) is to ensure that the extensions you make in your dialect(s ) conform to the DC "grammatical principles" or meta-model.

i.e. if you introduce a new property and describe it as an element refinement of dc:date, then if an application acts on that assertion "in good faith" and "dumbs-down" your metadata to Simple DC, the values that you have assigned to your property should be valid values for dc:date.

So for example, while it would be quite reasonable for one of your dialects to introduce a property "updatingFrequency" which describes how often a resource is modified, and takes values like "monthly", "weekly" etc, it would _not_ be appropriate to describe that property as an element refinement of dc:date, because those values would not be appropriate as values of dc:date.

For some further information on DC Application Profiles, see

http://askdcmi.askvrd.org/index.asp?cat=1735

http://www.cenorm.be/isss/cwa14855/

Regards

Pete Johnston

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Project manager

Response given on: 2004-05-25 04:08:00 by Palle Aagaard

Palle Aagaard

Fiona,

Perhaps you should rephrase your question including an example of your problem, because mayby I have misunderstood your question.

But as a general help I can state the following about using DC.

For each DC element in use (for your records ) you have to fill in the content of the elments. E.g for the element "DC.Title" you have to fill in the title of the resource. The same for e.g. "DC.Decription" where you fill in a description of the resource.

How search engines later index and uses this kind of information (also free (or full ) text ) for later search and retrieval by users, is another problem whch is solved by "the search engine people".

Question asked on: 2004-05-17 00:00:00 by Vicky

Hi,

I'm writing on behalf of the Center for Experimental Social Science at New York University. We're conducting a research project on standard setting groups. DCMI has been included in our sample. We're trying to find out how long it takes on average for a standard to travel from its original proposal to its adoption within an SSO. It'd be greatly appreciated if you can respond with information regarding the average time required to complete a standard setting process at DCMI.

Thank you very much for your attention. We look forward to hearing from you.

Vicky Chang
Center for Experimental Social Science
New York University


Response given on: 2004-05-22 11:17:00 by Makx Dekkers

Makx Dekkers
Managing Director, DCMI

Defining exactly what is the turnaround time for "completion of a standard" is not straighforward. Looking at the process we went through with the Dublin Core Element Set v.1.1 (see http://dublincore.org/documents/dces/ ), you could say that the process started at the first Dublin Core meeting in 1995 (see: http://dublincore.org/workshops/dc1/ ). As the International Standard ISO15836 was formally adopted in late 2003, the total process could be said to have taken eight years. However, it makes more sense to take the date of finalisation of version 1.0 as the starting date (September 1998 ). The definition of version 1.1 was finalised in July 1999, so less than one year later. NISO then started the process to move this to a US National Standard and published NISO Z39.85 in September 2001, two years after it was proposed; at that point, it was proposed for the fast-track procedure in ISO which led to publication of the ISO standard in late 2003, so another two years. The total process therefore took five years (one for DCMI, two for NISO and two for ISO ).
For the internal processes withing DCMI, it is our experience that new proposals (e.g. for refinements or additional elements ) take between six months and a year, but again this depends on what starting point you take. If you start counting when the proposal is written up for review and comment, then it would be up to a year; if you start when the initial idea is brought up, it could be two years depending on the complexity of the issue and the available resources.

Question asked on: 2004-05-12 00:00:00 by Tammy

I was looking over the DCMI Metadata Terms document on the website. Can I use the qualifiers listed with any element that is also listed? Our project would find it useful to have more granularity by doing this. I have a table that shows this if you would like me to send it.

Tammy Jones

Response given on: 2004-05-14 13:59:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

Tammy,

The term "qualifier" was used historically to refer to a set of terms that are now typically treated as two distinct classes of term: element refinements and encoding schemes.


1. Element refinements

Elements and element refinements are both properties, and are used to make statements about resources of the form:

resource - property - value

Element refinements are properties that are related to exactly one DC element, in such a way that they narrow the semantics of that element. Any statement made using an element refinement implies that the statement made using the corresponding element is also true. e.g. if I say

myText dcterms:created "2004-05-14"

I imply

myText dc:date "2004-05-14"

Or if I say

myText dcterms:spatial "North America"

I imply

myText dc:coverage "North America"

An element refinement is always associated with only one element, and that association is part of DCMI's definition of the element refinement.

So, for element refinements, the answer to your question is that you can not associate the element refinement with another Dublin Core element.


2. Encoding schemes

An encoding scheme provides more information about the value of an element or element refinement. So, for example, the use of dcterms:LCSH as an encoding scheme specifies that a value is a specific type of subject heading, one drawn from the LCSH. The use of dcterms:W3CDTF as an encoding scheme specifies that a string representing a date is encoded in a particular form, and can be interpreted according to a published set of rules.

DCMI does recommend the use of these encoding schemes for the values of specific elements or element refinements. So dcterms:LCSH is recommended for use with dc:subject; dcterms:W3CDTF with dc:date (and its refinements ), and so on.

In theory at least, you could use an encoding scheme with an element or refinement other than the one it was recommended for, without breaking the "data model" on which DC is based - though you would have to decide on a case by case basis whether it was useful to do so: an encoding scheme for language codes would make no sense applied to the value of dc:date, for example. And looking at the encoding schemes available, I'm not sure there are many cases where they are useful for values of an element other than those for which they are recommended.

I'd also add a note of caution that it's worth bearing in mind that such associations between an encoding scheme and an element (or element refinement ) are typically prescribed by the context in which your metadata is being used. e.g. applications processing your metadata records may work on the basis that all your values for dc:subject are LCSH terms. In DCMI terminology, this would be described as working with a specific "Application Profile" of Dublin Core. So you'd need to make sure that applications using your metadata records were able to process the values you supplied.

Regards

Pete Johnston
