Question asked on: 2004-05-05 00:00:00 by Paul

Is "time" implied in the "date" element? If not, why isn't "time" a unique element? (Time is defined as time when the content became publically available.)

Thx,

Paul

Response given on: 2004-05-14 12:17:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

Paul,

The usage guideline for dc:date recommends that the value conforms to the W3C Date/Time Format

http://www.w3.org/TR/NOTE-datetime

That format allows you to specify a time down to a decimal fraction of a second:

YYYY-MM-DDThh:mm:ss.sTZD
(eg 1997-07-16T19:20:30.45+01:00 )

where

YYYY = four-digit year
MM = two-digit month (01=January, etc. )
DD = two-digit day of month (01 through 31 )
hh = two digits of hour (00 through 23 ) (am/pm NOT allowed )
mm = two digits of minute (00 through 59 )
ss = two digits of second (00 through 59 )
s = one or more digits representing a decimal fraction of a second
TZD = time zone designator (Z or +hh:mm or -hh:mm )

Question asked on: 2004-04-22 00:00:00 by Evan Prodromou

I'm wondering what the proper syntax is for a <dc:identifier> element that is a URI. My instinct is to write it as:

<dc:identifier>http://www.example.net/some/uri</dc:identifier>

However, some of the examples in the Qualified Dublin Core in RDF/XML use this format:

<dc:identifier rdf:resource="http://www.example.net/some/uri" />

My understanding is that by using rdf:resource, one is saying that the identifier is the resource at the URI, not the URI itself.

But the URI qua URI is the actual identifier -- not whatever's identified by the resource.

Am I misguided and incorrect? Does it matter? Which is the preferred format?

Response given on: 2004-05-14 12:10:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Research Officer, UKOLN;Chair, DC Collection Description WG

Evan,

The Guidelines for Simple DC in RDF/XML [1] recommend that the URI of a resource is encoded using RDF/XML's "built-in" rdf:about mechanism, rather than using a dc:identifier property.

So

http://my.org/">
My resource


I think your understanding of the example (2.3.8.1 ) in the Qualified DC in RDF/XML document is basically correct - you are not misguided (and I think that example would benefit from some additional commentary because it is not clear that it is saying something rather different from the example above )

i.e.


http://my.org/"/>
My resource


The subject is a blank node, so this says, "There exists a resource, which has as identifier a second resource denoted by the URI http://my.org/".

I queried this myself recently and the example I was given was that of a person and their passport.

As a person, I don't have a URI assigned to denote me. I do have a passport. That passport has a unique identifier that I could use as the basis of a URI. Using a pattern like the one above, I could make an RDF statement saying that my passport identifies me. e.g.


http://passports.gov.uk/1234567"/>
http://somedomain/Person"/>


The subject of that statement is a resource of type Person (me ). The object is my passport. My passport acts as an identifier for me, but that URI denotes my passport, not me.

So, as for which is the preferred format, it depends exactly what you want to say. ;- )

If your URI denotes your subject resource, then use the rdf:about approach. If your URI is really the URI of a second resource, and that second resource acts as an identifier for your subject, then use the convention of example 2.3.8.1 in the QDC in RDF/XML doc.

Regards

Pete Johnston

[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmes-xml/
[2] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-rdf-xml/

Tags: Name:RDF; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2004-04-22 00:00:00 by Andrew

I have two versions of a document -- the original in English and the translation in Spanish. For the English version it is clear that 'creator' is the author. For the Spanish translation is the 'creator' the original author (he is primarily responsible for the content) or the translator (he is resposible for making the resource)?
Should the translator be considered a 'contributor'?

Thanks

Response given on: 2004-05-22 07:52:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Andrew:

I don't think there's a "right" answer to this question. As you know, DC does not have an underlying rule base, like AACR2, to answer questions like this unambiguously.

Clearly, for the translation you need to list both the original author and the translator. I suspect that if you use the author as creator for both the original and the translation, with the translator as a Contributor on the translated version, you'll be taking the path that most implementors would choose.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Response given on: 2004-05-17 08:32:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Research Officer, UKOLN;Chair, DC Collection Description WG

We have found that some validating parsers do throw errors when processing the complexTypes in dcterms.xsd based on the SimpleLiteral type. However, based on comments on the XMLSchema-dev list and our testing with some of the major parsers such as XSV and MSXML, we felt that this usage was correct. See Appendix A of the Notes at

http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/qdc/2003/04/
02/notes/

for more details.

The major rationale for defining the SimpleLiteral type as we did was for maximum flexibility. We expected implementors to be able both to extend and restrict this type to meet their own needs.

If you knew you would never need/want to extend the SimpleLiteral to allow child elements, you could simplify the schemas by taking a copy and changing the SimpleLiteral type in dc.xsd to a simpleContent model something like:









Regards
Pete Johnston

Question asked on: 2004-04-12 00:00:00 by Steven

I am proposing to develop an ontology for my Doctoral thesis. It could contribute to the Dublin Core or perhaps become a new working group. How would one go about pursuing that?

Many thanks,

Steve Sherman

Response given on: 2004-05-22 11:24:00 by Makx Dekkers

Makx Dekkers
Managing Director, DCMI

Working Groups are established at the annual DCMI event in a meting of the DCMI Advisory Board.

Proposals can be made by anyone in the DCMI community, provided there is a clear definition of the issue and sufficient interest (and commitment to assign resources ) in the DCMI community.

One approach that you can take is present the idea and an outline of your work on DC-General and see if there are people on that list who would be interested to pursue this issue further.

Question asked on: 2004-04-08 00:00:00 by Maria Gavrili

I would like to know how to use the DC for videos' description in combination with Nordic Template. More specifically, I would like to know how to add in the Nordic Template the DCMI Qualifiers or if there is another template only for videos.

Thank you in advance.


Response given on: 2004-04-15 06:26:00 by Palle Aagaard

Palle Aagaard

Dear Maria Gavrili,

When using metadata connected to information you always have to define what "problem" you will solve when using the metadata. Is it resource (information ) discovery, is it resource (information ) workflow, is it describing the information etc. Dublin Core is for resource discovery, so if the "problem" you will solve is retrieval of videos you can use Dublin Core - nevertheless you maybe will need more metadata elements for describing videos.

Regarding the Nordic template. This template produces DC metadata in lines with a correct syntax. If you will add the qualifiers you must take these lines and add the qualifieres with the correct syntx by hand.

Regards

Palle Aagaard

Response given on: 2004-05-14 07:13:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Duncan:

DCMI recommends either of two encoding schemes for date: W3CDTF (see http://www.w3.org/TR/NOTE-datetime ) or DCMI-Period (see http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-period/ ).

W3CDTF is the most commonly used.

Question asked on: 2004-03-26 00:00:00 by Anna Koulikourdi

I am having a project about finding metadata standards for museum collections. I would like to know about DC templates, editors and generators for museum collections (and which museums make use of DC) or of other metadata standards about museums if they exist (such as CIMI)

Question asked on: 2004-03-03 00:00:00 by John

Am I correct in concluding that DCMI specifications are compatible with SCORM conformant Learning Management Systems (LMS) that have been tested and approved through the various phases (to 1.4) at the ADL Co Lab Plug Fests? Reference (www.ADLNet.org)

V/R
John Driscoll

Response given on: 2004-03-05 18:33:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

SCORM fully adheres to the IEEE LOM and "strongly recommends the use of the IEEE LOM for describing SCORM Content Model Components." (SCORM Content Aggregation Model, Version 1.3, January 30, 2004, p. 93 ) Since IEEE LOM documentation explcitly maps the LOM to unqualified Dublin Core, by inference can say that the DCMI specification for unqualified DC is compatible.

Question asked on: 2004-03-02 00:00:00 by Maria

I am interested to learn if any institution is using DC to describe digitized maps. If so, if they have furthered refined and labeled DC qualifiers and came up with some type of standard.

Response given on: 2004-04-20 13:16:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Many libraries have extensive collections of maps. Those that provide DC interfaces to their catalogues will normally use dc:coverage to indicate the spatial extent of the map.

The value of the dc:coverage can use the dcmi Box encoding to record the extreme values of the area.

"Digitised" maps: if they merely represent a pre-existing mapsheet, then these are just a different manifestation of the map, and most of the metadata would be the same. However, many jurisdictions are now managing most of their geospatial data in digital form throughout. In this context "mapsheet" is becoming obsolete, since the information is often not stored this way. Mapsheet products are often still available, and dc:coverage should be used, as discussed above.

Simon Cox
Commonweath Scientific & Industrial Research Organisation, Australia

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2004-02-23 00:00:00 by a

How can I use DCM tags in my project? What steps I have to follow to implement the same?

Response given on: 2004-02-23 08:43:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Your question is really rather complex in that it has a number of sub-questions that must be asked (or additional information provided ) about your project before a full answer can be given. However, off the top, you should note that if the Dublin Core Metadata Element Set (DCMES ) meets your project needs, you are perfectly free to develop "tags" using it.

(1 ) Semantics and sufficiency: First, you need to make sure that the kinds of metadata descriptions your project needs can be sufficiently handled by the DCMES. If the answer is that there is a good match with the semantics (meaning ) of the DCMES and your project needs, you are in good shape with this key question. I say it is key in the sense that to say that your metadata is DCMES metadata, you need to adhere to the meanings as they have been defined. See Overview" target=_BLANK>http://www.dublincore.org/usage/documents/overview/
">Overview of Documentation for DCMI Metadata Terms as the source for links to official explanations of the "meanings" or semantics of the DCMES.

(2 ) Best practices: The benefits of having metadata at all can be enjoyed only if the metadata created is relatively consistent in quality and content. As a result, every project should develop a set of "best practices" to help those that will be creating the metadata to do so in useful ways. The DCMI has developed an excellent set of general "best practices" called Using" target=_BLANK>http://www.dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/
">Using Dublin Core that can be used "as is" or can be extended to meet the particular needs of your project.

(3 ) Syntactic binding: The DCMES defines a set of elements and element refinements (element qualifiers ) and their associated meanings (semantics ). It does not require that each metadata record be syntactically bound (i.e., encoded ) in any particular way. That decision is dependent on where and how your project's metadata will used. As a result, DCMI does not say that you have to syntactically bind your metadata as HTML, or XML, or XML/RDF (Resource" target=_BLANK>http://www.w3.org/RDF/">Resource Description Framework ) tags. That decision is up to you and to your technical people depending on project needs.

(4 ) Metadata generation tools. Finally, you have to create your metadata and store it in a metadata repository where it can be used by your project (and, perhaps, be harvested for use in other projects ). The best place to start looking for tools to generate metadata and to store it and make it accessible is the DCMI Tools" target=_BLANK>http://dublincore.org/tools/">Tools and Software page.

In the end, each of these four topics will probably trigger additional questions when you begin to drill down into them. But, this should get you started thinking about the "how" of DCMES use in your project.

Best of luck with your project!

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Programmer

Question asked on: 2004-02-19 00:00:00 by thomax

hello,

i'm new to this system, but i'm not afraid to use it. i hope a kind person will respond with a helpful hint.

i'm a data artist and i designed the open meta archive which is able to produce dublin core compatible output, encapsulated in rdf.

currently the meta archive uses a refined data set according to the ebu (european broadcast union) refinements. now i like to know what would be a clear and compatible syntax to express the refinements.

an example is the dc title. it is refined by the ebu to an additional title alternative.

if i express this like:
:
<dc:title>First Title</dc:title>
<dc:title ebu="alternative">Alternative Title</dc:title>
:

would you say it is still OK with the dublin core specifications and will be OK for parsers out there?

i would be glad for hints if this is a completely wrong approach.

thomax


Response given on: 2004-02-20 13:43:00 by Pete Johnston

Thomax,I don't think you can take the approach you suggest in RDF/XML. The RDF/XML syntax is designed to be a serialisation of an RDF graph, a set of RDF triples: when an RDF/XML document is parsed the components of the XML tree are mapped to parts of the RDF graph as described in the RDF/XML syntax specification [1].So the XML elements in your RDF/XML document are interpreted as what RDF/XML calls "node elements" or "property elements" e.g. the XML element rdf:Description is a node element and corresponds to a node in the RDF graph; the XML elements dc:publisher. dc:identifier (etc ) are property elements and correspond to arcs in the graph. But your use of a "type" XML attribute doesn't fit the RDF/XML language. To represent a triple using the property http://purl.org/dc/terms/alternative in RDF/XML, you need a corresponding property element i.e.http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/"xmlns:dcterms="http:/
/purl.org/dc/terms/"> alternative title Similarly for your non-DCMI terms, you need to assign URIrefs for those properties and use appropriate XML elements to represent themhttp://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#" xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/"xmlns:your="http:/
/your.org/terms/"> 2004-01-29T13:54:18Z The W3C provides a Web-based validation service for RDF/XML, which you may find useful [2]Are you sure you want to use RDF/XML? DCMI also provides a convention for encoding DC metadata in XML - though it adopts similar conventions to RDF/XML and would require the use of distinct XML elements for each property. [3]In using a type attribute as you do, you are really creating your own XML format - which is OK as far as your own application is concerned, but other applications will not interpret it as you expect.Pete[1] http://www.w3.org/TR/rdf-syntax-grammar/ [2] http://www.w3.org/RDF/Validator/[3] http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/

Response given on: 2004-02-13 06:37:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

I think that efforts on implementing/realising the Warwick Framework effectively shifted to the W3C's work on the Resource Description Framework (RDF ) family of specifications and the Semantic Web activity.

Although more recent documents on RDF don't reference the Warwick Framework, there are a number of earlier references that describe RDF as an "instantiation of the Warwick Framework on the Web" or at least acknowledge the influence of WF on RDF.

See for example

http://www.ariadne.ac.uk/issue15/events/stakis.html
http://www.lib.helsinki.fi/meta/oclc/sld017.htm

http://www.w3.org/People/EM/talks/www7/tutorial/
part1/sld047.htm

cimi/taiwan/day2/iap-html/sld046.htm

http://www.dlib.org/dlib/may98/miller/05miller.html

Question asked on: 2004-02-13 00:00:00 by Erin

Can you clarify for me the status of Audience?

It does not appear in:
http://dublincore.org/documents/dces/
nor the NISO standard

But is statused as Recommended in:
http://dublincore.org/documents/2002/10/06/current-elements/index.shtml
and provided for as the 16th element in the User Guide at:
http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/elements.shtml

If it is indeed a ârefinementâ and not a core element, how do we explain this given that it is not a child?

Thanks for any help you can provide,
Erin

Response given on: 2004-02-23 08:53:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

NOTE: This answer is from an email exchange posted to the DC-Corporate and combines responses from Stuart Sutton and Thomas Baker.

[Stuart Sutton]
"Erin, 'Audience' indeed has the DCMI status of recommendation and is equal in that sense with any of the original 15 DC elements. While it appears in the same namespace as the refinements, it is nevertheless what I consider a top-level DC element since it is not a refinement of any other element. The 'Audience' element was first put forward by the DC-Education Working Group (which I co-chair ) and was granted recommended status by the Usage Board on 5/21/2001. While there are various logical explanations for why this element appears in the same namespace as the refinements and not in the one with the original 15 elements, *none* of those explanations dispel the confusion created by the fact that the namespaces are different. So, no matter what machinations we go through to explain and to distinguish ... bottom line, 'Audience' is the 16th element and may be used as such with the full blessing of DCMI (as far as I can tell ). That is not to say that some legitimate uses may be limited by fiat or logic to the original 15."Stuart is correct. Logically speaking, there are top-level elements (the fifteen "core" elements plus Audience ) and elements that are also sub-properties of top-level elements ("element refinements" ). There are historical reasons for this particular terminology on which one could elaborate, but Stuart is quite right to get to the bottom line."
[Stuart Sutton

[Thomas Baker]
"That the URI for Audience (http://purl.org/dc/terms/audience )
has a different prefix than the URI for Title
(http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/title ) is also due to historical reasons. As pioneers in the use of URIs to identify terms, DCMI was among the first to learn -- the hard way -- the dangers of hard-coding 'grammatical' and versioning information into a URI string -- which is why DCMI switched from using a prefix with the string 'elements/1.1'
to using a prefix with the generic string 'terms'. Here too, one could elaborate for pages...

When Audience was approved, we considered calling Audience 'the sixteen element of the Dublin Core'. However, that implied a Dublin Core 1.2, which in turn suggested there would be a Dublin Core 1.3, 1.4, and so on. Whereas we found that people generally preferred the notion of a small set at the center which remains reliably stabile. The price we paid was this nagging seeming-inconsistency with regard to Audience."
[Thomas Baker]

Question asked on: 2004-01-29 00:00:00 by Jill

Kia ora.
Have been doing some thinking about extending our metadata to encompass

* the ability to use metadata to locate a physical object - eg a regional museum - by using geospatial type metadata
I think I've seen some work in this area and am wondering what standards are emerging - GPS type co-ordinates? or are we stuck with eg electoral boundaries type locations (which change so don't really like that idea). Any thoughts?

* the ability to use metadata to locate human beings - by discipline, subject expertise, interests, location, entity

I read some papers about work being done in this area a year or so ago. Is there any real standard/approach emerging to creating people networks discovery?

Cheers

JILL

Response given on: 2004-04-20 13:23:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Jill:

Do you want to locate the museum, or an artefact housed in a museum? If the former, then it is straightforward: dc:coverage is used to record
the spatio-temporal location of the item being described. So the dc:coverage element of the metadata set for the museum will indicate its location.

If it is an artefact in the museum, then I guess dc:coverage should still indicate its *present* location, though museums may also choose to record other locations associated with the artefact as well in additional dc:coverage elements.

The DCMI Agents Working Group (http://dublincore.org/groups/agents/ ) is currently exploring the issues around the identification of people and organizations relevant to Dublin Core. It might be useful to take a look at their webpage, and perhaps consider joining their working list and asking your question there.

Geospatial answer by:
Simon Cox
Commonwealth Scientific & Industrial Research Organisation, Australia

Agents answer by:
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2004-01-23 00:00:00 by Malena

Why has the Administrative Core-group been deactivated?

Response given on: 2004-02-18 12:54:00 by Makx Dekkers

Makx Dekkers
Managing Director, DCMI

The Administrative Metadata Working Group was established in 2000 with the objective to "propose an element set for the management of metadata based on the work with Admin Core - Administrative Container Metadata (A-Core )." In October 2003, the group delivered its results under the title "AC - Administrative Components", available at http://www.bs.dk/standards/AdministrativeComponents.htm, which in effect completed the work of the group. At the DCMI Advisory Board meeting on 3 October 2003, the AB agreed a proposal from the group's chair, Leif Andresen of Denmark (lea@bs.dk ), to deactivate the group.

Tags: Name:Boards, Committees, Working Groups; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Librarian

Question asked on: 2004-01-16 00:00:00 by Jana

In the Relation field, and other fields, why are the first words of the statement run together? For example, "IsVersionOf"

Response given on: 2004-01-16 09:56:00 by Thomas Baker

Thomas Baker

As described in the Introduction to http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms, every term has a Name and a Label. The name is meant to be used in computing environments and read by machines and is therefore, by convention, a single string without spaces "isVersionOf". The Label, which is meant to be read by humans, does have spaces: "Is Version Of".

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian

Response given on: 2004-01-15 16:53:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

Yes, your refinements should be cut down to dc:creator if you want to use DCMI properties. Alternatively, you could define your own XML namespace and new properties within that namespace for tki:name and tki:contact - but that wouldn't be DCMI metadata.

I would encode an ISBN as a URN as follows


urn:isbn:12345-67890

Question asked on: 2004-01-12 00:00:00 by Paul

Need examples of using dc:subject with a formal classification scheme.

Given

<dc:subject>516110</dc:subject>

How do I express that it is from NAICS [1](or some other vocabulary)?

See also [2].

[1] http://www.census.gov/epcd/naics02/def/ND516110.HTM#N516110
[2] http://groups.yahoo.com/group/rss-dev/message/6029

Response given on: 2004-01-23 14:11:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

Hello Paul,

DCMI issues guidelines for expressing DC metadata in RDF/XML [1, 2], XML [3] and XHTML [4]. In [2], [3] and [4], there are examples that signal that a value for dc:subject uses a formal classification scheme (what DC calls an "encoding scheme" ).

From your reference to RSS and Weblog entries, I'm assuming you are wishing to encode this in RSS?

The RSS 1.0 spec uses RDF/XML syntax, so the MESH example in section 2.3.9 of [2] may be of particular interest. (In the RSS context it does not seem to me appropriate to introduce the UDDI category bag convention. )

Although RSS 1.0 uses RDF/XML syntax, the use of Qualified Dublin Core metadata in RSS 1.0 in described in an RSS module [5]. The module provides only limited information (one example at the end of section 1 ) on the use of "encoding schemes".

Although it suggests that in theory the RDF/XML conventions described in [2] could be applied, I'd sound a small note of caution that you may find that some RSS 1.0 tools may support only what the module calls the "simple content model", rather than the full RDF/XML syntax.

Regards

Pete Johnston

[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmes-xml/
[2] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-rdf-xml/
[3] http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/
[4] http://dublincore.org/documents/dcq-html/
[5] http://web.resource.org/rss/1.0/modules/dcterms/

http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/

1) Is/Are there a Recommendation status XML Schema(s)?

3) If so, are they considered stable or do you see changes coming to them in the foreseeable future?

W. Paul Kiel

919-846-0224

Response given on: 2004-02-18 12:36:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Research Officer, UKOLN;Chair, DC Collection Description WG

Paul,

My understanding is that the XML Schemas do not have a formal status in the way that the human-readable documents published by DCMI do.

Yes, for representing Qualified DC metadata, the schemas dated 2003-04-02 are the current versions.

(There is a separate schema for Simple DC metadata only, which uses a different content model, of which the version of 2002-12-12 is the current version. )

In terms of stability, the content of those schemas will not change.

However, DCMI _may_ issue a _revised_ set of schemas to reflect two possible changes:

(a ) changes in the DCMI metadata vocabularies. In particular, new terms are likely to be added to the "dcterms" vocabulary, and new XML element declarations will be added to the XML Schemas for the http://purl.org/dc/terms/ namespace to reflect those updates;

(b ) possible changes to the XML encoding conventions [1] to enhance their capacity to express all the features of DC metadata, particularly in the light of discussions on the DC Abstract Model [2]

At the moment, there are no concrete plans for (b ) and I flag it only as a possibility to be aware of. The DCMI Usage Board regularly considers proposals for new metadata terms, so changes of type (a ) are quite likely in the medium term.

But any changes would result in the issuing of a new set of schemas with URLs reflecting a datestamp.

Regards
Pete Johnston

[1] http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/

[2] http://www.ukoln.ac.uk/metadata/dcmi/abstract-model/

Question asked on: 2003-12-18 00:00:00 by Kai

I am drawing up an RDF to describe research projects. How do I describe Sponsors and Partners to a project? They are very different.

Response given on: 2003-12-19 15:41:00 by Thomas Baker

Thomas Baker

Dublin Core was originally designed for describing "information
resources" with attributes such as Title, Date, Creator,
Subject, and Format. It has long been recognized that
a fuzzy class of objects known as "agents" tend to have
different sorts of attributes, such as Address, Affiliation,
Title (in a different sense ), and Name. While Dublin Core
itself may be "adapted" to describe agents, it is often not
appropriate to do so.

The fact that no "agent core" has yet emerged analogous
to Dublin Core is in part a reflection of the diversity of
requirements for such a core in the wide diversity of contexts
for which agents need to be described. With respect to the
description of projects in particular, the recent White Paper
"Describing Projects" by the DELOS Working Group on Project
Metadata provides a number of links to significant work in
this area.

http://delos-noe.iei.pi.cnr.it/activities/standardizationforum/
Projects.pdf

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2003-12-03 00:00:00 by Judith

Hello,

at the moment I'm writing my bachelor thesis. The subject is "E-Learning-Standards".

Because of this, I need some information about your organisation.

1. When was CEN founded?
2. Where is the head office?
3. Who is the chairman or director?
4. Which specifications/standards have been developed by CEN?

Could you please send me a flyer (PDF format) about CEN? Perhaps in German?

Thanks for all

Response given on: 2003-12-19 14:42:00 by Thomas Baker

Thomas Baker

CEN, the European Committee for Standardization (or [C]omite'
[E]uropeen de [N]ormalisation ) is an organization in Brussels
founded in 1961 to develop and promote voluntary technical
standards that advance the goals of the European Union in a
diversity of areas such as trade, environmental protection, and
computer network interoperability.

As Dublin Core is an important metadata standard in
the European Union, CEN sponsors the development of
Dublin-Core-related specifications in the context of
an ongoing "Workshop on Dublin Core Metadata" (see
http://www.cenorm.be/sh/mmi-dc ).

The CEN Dublin Core Workshop is philosophically similar to
the Dublin Core Metadata Initiative itself inasmuch they
both mobilize the efforts of experts in mailing lists and
meetings that are open to interested members of the community.

Organizationally, the CEN workshop and DCMI are entirely
separate. In practice, however, there is significant overlap
in membership between DCMI and the CEN workshop, and the
two organizations closely coordinate their complementary
standardization efforts.

Tags: Name:About DCMI; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2003-12-03 00:00:00 by keith

Hello,
I am currently redoing some html for a company and I came across html code for another site that has your tags on it:

<META NAME="DC.Title" CONTENT="retro clip art, retro clipart, food clipart, sports clip art, funny clip art, clip art">
<META NAME="DC.Subject" CONTENT="retro clip art, retro clipart, food clipart, sports clip art, funny clip art, computer clip art, holiday clipart, business clipart, clip art pictures, clipart images, clip art online, fifties, forties, thirties, clip art">
__________________________________________

My question is this. Is it ok for me to use META NAME="DC.Title" and <META NAME="DC.Subject" onto my site: retroart.com, and will this higher us up on the rankings? I have never seen html like this before and quite honestly I do not understand how it is beneficial to a site, and what the terms of use are.

If you could email me back some answers and insight into this that would be much appreciated.

Thanks

Keith hoffart
keith@wedshow.com

Response given on: 2003-12-22 19:22:00 by Andrew Wilson

Andrew Wilson
Project Manager, Digital PreservationNational Archives of AustraliaMember, DC Usage Board

Hi

Yes, it is quite OK for you to use DC.Title and DC.Subject on your website. The DCMI element set can be used by anyone for any website. However, I should point out that the site you refer to has misused the Title element. Title should be used for the actual title of the html page you are describing.

As to whether using DC Title and Subject will mean your page comes higher up in search results on commercial search engines then the answer, at the moment, is unfortunately not. None of the major public search engines like Yahoo! or Google make use of DC tags.

cheers
Andrew Wilson

Question asked on: 2003-11-17 00:00:00 by Jean

To whom it may concern,
I am currently working on a digital library project in Cold Fusion where I have to include Dublin Core Metadata. And I have a few questions concerning that.

1.Can the dublin core metadata be generated dynamically from a database when we access the digital resource?

2.if I use a database for my search engine, what is the dublin core utility

3.I know dublin core is made to describe objects, but how those metatags can be accessed through a search

4.how does it work?

Thank you in advance

Jean Roy
ARts Netlantic
Programmer/Analyst and
research assistant
UniversitÃÆÃÂ© de Moncton

Response given on: 2004-01-07 13:43:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Jean:

Apolgies for the delay in replying. I'll answer your questions in the order you asked them.

1. Yes, DC metadata can certainly be generated dynamically from databases. Many projects do this. The generated metadata is only as good as the program that creates it, so be sure that the metadata your project generates is of good quality.

2. I'm not entirely sure I understand this question, but let me take a stab at it. You seem to be asking "why bother?" if your database is accessible via your website's search engine. I think if this is all you want to do, it probably is unnecessary. However, once you look beyond your site, or you decide to provide other services besides search, having standard metadata can be a good investment. Not everyone will want to go through your site to discover your resources, and if you partner with others to provide services you'll not necessarily want to provide access to your database to accomplish that.

3. I'm not sure you need to expose the metadata in your search results, but it can be helpful to do so, particularly if your search results display is brief. If you go to http://nsdl.org and do a search, notice that there is a "more info" button beside each result. If you click on that, you will see the metadata showing up beside the results. Users tell us that this helps them make decisions about which resources meet their needs.

4. It works a lot of different ways, depending on the project. Take a look at the DCMI Projects page (http://dublincore.org/projects/ ) for other projects that might be similar to yours. You'll find that there are lots of different approaches to making metadata work.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2003-11-13 00:00:00 by Karen

When cataloging very large technical reports in pdf format, I would like to be able to record the number of pages. Would the Format.extent refinement be the proper place for number of pages, or is it only for file size (in kilobytes)?

Response given on: 2003-11-14 12:15:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation;Chair, DCMI Collection Description WG

The definition of the Extent refinement is sufficiently broad to include any measurement of the size or duration of a resource, so yes, it would be reasonable to use it to record the number of pages in a document.

If you wish to record the size of the digital object also, it would be good practice to express these two values as two separate occurrences of the Extent refinement within a single description.

Extent = "66 pages"
Extent = "999 kilobytes"

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Cataloger

Question asked on: 2003-11-11 00:00:00 by Martin

In the project European Music Navigator we are about to classify information on music. Information on scores, recordings, composers, events and so on from a lot of partners all over Europe will be assembled in an Internet portal with a search engine.

To enhance the search result we would now like to make a content classification based on the metadata standard Dublin Core.

However we need to add a new controlled vocabulary to Dublin Core as the existing ones do not fully support our needs. We would like to add in the HTML-code a meta tag consisting of the subject element combined with something like <scheme=EMN>

My question is in what order this should be done.

a) Is it suitable or not to start naming and using a scheme and then register it later with DCMI?

b) Would it be better to register the scheme immediately even if we will add or refine parts of the controlled vocabulary later on, or

c) Should we rather start using DC without expressing anything like <Scheme=EMN>.

In addition I also wonder if the schemes EMN, EMNT and EMNG are still available?


Response given on: 2004-01-06 01:00:00 by Andrew Wilson

Andrew Wilson
Manager AGLS, NAA 1999-2002Digital Preservation Project Manager NAA 2002-2005Preservation Services and Projects Manager AHDS 2005-DC Usage Board 2003-PREMIS Elements Working Groupetc.

Hi Martin

I'm sorry there's been such a delay in replying.

I think it is quite appropriate for you to name and start using your scheme without having it registered with DCMI. There is no requirement that you register schemes with DCMI before you use them, and by using it staright away you will encourage your users to think about subject classification right from the start of your implementation. I think you should go ahead and starty using your scheme immediately. You might like to register it with DCMI once you have a reasonably stable version of the scheme.

As far as I know the scheme names EMN, EMNT, and EMNG are all available - they have not been registered with DCMI as scheme names. So they should be available for you to use as soon as you like.

One thing I should add is that it would be very useful if you could establish a namespace for the scheme as soon as possible. Schemes ideally should be publicly accessible over the Web so they can be more widely seen and used, and setting up a namespace will establish your claim to the scheme name you wish to use.

I hope this has been of some help.

Cheers
Andrew Wilson

Question asked on: 2003-10-29 00:00:00 by Denise

A DCSV scheme indicates that I can string together a list of labeled values. For the element Creator, I would like to use the following structured value Scheme "creator.lastname=Doe; creator.firstname=Jane; creator.role=transcriber; creator.dates=1920-2000" when I am exchanging data with a third party. My question is can I include creator.lastname, creator.firstname, creator.role and creator.dates as separate individual elements in my metadata scheme and then use XML (XSLT?) to concatenate the 4 individual elements into the string when I am transmitting the data? Would the 4 individual elements be local tags because of the refinements I am using?

Response given on: 2003-10-29 16:29:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

Your proposed usage of DCSV is unlikely to be interoperable with anyone else - therefore I would advise against it. In particular, the 'role' is not an attribute of the creator, but gives the relationship between the creator and a particular resource. Therefore it should be modelled as an element refinement.

In general, you can do what you like within the confines of your own application. It is only when exporting metadata to external systems that you need to get the modelling correct - but I'm not convinced that the model in your proposed usage of DCSV is the correct one.

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2003-10-29 00:00:00 by Denise

In the DCMI document about string values, "DCMI DCSV: A syntax for writing a list of labeled values in a text string", it gives the example <META NAME="DC.Contributor" SCHEME="DCMIDCSV" CONTENT="name.given=Eric; name.family=Miller; employer=OCLC; height=170 cm"> I am a bit confused about this. Doesn't the inclusion of employer and height information violate the One-to-one Principle since you are now describing the Contributor as well as the resource? We have some plans to revise the documentation associated with DCSV, but no firm timescales for when this might actually take place - partly because we have been waiting for the related discussions about the DCMI Abstract Model to reach some form of conclusion.

Question asked on: 2003-10-24 00:00:00 by Jeffrey

Dear all,

I work for the United Nations on a humanitarian information project called ReliefWeb. We are trying to develop a metadata system based on ISO 15836-2003 for cataloging project data.

We are working with a selection of elements from the Dublin Core Metadata Element Set. Most of these elements correspond quite logically with metadata fields in our current data set. For other fields in our current set we are developing what might be called sub-elements. For a given document on ReliefWeb, we record, for example, a number of dates -- the date the document was created, the date it was published to our site, and subsequent dates when the document's metadata was updated or modified. We're under the impression that we can remain compliant with the DCM element even with our sub-elements: Date -> Creation; Date -> Publication; Date -> Modification. (please correct us if we're wrong, of course)

The problem pops up for fields that we cannot locate logically within the DCM element set. For example, we classify text documents with a descriptive term from a controlled vocabulary -- 'News', 'Press Releases', 'Situation Reports', etc. Is this a DCM element set 'Description', a 'Format', a 'Type' or, if not any of these, then what? If it is a 'Type', what is the appropriate sub-element? We could use Type -> Text, but that doesn't refer to a descriptor used for categorization of data, which is our purpose for using such terms in the first place.

It is quite possible that we have not grasped some fundamental metadata concepts. But in any event, we would certainly appreciate your feedback.

Thanks and best regards,

The ReliefWeb Team
http://www.reliefweb.int


Response given on: 2003-10-30 13:45:00 by Pete Johnston

Pete Johnston
Technical Researcher, Eduserv Foundation

Jeffrey,

The Dublin Core Metadata Element Set provides 15 "elements" to describe the properties (characteristics, attributes ) of a resource. As the DCMES provides only 15 elements, the distinctions you can make between characteristics of your resource are limited: you can make only simple "statements" such as "this is a date of an event in the lifecycle of the resource I'm describing" (using dc:date ) or "this second resource is related (somehow! ) to the resource I'm describing".

So the short answers to your two questions are:

(i ) Using only the 15 elements of the DCMES, all the date fields you describe would provide values for the dc:date element - and using only the DCMES, a consumer of your DC description would not be able to distinguish between the date of creation, publication and modification. They are all simply "dates of events in the lifecycle of the resource".

(ii ) The examples you cite for your "descriptor used for categorization of data" look to me like examples of values for dc:type. They describe (I think! ) "the nature or genre of the content of the resource". DCMI does provide a simple, high level "DCMI Type Vocabulary" (which, as you note, includes the term "Text" ), but you are not limited to using that Type Vocabulary and can use your own "local" vocabulary to provide values for dc:type. Having said that, you need to bear in mind what applications will be consuming your metadata and whether those applications are programmed to interpret your values as you expect. Especially if you are sharing your metadata with applications that may not have "built-in knowledge" of your "local" Type Vocabulary, it is considered good practice to include a term from the DCMI Type Vocabulary where possible. All DC metadata elements are repeatable (unless in the context of your application some additional constraints have been specified ), so you could include two occurrences of dc:type in each metadata description:

dc:type = "Text"
dc:type = "Press Release"

To answer your question slightly more fully, it's probably best to say a little bit about Dublin Core's concept of "qualification".

DCMI provides two mechanisms that allow you to make more precise statements than are possible using only the 15 elements of the DCMES.

Firstly, DCMI provides several "element refinements". Each element refinement is explicitly related to exactly one of the 15 DC elements. Just like the 15 elements, an element refinement describes a property of a resource, but it allows you to make a narrower, more precise statement e.g. there is a refinement "date created" (dcterms:created ), which refines dc:date and allows you to specify that this date is specifically the date of creation of the resource. It's very important to note that element refinements always provide a "narrowing" of the meaning of the element which they refine: central to this idea of refinement is the principle that any occurrence of an element refinement can be replaced by an occurrence of the element which it refines with no loss of meaning. A "date created" is always a "date"; an "abstract" is always a "description"; saying A "is part of" B is completely consistent with saying simply A "is related to" B.

Secondly, DC defines several "encoding schemes". The use of an encoding scheme provides some additional information about the value of a DC metadata element (or element refinement ), and encoding schemes fall broadly into two classes:

- vocabulary encoding schemes specify that a value is a term from a controled vocabulary. So for example, the specification of an encoding scheme might help a consumer of a DC description determine whether a dc:subject value "Jethro Tull" referred to the 17th century agriculturalist or to the 20th century progressive rock band. Your type vocabulary is an encoding scheme for dc:type.

- syntax encoding schemes specify that a value is formatted in accordance with some set of rules, such as that a date has the form YYYY-MM-DD

If should still be possible to use the value without the encoding scheme: if an application has no built-in knowledge of an encoding scheme, the value alone may still be useful to a human reader.

The use of these two distinct mechanisms is often described generically as "qualification", leading to the notion of a "qualified Dublin Core" description that uses this larger, richer set of terms, as opposed to a "simple Dublin Core" description that uses only the 15 elements of the DCMES.

In a "qualified Dublin Core" description, you could express your distinction between your different types of date using the element refinements, dcterms:created, dcterms:modified and dcterms:issued, and you could include an indication that your type values were drawn from a specific vocabulary encoding scheme. But this would only be useful if an application reading your metadata is programmed to interpret these terms. An application that is programmed to interpret only the 15 elements of the DCMES would not be able to interpret these other terms.

You've probably noticed that I've avoided using your term "sub-element" above, and this was deliberate. You may well find that term used in some documents related to DC, particularly older documents, but as a rule DCMI tends to avoid using it, in part because it does it does not clearly specify the nature of the relationship between "sub-element" and "element" and in part because it can be confused with the specific concept of element containment/nesting in XML documents.

Regards

Pete Johnston

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2003-10-24 00:00:00 by Denise

Instead of Creator (with the full name as its content), can I split it into Creator.Surname and Creator.Firstname? If I dumb-down the element names, then the contents won't make sense on their own e.g. Creator=Doe and Creator=Jane. Can I call Creator.Surname and Creator.Firstname DC tags or are they local tags? If I decide to use the two Creator fields, when I want share the data with another party, can I use XSLT to merge them into one field called Creator, which will then be a true DC element? Or from the onset, in addition to Creator.Surname and Creator.Firstname should I also have a tag called just Creator and duplicate the full name in its contents (to ensure easy interoperability)?


Response given on: 2003-10-29 16:31:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

Creator.Firstname and Creator.Surname are not valid element refinements. Why? Because, as you argue, they do not dumb-down correctly.

You can, of course, choose to use these tags within your internal system - but you should combine them into a single dc:creator element when you expose your metadata to external systems.

Question asked on: 2003-10-23 00:00:00 by Paul

I'm a web applications developer at the University of Minnesota Libraries. I come from a computer science and RDBMS background. Computer Science departments generally don't teach XML or deal with front-end semantics/markup, but instead emphasize back-end storage mechanics and data normalization.

Have you built any sets of standards for people who are implementing a Dublin Core set of data in an RDBMS environment (field names, data types, primary keys, enforced volcabularies) and this sort of thing? Libraries have never gone down this route since MARC (hundreds of fields and subfields) is not "doable" in a normalized RDBMS. But DC certainly is... So I'm curious how DC might be implemented in an RDBMS, in terms of relationship, normalization, etc. (not merely field name semantics). Thanks for any help!

Response given on: 2003-11-17 22:20:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Paul:

DC maps fairly well to a standard RDBMS. One way to do this is to relate each metadata value to a metadata 'name' in a table structure like this:

Metadata_Names
(PK ) Name_ID (Integer )
Element Name (String*50 )
Refinement Name (String*50 )
Scheme Name (String*50 )

Metadata
(PK ) Metadata_ID (Integer )
(FK ) Name_ID (Integer )
(FK ) Vocabulary_ID (Integer ) OPTIONAL
Value (Memo )

In this scenario, controlled vocabulary values could be stored as regulated rather than related values in the Metadata table. If storage space was at a
premium or there was concern that controlled vocabularies might be revised over time, you could add another table for managing the vocabulary and add an additional foreign key to the metadata table (OPTIONAL above ), using it rather than the memo field when a controlled value was referenced.

The Metadata_Names table will contain an entry for each possible combinationof element/refinement/encoding_scheme in qualified DC. There's an example
here:
http://repository.comm.nsdl.org/cgi-bin/wiki.pl?
QualifiedDCTable

The resulting Metadata & Metadata_Names relationship can be viewed like this:
http://dev.nsdl.org/prs_web/SE_MREC.php?ID=288897 (note this is a temporary URL )

Hope this answers your question.

Jon Phipps
National Science Digital Library
http;//nsdl.org

I would like to include the birth-death dates of a creator and the role that person plays in the creation of the item (e.g. author/illustrator/transcriber, etc.) However, I am concerned about two things, namely, extending the semantics of the Creator tag, and the dumb-down principle. Response given on: 2003-10-29 16:26:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

Creator.Role and Creator.Dates are not valid DC element refinements. Why? Because, as you argue very clearly, they do not dumb-down correctly.

The creator role can be encoded using an element refinement of dc:creator, for example my:illustrator. See the DC LIbraries proposed application profile for an example of the use of MARC relator codes as refinements of dc:contributor.

Birth and death dates could be encoded, in an unstructured way, in the value string for dc:creator, using the kind of formatting used in name authority files, e.g.

Andy Powell, 1960-

Question asked on: 2003-10-21 00:00:00 by Denise

I am trying to use DC to describe images. For each image record, I need to use metadata to describe both the image and the original (source of the image). This means in many cases, I will need duplicate DC element terms but one which describes the image and one that describes the original. E.g. Publisher.Digital and Publisher.Original. Date.Created.Digital and Date.Created.Original. The information on both the digital image and the original must be kept in the same record.

Three questions:

1. Can I have three or four part element names (refer to above)?
2. Am I going to get into a lot of trouble in terms of interoperability if I have to make many local refinements to the basic DC element names? So far I have 109 metadata tags based on the 15 DC element names (I have used many refinements because I need to describe both the image and the original).
3. I am concerned about the Dumb-Down Principle. I need Publisher.Digital and Publisher.Original, but if both terms dumb-down to Publisher, then how do I retain the distinction between the Digital object and the original item.

Thank you.

Response given on: 2003-10-29 16:36:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

Denise,
I think your approach is wrong. The original image and the digitised image are separate resources. Therefore, they need to be described separately. Yes, there will be some repetition in the metadata records. You may choose to design your internal system (e.g. a relational database ) to remove the repetition, but at the point to expose your metadata to external services, you should generate separate metadata records, each with the full set of details in it.

You may also choose to use dc:relation, or dc:source, to indicate the relation between the digital version of the image and the original image.

Question asked on: 2003-10-14 00:00:00 by Martin

Response given on: 2003-11-02 08:37:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Martin:

I think in general implementors don't attempt to link from the resource to the metadata, but rather the other way around: from the metadata to the resource. In many cases, metadata repositories have no access to the resource content, so such tight linkages are impossible.

Question asked on: 2003-10-08 00:00:00 by Tom

All DC literature refers to web (XML, HTML) applications. Response given on: 2003-11-02 08:34:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

It is probably not the size of the project that determines whether Dublin Core is sensible, but rather the nature of the content to be described. DC description covers a wide range of material, but not all--some scientific data sets, for instance, are difficult to describe with DC.

However, your point that any kind of data needs structure and definition is a correct one. The project I work on at present uses Oracle, with Qualified DC as its base, and surely there are others that use it similarly.

Diane I. Hillmann

Question asked on: 2003-10-02 00:00:00 by Cate

When implementing Dublin Core metadata, I know it has to go on every web page, does it also have to go on images and files such as pdfs?

Response given on: 2003-10-14 09:13:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Cate:

How you implement Dublin Core is entirely up to you, and should depend primarily on the needs of your project and the resources you have available.

You may choose to add DC metadata to every webpage, or only some. You may choose to describe all images and files with DC metadata or only a selection (or none ).

Many implementors manage their metadata separately from their resources or pages, using a database. Again, the decisions about what to describe, and how, are up to them.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"
Member, DC Usage and Advisory Boards

Question asked on: 2003-09-18 00:00:00 by fabiana pinna

I would like to have further information about:

What is the syntax to insert it?
Thank you!


Response given on: 2003-10-02 17:30:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Fabiana:

At present, DCMI provides the two encoding recommendations: DCMI Box (http://dublincore.org/documents/2000/07/28/dcmi-box/ ) and DCMI Point (http://dublincore.org/documents/2000/07/28/dcmi-point/ ), both of which are designed to insert labels within a text string.

As text string solutions, these techniques are "syntax-free"--they can be used in any number of syntaxes or data structures. They are admittedly limited, and perhaps not the best solution for an implementation heavily dependent on geographic information.

Regards,
Diane I. Hillmann

Response given on: 2003-09-15 10:53:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Fabiana:

It's not clear to me what your question is.

The category of "sub-elements" is not currently used by Dublin Core. Instead, the concepts of two kinds of "qualifiers": "element refinements" and "encoding schemes" are now used.

There is a new version of "Using Dublin Core" available now at the DCMI website (see: http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/ ), with additional information on qualifiers.

Question asked on: 2003-08-28 00:00:00 by carolina

Hi!
In our library, most of the volumes that are being digitalized are 19th century's, so they don't have ISBN, so we were wondering how can it be referenced in DC, information about the original book, such as original publishing date, publisher...we think that it could be in DC element "Source" but is it intented to have alphanumerical full description or just a call number (we think it wouldn't make sense to refered to a internal call number?)

thanks in advance...

C



Response given on: 2003-08-29 04:06:00 by Palle Aagaard

Palle Aagaard

Hi Carolina,

As you describe your task the element "Source" is correct.

You can see the definition at: http://www.dublincore.org/documents/dces/

And it is recommended to use a string or number conforming to a formal identification system. But the only rule is: "A Reference to a resource from which the present resource is derived"

So, if there is not such a formal system you can use "free text".

An example could be: <META NAME="DC.Source" CONTENT="Hans Christian Andersen: The Improvisator. Original Novel (1835 )>

Regards

Palle

Question asked on: 2003-08-28 00:00:00 by Armands

Is it possible to create my own element refinements? And if the answer is yes then what would be the best way to do it? If I understand correctly then Dump-Down principle is created exactly for such a cases, right?

Thanks in advance!

Response given on: 2003-09-27 18:50:00 by Andrew Wilson

Andrew Wilson
Manager AGLS, NAA 1999-2002Digital Preservation Project Manager NAA 2002-2005Preservation Services and Projects Manager AHDS 2005-DC Usage Board 2003-PREMIS Elements Working Groupetc.

Hi

Yes it is possible to create your own element refinements. The best way is to determine which element(s ) you wish to refine and then determine how your refinement(s ) narrows or limits the semantics of the element(s ). You need to remember that refinements are specifically for narrowing the definition (semantics ) of the parent element. It is also important to remember that the refinement cannot duplicate the semantics of any other exisitng element or registered element refinement.

The 'dumb-down' principle is an important part of the interoperability principles of the DCMI model and is intended to ensure that element values have meaning even when element refinements, encoding schemes and any other 'markup' is removed.

cheers
Andrew Wilson

Tags: Name:Refinements; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Programmer

Question asked on: 2003-08-14 00:00:00 by Claudia

What is the exactly semantic difference between "term" and "element"

Response given on: 2003-08-26 11:16:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Claudia:

The opening sentence of the Overview of Documentation for DCMI Metadata Terms(http://dublincore.org/usage/documents/overview/ says: "This document includes the up-to-date specification of all metadata terms maintained by the Dublin Core Metadata Initiative, including elements; element refinements and encoding schemes ("qualifiers" ); and controlled vocabulary terms (the DCMI type vocabulary )." The simple answer then is that "terms" is the general, "element" is specifically those terms defined at the element level (the original 15 and Audience ).

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2003-08-14 00:00:00 by Claudia

Dear sir:
I would like to know if DCMI could replace the AACR2 in the bibliographic description. Or both could be complementary

Response given on: 2003-08-19 10:35:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Claudia, in defining the Dublin Core Metadata Element Set (DCMES ), the DCMI community did not define any rules for encoding values. For example in defining the element 'creator', DCMI did not establish any rules to control the manner in which the value--i.e., the name of the creator--is encoded. Since AACR2 is solely a set of rules to control the content and structure of values entered in elements it is not possible for the DCMES to "replace" AACR2. In fact, there is nothing that says that AACR2 cannot be used as the set of rules to be followed where applicable in entering values into Dublin Core records.

Tags: Name:About DCMI; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2003-08-11 00:00:00 by Eli

Hello,

I am digitizing several thousand photographs from an archival collection and using DC to enable access. Each photograph has a unique identifier, but occasionally I want to describe a series of photographs using one Dublin Core record. The question: is it appropriate to be using the photographic identifier as the DC.Identifier? If so, should multiple identifiers use repeated DC.Identifier elements, or should I use a range or serialization in one DC.Identifier element (i.e. Nos. 57-88, or 1,2,3,7,19)?

Response given on: 2003-08-26 11:32:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Eli:

It is certainly appropriate to use the photographic identifier as the DC Identifier, particularly if that is the only identifier you use.

If you want to do a series of photographs in on DC record (a mini-collection, as it were ) I would recommend repeating the element, rather than using a range or series of numbers within one element iteration. This seems to me a safer strategy, given that there's no way to know what the future might hold for the metadata.

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2003-08-08 00:00:00 by carolina

Hi!!
I wanted to know what is the correct syntax to insert the "Relation Types" in a rdf, for example when I have an object that has a relation "isPartOf".
I've looked at http://dublincore.org/documents/2002/04/14/dcq-rdf-xml/ and also I checked http://dublincore.org/documents/1999/04/29/rdf-relation-types/
but my doubts haven't been cleared up.

carolina

Response given on: 2003-08-08 09:12:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

<?xml version="1.0"?>
<rdf:RDF
xmlns:rdf="http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#"
xmlns:dc="http://purl.org/dc/elements/1.1/"
xmlns:dcterms="http://purl.org/dc/terms/">;
<rdf:Description rdf:about="http://www.example.org/example/">;
<dcterms:isPartOf rdf:resource="http://www.example.org/"/>;
</rdf:Description>
</rdf:RDF>

Question asked on: 2003-08-07 00:00:00 by Paul

Hello! I'm trying to create a crosswalk to go between DC, MARC21, and fields that I've created for an image database of educational visual aids that were created by the New Deal agency, the WPA. I've created a field called "Production Place" to refer to where the item was manufactured or published. It seems like I would need a refinement of the element Publisher (I'm referring to organizations not individuals) but cannot seem to find an appropriate refinement that defines a place of production. What would be your recommendation for what element and refinement I should use?

Thanks very much!


Paul Lefrak
Broward County Library

Response given on: 2003-08-08 05:58:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

Place of publication is not a refinement of dc:publisher (because it would not dumb-down correctly - e.g. my:publicationPlace=England --> dc:publisher=England is not correct ).

I'm not aware of any widely used element for this - so you may well have to invent your own.
However, creating a new element in this way will not be interoperable with other metadata applications.

For example, in the BIBLINK project we created a new property called biblink:placePublication (see http://hosted.ukoln.ac.uk/biblink/wp8/fs/bc-semantics.html ). Note, BIBLINK is not an active project - therefore I wouldn't suggest re-use of this particular element.

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Crosswalk; Audience:Cataloger

Question asked on: 2003-07-29 00:00:00 by harpreet

Which syntax is better to use in a library application profile..html or xml?

Response given on: 2003-07-29 07:57:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

That depends on what your application is! :- )

If you want to share metadata between software components within your digital library (for example between a metadata creation tool and a back-end catalogue/database ) then I'd use XML.

On the other hand, if you want to provide document authors with a simple way of providing metadata that can be harvested into a search-engine using HTTP, then I'd use HTML.

Question asked on: 2003-07-29 00:00:00 by harpreet

Which are the elements which are mandatory in
DCMI 15 element set which should be there in a Dublin Core record?

Response given on: 2003-07-29 07:34:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

In DC metadata, all elements are optional. I.e. none are mandatory.

Particular applications of DC (commonly referred to as application profiles ) may indicate that some elements are mandatory and some optional.

I would expect that in most DC applications both dc:title and dc:identifier are mandatory.

Question asked on: 2003-07-19 00:00:00 by Kevin

I notice that in describing various DCMI documents that you often have a "Document Status" field, and yet this does not seem to be one of your basic elements.

How can I best describe Document Status using Dublin Core (eg 'Draft','Available','Withdrawn','Superceded', and so on)?

Response given on: 2003-07-29 08:29:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

"Document status" is not a part of the Dublin Core element set, but is instead an administrative attribute internal to the workings of DCMI. You could think of it as a "local" controlled vocabulary, part of the administrative metadata that DCMI uses to make its documentation available to the outside world.

For your purposes, you may choose to do the same sort of thing, but the specification for *how* you do it will be part of your local practice, outside or beside the Dublin Core description of your documents.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"
Member, DC Usage Board and Advisory Board

Question asked on: 2003-07-10 00:00:00 by Aree

We are not sure if we should provide our metadata in HTML or RDF, for our website. Which is a better option? What should be our criteria for choosing? Also, in HTML can we have all elements from DC except for "description" and "keyword" or is it better to repeat the information in "description" and in "DC.Description"?

Response given on: 2003-07-16 15:55:00 by Stuart Weibel

Stuart Weibel
Convener of the DCMI workshop series and director of DCMI

The choice of an encoding syntax remains one of the vexing design choices of current metadata deployment. The answer must reflect a combination of factors, including available expertise, nature and complexity of the application, and the nature of any peer-applications (if any ).

The choices include HTML, XML, and RDF XML

HTML is the easiest approach - metadata can be embedded directly within the resources if they are HTML, and are available for harvesting by web crawlers (this may or may not be an advantage ).
HTML is also the crudest encoding idiom, and will not easily support things like embedded namespaces that can facilitate the mixing and matching of different element sets.

XML affords additional options that are intrinsically structurally richer. The Open Archive Initiative uses this approach, and conforming to their practices may increase the likelihood of future interoperability with other applications.

RDF-XML is a 'dialect' of XML targetted towards declaring and reusing metadata vocabularies in a way that is intended to make it easier to extend metadata sets and mix them. The state of RDF still requires fairly sophisticated developers, and the result is that it is not as widely used as might be hoped. Risch Site Summaries (RSS ) is a syndication technology that is commonly found in Blogging systems, and which uses RDF. Looking at this flavor of application may help to refine your interests in the technology.

Tags: Name:Syntaxes; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Librarian


Question asked on: 2003-07-03 00:00:00 by DPI Photo Resources

For an online digitized still photo library , is there a certain metadata element set, or proper classification scheme in which still photos are to be categorized?

- How do you properly categorize still images for an online repository/library?


Response given on: 2003-07-16 15:42:00 by Stuart Weibel

Stuart Weibel
Convener of the DCMI workshop series and director of DCMI

The Dublin Core is widely used as a metadata set for still images, and it can be used with or without a formal classification scheme. The Pictures Australia site <http://www.pictureaustralia.org/> is an excellent example of such an application.

The question of which (if any ) classification scheme to use is largely a local issue. The additional expertise necessary is likely to increase both the cost and the eventual usability of the collection. The decision of which scheme to select should be influenced by available expertise, the domain of the collection, and usage of similar schemes among peer applications (is the host library an LCSH house? DDC? Special domain with a specialized thesaurus available? )

Tags: Name:Implementation; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Cataloger

Question asked on: 2003-07-02 00:00:00 by Daniel

Hello,

I come from the GIS world, where metadata is synonymous with spatial data metadata. I am trying to learn more about non-spatial metadata for a project I am working on, and am surprised that there is no mention of GIS, geographic or spatial data, or anything related at DCMI. What is the reason for this? Where should I go for cross-over info: spatial *and* non-spatial metadata?

Thank you.

Daniel

Response given on: 2003-07-03 12:52:00 by Stuart Weibel

Stuart Weibel
Convener of the DCMI workshop series and director of DCMI

Hi Daniel,

Thanks for your question. As it happens, there has been GIS-related interest in DC from the very beginning, but as is probably obvious, the specificity and detail needed in GIS metadata often go far beyond what one expects to find in DC. Bringing these areas closer together requires a modular metadata model that supports the cross-domain generality of Dublin Core while supporting the extensibility necessary to extend vocabularies into the more esoteric needs of a domain such as GIS.

One source of expertise in this area can be found in the DC-Environmental special interest group [http://www.dublincore.org/groups/environ/], a group focused on the application of DC to the environmental domain, but which has a strong element of GIS interest in it.

Another approach worth exploring is to look at the DCMI Projects page and search or browse for projects with a strong GIS component. Again, the environmental domain and the science and technology domains offer several examples that may help you get a feel for what is being done.

ASKDCMI Administrator

John:

Some of the options you describe are not Dublin Core fields, so I'm not really qualified to tell you what to do about them.

In standard Dublin Core, recommended practice for something like a video format would be to use the DC:Format element, and choose an appropriate value from the IMT list (if one exists for the particular format you have--sometimes there isn't one yet approved ).

There are a number of mailing lists available on the DCMI site (http://dublincore.org ), organized by working groups. Anyone can join these lists. There's also a general mailing list, that everyone subscribed to the specialized lists also gets automatically.

Please do sign on and get involved!

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core" and member, Usage Board and Advisory Board

Response given on: 2003-06-26 05:42:00 by Palle Aagaard

Palle Aagaard

Hi,

First, this is not a Dublin Core specific question - as far as I can see. But nevertheless I will give you an answer to the best of my knowledge.

I understand your question as a question about if there exist "switching languages" for translation (mapping ) between different subject vocabularies (keyword schemes or classification schemes )- or some common set of keyword vocabulary which belong to different vocabularies.

Both approach will improve "subject search interoperability".

I do not know of any official (official published by a well known editor ) keyword vocabularies which e.g can be considered as a switching language. But such vocabularies exists e.g. in some domain specific areas - to some degree.

I will recommend a database search (library & information science ) - and perhaps also an Internet search.

Response given on: 2003-06-23 07:04:00 by Palle Aagaard

Question asked on: 2003-06-09 00:00:00 by Gregor

Response given on: 2003-07-09 16:57:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

Question asked on: 2003-06-05 00:00:00 by Gauri

Hi,
I was wondering if anyone could let me know what, if, CODEN exactly stands for.
Many thanks

Response given on: 2003-08-01 16:31:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Quite literally, CODEN stands for "Code Number."

DEFINITION: "A system of alphanumeric codes developed by the American Society for Testing and Materials (ASTM ) to uniquely and permanently identify sci-tech serial and monographic publications. Responsibility for administering the system was transferred to Chemical Abstracts Service (CAS ) in 1975. The CODEN is used in electronic information systems to process bibliographic data because it is more concise than the full title and less ambiguous than an abbreviated title."

Cambridge Crystallographic Data Centre. "The CSD System Documentation". Available Online (6 June 2003 ): http://www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/support/csd_doc/volume2/
z2c07165.html

"The coden is a number, assigned at the CCDC, and each coden corresponds to a single journal or part-journal, for example :

**Biochim.Biophys.Acta has coden 113
**J.Chem.Soc.,Perkin Trans.1 has coden 207
**J.Chem.Soc.,Perkin Trans.2 has coden 188

Codens are used to avoid inconsistencies in the spelling and abbreviation of journal names; also to save space in the database. Some users may be familiar with the International Coden system where, for example, Biochim.Biophys.Acta has international coden BBACAQ."

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:IT staff


Question asked on: 2003-06-04 00:00:00 by Darryl

How would one go about implementing Dublin Core for a subject metadata field within Canto's Cumulus digital assets management system? Does an institution pay a fee for connecting to DC as a dropdown menu to select a subject description? I'm assuming we would have to develop scripting that would link the subject field from within Cumulus to the DC list.

Response given on: 2003-06-25 13:32:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Darryl:

At the moment, DCMI recommends a small number of subject schemes for use with Dublin Core metadata. These schemes are not maintained by DCMI, but are in fact maintained by other organizations. Examples are the Library of Congress Subject Headings (LCSH ), maintained by the Library of Congress and the Dewey Decimal Classification (DDC ), maintained by OCLC, Inc. (See http://dublincore.org/documents/2002/10/06/current-schemes/ for a list of currently valid Encoding Schemes ).

DCMI is planning to have a method for registering other subject schemes for use with DC metadata sometime this fall. At that time, implementations or maintainers of thesauri will be able to register their subject schemes with DCMI and be able to use an unambiguous token with their metadata to express that scheme.

As you may see from this explanation (I hope ), using DCMI subject schemes is considerably simpler (and more complicated ) than a simple script to a list maintained by DCMI.

I hope this helps.

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core," and member DC Usage Board and Advisory Board

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff


Question asked on: 2003-06-02 00:00:00 by Francois

Is DC run by any major search engine as -for example- Google ?

Response given on: 2003-06-06 08:23:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

The answer to your question is short and simple. "No", Dublin Core is *not* run by any major search engine. It is technically housed at OCLC (http://www.oclc.org/home/ ). DC can best be characterized as a very large group of volunteers (metadata practitioners and scholars ) with an interest in supporting the building of a coherent Web environment through the use of metadata. Its focus is primarily (but not solely ) on metadata for resource discovery.
