Question asked on: 2003-06-02 00:00:00 by tj

We're creating a digital collection of Victorian Trade Cards. We want to include an element for the geographic location of the company whose product is being advertised (creator). But we aren't sure where it might fit within the DC schema. The same company also typically produced the card (publisher). A refinement of the publisher element would seem to make the most sense. Any suggestions?

Response given on: 2003-06-25 13:50:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

TJ:

Your question is an interesting one, and challenges the notion of "simple metadata" quite a bit!

Let me try to explain what I mean: the items in your collection are the trade cards. You have determined that the company whose product is exposed in the cards is to be described as the creator. So far so good. But now you want to also include more information about the creator--in your case, the geographic location of the company. I can see the utility of this, but are you not now describing another resource, not the card at all, but the company?

DC has something called the "one-to-one" rule, which basically says: "describe one thing at a time." Recognizing that this is sometimes insufficient, we have begun to reconsider something called Structured Values (see http://dublincore.org/documents/2000/07/28/dcmi-dcsv/ but note that this document is currently under revision! ).

I would note here that refinements of DC terms that are not "vetted" and approved by DC have the potential to create problems when you share your data, because such refinements are not represented in DC namespace. You could, of course, set up your own refinement, but if so, be careful that you express it properly in your instance data, with appropriate documentation for other implementors.

Thanks for your question.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core," and member, DC Usage Board and Advisory Board

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2003-05-27 00:00:00 by Tony

What is the connection between DCMI and the CORES project? Has the CORES project contributed to wider awareness of DCMI?

Response given on: 2003-06-11 09:58:00 by Makx Dekkers

Makx Dekkers
Managing Director, DCMI


CORES is a project funded under the Information Society Programme of the European Commission and runs from April 2002 through June 2003. It has two main objectives. Firstly, it organized a Metadata Standards Interoperability Forum resulting in an agreement among metadata standards initiatives -- GILS, ONIX, DOI, CERIF, IEEE/LOM, MARC21, and DCMI -- to identify metadata elements using the common syntax of Uniform Resource Identifiers (URIs, RFC 2396 ). Secondly, it established and maintained a metadata registry, building on prior work in the related projects SCHEMAS and DESIRE.

The Interoperability Forum involved DCMI as a participant. The resulting CORES Resolution records an agreement among standards communities on principles very much in line with those of DCMI; an article in the July issue of D-Lib Magazine will increase awareness of this important agreement.

The Registry activity based its data model largely on that of Dublin Core, drawing on software developed in a UK project (MEG ), but with a scope broader than Dublin Core alone.

Related links:

http://www.cores-eu.net/
http://www.cores-eu.net/interoperability/
http://www.cores-eu.net/registry/
http://www.dlib.org/dlib/july03/

Tags: Name:Other issues; Purpose:DCMI Participant; Audience:Project manager


Question asked on: 2003-05-23 00:00:00 by Guy

Hi,

I suppose this is a question that might have been addressed before, but I'd like to know what would be the DCMI recommendation on using Creator or Publisher when we're talking about a new media departement in a company.

For exemple, Radio-Canada, the public broadcaster is developing and hosting web pages. Should we indicate it as the publisher or creator of the ressource or both, and if we want to granularize the department responsible for creation of the resource (New Media), is it possible to include on the same value RAdio-Canada, New Media, either as a creator or publisher ?

Thank you,
Guy St-Onge
projects coordinator
Radio-Canada.ca


Response given on: 2003-06-09 22:34:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Guy:

It seems to me that the clearest thing would be to look carefully at the role these departments are playing in the creation and dissemination of the resources, and how those roles relate to the DC elements CREATOR and PUBLISHER.

If the departments are responsible for the intellectual content of the resource, they should clearly be listed as CREATOR. If their role is primarily dissemination, then PUBLISHER is probably more appropriate. As a general rule, people are more likely to be creators and organizations more likely to be publishers, but that's not always true, as we know.

As for repeating the name of the department or organization in both places, this may be confusing unless both roles are played by the same organization. The more you "default" information in either place, the less useful each element becomes to a user. Keep in mind that most indexing will lump them together in any case (on the theory that the user will not necessarily make the same distinctions that the cataloger did ), so repetition will tend to create a lot of "false hits" in the long run.

I hope this helps.

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core" and member, DC Usage Board

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff


Question asked on: 2003-05-23 00:00:00 by Mary Sprague

I have a basic understanding of using DC elements to tag web pages. However, many web sites are based on an underlying database and web pages are created on the fly. Can you refer me to DC documentation that applies to this?

Response given on: 2003-06-02 16:18:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Mary:

It's true that for many websites these days, the CONTENT of the site is maintained in a database, and the pages are created dynamically. This makes the option of including metadata on the webpages themselves impossible.

However, the metadata can also be managed in a database, linked to the content in some way. This is essentially the model that libraries have always used--metadata and content in separate places, linked: in the old days via a call number, now more likely by a URL or other identifier.

The DCMI documentation doesn't really deal with issues like this in any depth, but I think once the concept is understood, you'll be able to go on from there.

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core" and member DC Usage Board

Response given on: 2003-05-19 10:25:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

While it is not possible to determine the number of countries that have citizens and/or applications using the Dublin Core Metadata Element Set (DCMES ), we are able to reach some conclusions regarding such use from the number of languages into which DCMES has been translated. Response given on: 2003-05-15 17:25:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Scott, the semantics DC Title is as follows: "A name given to the resource." For a complete listing of the semantics (and other information ) for all of the DC elements and element refinements, see the following DCMI Web site URL: http://www.dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/

Tags: Name:HTML; Purpose:Documentation; Audience:Programmer


Question asked on: 2003-05-15 00:00:00 by Scott

What is the semantics of Title?

Response given on: 2003-05-20 17:38:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

DCMI defines Title as "A name given to the resource."

Not all resources have titles, of course, so Title, like all DC elements, is optional. Some resources have several titles, so the Title element is also repeatable (like all DC elements ).

You might wish to take a look at "Using Dublin Core" at http://dublincore.org/documents/usageguide/ for more information and examples for the use of the element Title.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Response given on: 2003-05-05 11:37:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Dr. Knolmayer

1. By the "original 15, I was referring to the ELEMENTS defined at: http://dublincore.org/documents/dces/ -- not refinements for the element RELATION.

2. If IMS has defined 'isBasedOn' that should be perfectly usable, SO LONG AS the schema correctly attributes that refinement to an IMS namespace, not Dublin Core.

3. All Dublin Core elements and element refinements are optional and repeatable. Any requirements to include references come from elsewhere--Dublin Core does not make those kinds of requirements. Be careful as you go forward with your implementation that Dublin Core is at heart a very simple metadata set, without the sorts of internal structure you might be used to within IMS.

Thanks for referring to "Using Dublin Core" in your paper.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"
Member, DCMI Usage and Advisory Boards

Question asked on: 2003-04-26 00:00:00 by shubhada

We are digitizing Marathi old manuscripts. We are using DC translated marathi elements for cataloguing purpose. I want to use xml output of the data which we have entered. Response given on: 2003-04-26 04:02:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

The XML tags should always be in English (, , , etc. ) as described in the Guidelines for implementing Dublin Core in XML recommendation at http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/. Software applications may present the 'labels' for the elements in any language to the end-user, but the machine encoding in XML should always use the same (English ) element names.

ASKDCMI Administrator

There is an additional term at the element level, beyond the original 15 elements of Simple Dublin Core. It can be found at http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/#audience

Because it is not part of the 15 elements of Simple Dublin Core, and resides in a separate namespace from those terms, it is not considered the "16th element." It would generally only be used in a qualified DC implementation.

Question asked on: 2003-04-24 00:00:00 by Dan

I'm probably missing something but cannot work out the intended cardinalities of the terms defined.

My question is:

What does the DCMI recommend or intend as the cardinalities of the terms for a single catalogue entry?

It is fairly clear to me that there may be many contributors.
It isn't so clear to me that the intention is to have only one or possibly many creators.
I can work it out for contributor because the natural understanding of the term leads me to believe there may be more than one.
I'm also a tiny bit confused because I think the creators are contributors. They are preeminent contributors.
Is it intended that I do not repeat the creator as a contributor?
I do admire the intent of flexibility and flexibly given in exactly what the terms are intended to signify.
If the specifications were too prescriptive they won't apply widely and existing catalogues won't fit we in to the scheme.
Both of these are undesirables.
I'm using 'intended' to represent what the DCMI purposes the terms for. You could just as easily say 'recommended'.


Response given on: 2003-04-28 03:47:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

All DC elements are both optional and repeatable. So, within a single catalogue entry you may repeat both creator and contributor as many times as you like.

There has been an ongoing discussion over the last year or so about whether creator is an 'element refinement' of contributor, i.e. whether all creators are automatically also contributors. This discussion has not yet been resolved. However, some implementers are choosing to use only the contributor element rather than using both creator and contributor, i.e. they are not distinguishing those parties that were 'primarily responsible' for contributing to the resource.

If you choose to use both creator and contributor, do *not* repeat each creator as a contributor.

Tags: Name:Other; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Programmer


Question asked on: 2003-04-23 00:00:00 by Gerhard

At present, I am directing a seminar in which we want to implement the relation attributes between e-learning objects (=resources) as defined in the Dublin Core Metadata Initiative.

Unfortunately, it is difficult to find answers to the following questions:

(1) Why is a certain type of relation always defined in both directions? This would make sense if there could be asymmetrical relations but we cannot imagine such cases.
(2) Is the 2000 version of qualifiers on relations the last version? Is it different from LOM or IMS? Several organizations have "customized" the original set of qualifiers?
(3) We have many problems in finding good examples of the qualifiers defined and in distinguishing them. For instance, can we say that

Requires is stronger than references?
Requires in stronger than IsBasedOn?
References is stronger than IsBasedOn?

Would it make sense to define only the strongest qualifier because the weaker ones hold automatically?

(4) Let us assume that resource 1 cites (=makes reference) to a book. The short form of the citation in resource 1 requires that we list the book on the reference section. Would the Dublin Core describe that situation by "requires" or by "is referenced by"?

Many thanks
Gerhard

Response given on: 2003-05-02 09:14:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Gerhard:

You've asked some very good questions. Let me take them as you've organized them:

(1 ) Not all relation refinements are defined in both directionsconformsTo, for instance, is unidirectional. It is also not required that both directions be utilized, but since there were situations when we could conceive that someone would want to use reciprocal relationships, we made it possible to do so.
(2 ) There is not really any such animal as "the 2000 version of the qualifiers." The DCMI Usage Board, which is charged with reviewing proposals for new semantics, will undoubtedly be adding new qualifiers in future, though probably not many. This is a quite different process than IMS or LOM, I believe, and although there is an agreement about core elements (the original 15 ) used in common, that does not extend to qualifiers. It's entirely possible that some organizations have "customized" the qualifiersin fact, so long as they do so in a manner consistent with good practices regarding schemas and namespaces, there should be no problem regarding interoperability in general.
(3 ) "Using Dublin Core" is at present being updated, and more examples should be available when that process is complete, but for the moment you are correct that good examples do not exist. So far as I know (and I should ) there is no qualifier "isBasedOn" (the definitive list is at http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/ ). The Relation refinements are not necessarily ranked as stronger or weakereach has a specific definition which should help determine where it might be effective. Your suggestion that only the "stronger" be defined really does not work well within a relatively simple set of terms like Dublin Core. Those sorts of assumptions would require a fairly sophisticated common understanding that I don't think exists presently, and would be difficult to build within the diverse community that Dublin Core serves.
(4 ) I'm not sure I understand your question here, but let me try and build out from your scenario. If Resource 1 cites another resource (which may or may not be listed as a Reference within the publication ), it could be included as a References relationship on the metadata, if you so choose. You could then, if you wished, make a reciprocal entry on the metadata for the referenced resource, as isReferencedBy. Whether or not you do so would likely be based on the needs of your implementation and whether the costs of enriching your metadata in that way made sense in your context.

Good luck with your seminar,
Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"
Member, DCMI Usage Board and Advisory Board

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:IT staff

dcterms.xsd is looking for dc:SimpleLiteral, but dc.xsd only has reference to dc:elementType. And should dc:any be xs:any?

Douglas R. Steen
Boulder CO

Response given on: 2003-04-26 04:17:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

Hi, the dc.xsd file that you want (i.e. the one that defines dc:SimpleLiteral ) is the one at http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/qdc/2003/04/
02/dc.xsd. The full list of XML schemas that should be used for 'qualified DC' applications is given in the table towards the end of http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/. (Note that the XML schema for 'simple DC' applications at http://dublincore.org/schemas/xmls/simpledc20021212.xsd does NOT define dc:SimpleLiteral and thus can NOT be used within qualified DC applications ).

Question asked on: 2003-04-16 00:00:00 by Shirley

Forgive me is this seems a basic question. I'm new to the learning objects arena and am involved in a research project where we're thinking of applying LOM. However, I would like to ask: can you please explain the relationship between the Dublin COre metadata activity and LOM. Are you working together? Or are your agendas completely separate? PLease point me to appropriate URLs if need be so I can get a better understanding of this.
Many thanks. Look forward to your reply.
Shirley


Response given on: 2003-05-01 18:25:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
Head of Development, Eduserv Foundation

DCMI provides a relatively simple set of metadata elements for describing 'resources', where resources is interpreted very widely and includes Internet resources, books, journals, images, movies, sound files, datasets, museum artefacts and so on. The intention of DC metadata is to support resource discovery (particularly in the context of Internet-based services ).

IEEE LOM is a metadata specification that primarily focuses on the description of learning objects - and its intention is to support discovery of learning objects and their use within (e )learning management systems.

IEEE LOM is somewhat more detailed than
simple DC.

There is a memorandum of understanding between DCMI and LOM at

http://dublincore.org/documents/2000/12/06/dcmi-ieee-mou/

While there is some overlap of semantics between DC and LOM, encoding syntax details in XML are significantly different. For an example of the mapping between a sub-set of LOM and DC and to see some of the differences in encoding details see

http://www.rdn.ac.uk/publications/rdn-ltsn-ap/

Question asked on: 2003-04-14 00:00:00 by Jim

I am the chair of the coordinating group for an international task force sponsored by the BIS, ECB, Eurostat, IMF, OECD, and UNSD (See www.sdmx.org). We are struggeling with how we should copyright our work, as we are not an incorporated legal entity. I see that you have copyrighted your work along the usage lines that we wish to have. The question is what is the legal status of DCMI and therefore your ability to seek copyright protection.

Thank you in advance for considering this question. If you wish to speak to me, I may be reached at 202-623-8143.

Best regards,
Jim McKee

Response given on: 2003-05-11 20:27:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Jim, currently, DCMI has no independent legal status beyond those rights derived from its host organization (OCLC ) that provides services, resources and salaried employees with commitments to DCMI. The intellectual property (IP ) statement for DCMI can be found at the following URL: http://www.dublincore.org/about/copyright/#copyright

In trying to answer your question, it is important that you understand that the answer provided here is not intended in any way as legal advice *or* as a statement of rights binding on OCLC, DCMI, or any other entity. They are merely the opinion of this author--someone with a fairly extensive understanding of both IP law in the United States and in the organization of DCMI.

That said, it is important to distinguish copyright interests held in the DCMI Web site and copyright interests in individual documents found on the Web site. As to the first of these interests (the Web site ), the site exists on a server owned and operated by OCLC. The design work (including navigation and a fair mount of the content etc. ) are largely the product of employees of OCLC produced within the scope of their employment and, therefore, under U.S. law would be the IP of OCLC as "work for hire." Also, IP rights in the Web site extend to the collation of works as a compilation under U.S. law. These IP rights in no way alter or extend IP rights in the individual works in the compilation.

I am assuming, however, that your concern is less with the Web site and more with the IP rights in individual documents on the Web site. Unfortunately, under current organizational circumstances, there is no clear answer that you are likely to find satisfying with regard to any specific document. The DCMI IP statement says of DCMI documents: "Who holds the copyright on DCMI documents? The original author of the document. Many documents are created by DCMI and we consequently hold the copyright. Owners who allow their works to be published on the DCMI site retain the copyright, but agree to the DCMI license for the redistribution of those materials from our site." This statement does nothing more than restate the outline of U.S. copyright law under Title 17 of the United States Code. Since many of the documents on the DCMI Web site are created by individuals and groups of individuals that have no legal affiliation with DCMI other than serving as volunteers (i.e., they are not employees of DCMI or independent contractors commissioned in writing by DCMI to create such documents ), IP rights in those documents vest in those individuals or groups of individuals under United States law (Title 17 of the United States Code ). As the DCMI PI statement notes, such owners allow their works to be published on the DCMI site but retain full IP rights and may take appropriate legal action against anyone that violates the DCMI license for the redistribution of those materials from the site.

Hope this helps.

Saedeh, I am not exactly sure of what it is that you are asking. However, let me try to respond to what I think you are asking. Bibliographic records in a bibliographic network *are* instances of metadata (i.e., metadata records ). Therefore almost everything that passes over a bibliographic network is metadata.

Question asked on: 2003-04-11 00:00:00 by Maewyn

Is there a difference between a sub-element and a refinement? If so, what is it?

Response given on: 2003-04-22 12:21:00 by Stuart Sutton

Stuart Sutton
Professor in the Information School. Member of the DCMI Usage Board and the DCMI Advisory Board. Co-Chair of the DC-Education Working Group.

Maewyn, in the past, many people have referred to what are properly known as element refinements as sub-elements. While in most instances, it is not the sort of reference that is likely to get anyone into trouble in terms of their understandings of the Dublin Core metadata model. However, it is probably best *not* to think of element refinements as sub-elements. Many prefer to think of an element refinement as simply another element that just happens to provide a more restricted set of semantics than the element it "refines".

Question asked on: 2003-04-11 00:00:00 by Kingsley

In the document
"Guidelines for implementing Dublin Core in XML" (http://dublincore.org/documents/dc-xml-guidelines/)

under " Example - a qualified DC record", the syntax for Subjec is:

<dc:subject>
national centre, network information support, library
community, awareness, research, information services,public
library networking, bibliographic management, distributed
library systems, metadata, resource discovery,
conferences,lectures, workshops
</dc:subject>
<dc:subject xsi:type="dcterms:DDC">
062
</dc:subject>
<dc:subject xsi:type="dcterms:UDC">
061(410)
</dc:subject>

however, if you're using multiple classification systems then wouldn't you want to identify the specific subject with a specific classification system?

something like:

<dc:subject xsi:type="dcterms:DDC">national centre </dc:subject>

<dc:subject xsi:type="dcterms:LCSH">public information systems </dc:subject>


?? Thanks.


Response given on: 2003-04-26 04:28:00 by Andy Powell

Andy Powell
UKOLN, University of Bath

Hi, in the example the values '062' and '061(410 )' are clearly marked as being taken from specific classification systems (dcterms:DDC and dcterms:UDC respectively ) using the xsi:type XML attribute. None of the other terms ('national centre, network information support, library community, awareness, research, information services,public library networking, bibliographic management, distributed library systems, metadata, resource discovery, conferences,lectures, workshops' ) are taken from a classification system - they are free-text keywords - therefore no classification system is indicated.

Question asked on: 2003-04-10 00:00:00 by Eli

I am attempting to build a site where users can submit archival material to a digital archive. Items in the archive will be described in DC based on input to a web form.

My question: what element or qualified element ought I use to give the name of the person submitting the material? This person will not necessarily or even usually be the creator. I am vacillating between DC.Publisher and DC.Location (since generally the physical item will remain with the person submitting the digital version) but perhaps there is a better alternative.

Response given on: 2003-04-17 15:23:00 by Diane I. Hillmann

Diane I. Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"Research Librarian, Cornell University Library

Eli:

The answer to your question could vary depending on what relationship the submitter has to the resource. A "person" representing an institution that holds and item might be the submitter, but may not be as important as the institution in describing the resource itself. On the other hand, that "person" may be important in an administrative sense, as being the creator of the metadata record about the resource, and may need to be recorded by you as part of the that transaction, but not necessarily exposed by you as a publisher or creator in a description of the resource.

If indeed the submitter is more than just the creator of the metadata, the Publisher element might be appropriate. Publisher is defined in DC as "An entity responsible for making the resource available." This is intentionally a bit fuzzy, and covers a possiblly broad range of activities. It is quite distinct from either "creation" which implies responsibility for the content of the resource, or "provenance," which seems to carry a different emphasis.

The Merriam Webster Dictionary, for instance, defines Provenance as "the history of ownership of a valued object or work of art or literature." Another definition I found, from the Glossary of Archival Terms (Simon Fraser University, Archives and Records Management Department ) [http://www.sfu.ca/archives/glossary.html] defines it as "The person(s ) or office(s ) of origin of records, i.e., the person(s ), family (families ), or corporate body (bodies ) that created and/or accumulated and used records in the course of that creator's activities or functions."
Both of these definitions emphasize "ownership" rather than creation of content or the responsibility for making the resource available.

I have seen suggestions that the Location element from the DC Libraries Application Profile might be used instead, but if you look at the definition for the MARC 852 field (to which that MODS element looks for definition [http://www.loc.gov/marc/bibliographic/ecbdhold.html#mrcb852] ), you can see that the Location "Identifies the organization holding the item or from which it is available. May also contain detailed information about how to locate the item in a collection." Subfield "a" of that field is defined as "The institution or person holding the item or from which access is given. Contains a MARC code for the organization or the name of the institution or person holding the item." This seems to me much more a passive assertionthe entity that holds an item is not necessarily the same one that makes it available, nor should one assume that it wishes to do so!

So, again, whether the submitter is more relevant to the metadata itself or the resource is the crux of the issue, and from there you may find your answer.

Tags: Name:Elements; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2003-04-09 00:00:00 by Walther

At a training session yesterday, where we discussed Dublin Core, I was corrected when stating that there where 15 elements. I was told there where now 16, where the last one added was the element "Audience".
Looking at your website under the heading elements, I still find 15 and a proposal for a 16th element. Can you tell me what the current status on the 16th element is?

Thank you in advance for your help

With kind regards

Walther Hasselo
City Archives
Leiden
The Netherlands

Response given on: 2003-04-17 16:18:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Walther:

There are still 15 elements in simple Dublin Core, those elements described in the document at: http://dublincore.org/documents/dces/. All of these terms are at the element level.

Audience is indeed a valid DC term, at the element level (see http://dublincore.org/documents/dcmi-terms/#audience ), but is not included in the group of 15 "core" elements, partially because those original 15 are seen as of more general use than any of the refinements and other terms approved more recently.

The DC namespaces reflect this difference: Audience appears in the same namespace as the refinements, not the original 15 elements. See http://dublincore.org/documents/2001/10/26/dcmi-namespace/ for the full Namespace Policy document.

I hope this isn't too confusing!

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core" and member DCMI Usage Board

Question asked on: 2003-02-04 00:00:00 by ASKDCMI

Is it okay if I use the following Dublin Core element for the ISSN of a series?

<META NAME="DC.Identifier" SCHEME="ISSN" CONTENT="ISSN 1617-3309">

The name of this series is: Freiberger Dissertationen Online. The ISSN is: 1617-3309

The single Dissertations in this series have a number.

And which element should be used for the number within this series?


Response given on: 2003-02-04 12:17:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

If you are creating a metadata record for the series itself, then your approach seems reasonable. However, if you are creating a record for an item within the series, information in the IDENTIFIER element should relate to the item, not the series.

One way to figure out how standard cataloging concepts work with Dublin Core is to take a look at the standard mapping between MARC and DC maintained at: http://www.loc.gov/marc/marc2dc.html. You will note there that 4XX and 8XX tags map to the qualified RELATION element, with a refinement IsPartOf.

When using the RELATION element for this purpose, the title, ISSN and number could be represented as a string, for example:

Freiberger Dissertationen Online (ISSN:1617-3309 ), no. 22

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Question asked on: 2003-02-04 00:00:00 by ASKDCMI

Does the emphasis on "resource discovery" imply that DC elements should primarily be embedded in the actual resources? I'm interested in using DC elements to build catalogs of resources, do you think that would somehow go against the spirit of DC?


Response given on: 2003-02-04 11:40:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

The decision of whether or not to embed DC elements within a resource or carry the metadata separately in an external database is entirely up to the implementor. It is certainly true that most implementations do not embed DC elements within a resource, and there are maintenance issues inherent in that approach.

Question asked on: 2003-01-31 00:00:00 by ASKDCMI

What's the difference between a thesaurus and a classification?

Response given on: 2003-01-31 15:18:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Your question about the difference between thesauri and classifications is interesting. From Merriam Webster's Dictionary:

Thesaurus: a book of words or of information about a particular field or set of concepts; especially : a book of words and their synonyms; b ) : a list of subject headings or descriptors usually with a cross-reference system for use in the organization of a collection of documents for reference and
retrieval.

Classification: systematic arrangement in groups or categories according to
established criteria; specifically : TAXONOMY

From these definitions it appears that a thesaurus implies a cross-references of some sort, e.g., among languages and synonyms.

A classification does not imply any cross-references, and is typically a taxonomy. I think it is important to maintain the differences. One should know when one is addressing a classification versus a thesaurus. Your expectations will be different for the two, as a classification implies a unique term for every concept or definition.

A third category is Authority List. An authority list has no meanings attached to the terms, but the terms may be arranged in a hierarchy (e.g., LCSH ).

Frequently people are building an authority list when they think they are building a classification.

Working definitions of each of these is important, although the boundaries among them are not clear. The structure (e.g., list, hierarchy, links ) is not the same as the function.

Thomas D. Wason, Ph. D.

Tags: Name:Metadata in General; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor


Question asked on: 2003-01-31 00:00:00 by ASKDCMI

Our art history professors would like to see more information on the cataloging record that emphasizes features of the object depicted in the art slides we prepare for their use. Do you think Dublin Core could help us toward this end?

Response given on: 2003-02-04 11:26:00 by ASKDCMI Administrator

ASKDCMI Administrator

Dublin Core can accommodate those kinds of needs in a number of different ways--which you choose to implement depends on how you think you'll use the data and who you might be sharing it with.

The simplest way would be to use keywords in SUBJECT or to describe the object or objects depicted in DESCRIPTION.

If you wished to do something more complex, either using controlled vocabularies or an application profile specifying elements from another metadata format, that should be possible but would require more planning and documentation to be successfully implemented.

You might wish to take a look at some of the museum projects on the DCMI website (http://dublincore.org/projects/http://dublincore.org/projects/>http://dublincore.org/
projects/> ) and see if they are moving in similar directions.

Diane Hillmann
Editor, "Using Dublin Core"

Tags: Name:Terms; Purpose:Project implementation; Audience:Implementor

Question asked on: 2003-01-20 00:00:00 by Makx

What is the URL for the NISO standard?

Response given on: 2003-01-20 16:34:00 by Makx Dekkers

Makx Dekkers
Managing Director, DCMI

The URL for the NISO standard is: http://www.niso.org/standards/resources/Z39-85.pdf. This standard "defines fifteen metadata elements for resource description in a crossdisciplinary information environment", also know as the Dublin Core Metadata Element Set version 1.1.
